It is based on the premise that organisations and individuals have millions of daily interactions with other people, and could utilise these opportunities to support those people in making positive changes to their physical and mental health and well-being. The idea is that small actions with lots of people will result in big population changes.</li>
<li>NHS Health Education England (2018b) provides free e-learning that covers 'Very Brief Advice on Smoking' and 'Alcohol Brief Advice'.</li>
<li>Public Health England (PHE 2018a) has produced a resource for healthcare professionals who are working with patients to prevent illness, protect health and promote well-being. The resource provides tips on having brief conversations, health coaching and motivational interviewing.</li>
</ul>
<p>These resources are evolving and are constantly updated. Make use of them!</p>
<p>Key areas for promoting health:</p>
<ul>
<li>Healthy eating and alcohol awareness.</li>
<li>Increasing activity levels.</li>
<li>Smoking cessation.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="healthy-eating-and-alcohol-awareness">HEALTHY EATING AND ALCOHOL AWARENESS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#healthy-eating-and-alcohol-awareness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are many well-documented benefits of healthy eating (see Box 8.1). Most people are familiar with the Eat Well Guide (NHS Choices 2016a), which replaced the earlier Eat Well Plate.</p>
<p>Foods higher in fat, salt and sugar, such as crisps, biscuits, cakes and sweets, have been taken off the plate because they are not needed in the diet and should be eaten in only very small amounts. The latest guide has attempted to simplify the healthy eating message and demonstrates a well-balanced diet with the emphasis on 'good' foods. The authors obviously took on board the information suggesting that sugar should not be represented on the plate at all because of the mounting evidence that clearly identifies its detrimental effects, particularly on dental health. Some authors are convinced that sugar is responsible for the obesity epidemic (Lustig 2012), and indeed a review of 20 studies demonstrated overwhelmingly that sugar consumption, specifically in the form of sugary drinks, is almost certainly linked to obesity in adults and children (Cohut 2018).</p>
<p>It is certainly a political hot potato (maybe an unfortunate term in view of the carbohydrate in a potato!), and in April 2018 the UK introduced a sugar tax on soft drinks that contain high levels of sugar in direct response to the mounting evidence of the links with obesity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-8-1-benefits-of-healthy-eating">Box 8.1 Benefits of healthy eating<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-8-1-benefits-of-healthy-eating" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Lower cholesterol</li>
<li>Weight control</li>
<li>Good bowel function</li>
<li>More energy</li>
<li>Reduced risk of some chronic disease (e.g. heart disease, diabetes)</li>
<li>Stronger teeth and bones</li>
<li>Lower blood pressure</li>
<li>Clearer skin</li>
<li>Improved immunity</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tips-for-healthy-eating">TIPS FOR HEALTHY EATING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tips-for-healthy-eating" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This advice is suitable for most people, but may vary for groups of people with specific health problems, pregnant women or those with special dietary requirements. They should be referred to the dietician or someone with specialist knowledge if necessary.</p>
<p>Most of the information used in the tips below comes from the Healthy Eating leaflet produced by Patient.info (Tidy 2016a):</p>
<ol>
<li>Carbohydrates can be complex, such as that found in bread, pasta, rice and potatoes, or simple, such as that found in sweet sugary foods. Most guidelines currently recommend making up about one-third of our food with complex high-fibre carbohydrates, such as wholegrain cereals and wholemeal bread. However, keep a close eye on the guidelines over the next few years because information about the amount of carbohydrate we need is changing all the time, especially for some groups such as diabetics (see Chapter 16).</li>
<li>Eat plenty of vegetables and fruit (see Figure 8.2). Most people are familiar with the 'five a day' advice, but some authors suggest that eating at least seven or even ten portions of fruit and vegetables (with the emphasis on vegetables) reduces the risk of many illnesses, such as stroke, heart disease and some cancers</li>
</ol>
<p>(Aune et al. 2017). It may well be that some of this evidence is affected by the fact that most people who eat a lot of fruit and vegetables also tend to lead more healthy lifestyles, but even so it seems as if the more fruit and vegetables you can eat, the better it is for general health. The portions should be from a variety of sources. Examples of portions are: one large fruit such as an apple, pear, banana or orange; one handful of small fruits such as grapes or strawberries; two smaller fruits such as satsumas, kiwis or plums; one tablespoon of dried fruit; two large tablespoons of fruit salad, stewed fruit or canned fruit in natural juices; one glass (150 ml) of fresh fruit juice; and three heaped tablespoons of any vegetable. Aim to include at least two different vegetables with most main meals.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-591ca34c.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg">
Figure 8.2 HCA eating an apple</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Eat plenty of fibre. This is useful to help the bowels function properly and may help reduce cholesterol. It is filling but has few calories. Eating more fruit and vegetables will increase fibre intake, especially if the skin is left on when possible. Including pulses, such as lentils and beans, in the diet will also help to increase the fibre content.</li>
<li>Eat some dairy foods or other calcium-rich foods. Dairy foods are a source of protein and provide calcium, which is needed for healthy bones and teeth. People who don't eat dairy foods can get calcium from calcium-enriched soya milk and some cereals, dark green leafy vegetables and tofu. An adequate supply of vitamin D is also needed for the body to be able to synthesise calcium. This can be obtained by exposure to sunlight, but people who are housebound or living in residential homes may need vitamin D supplements.</li>
<li>Eat protein in moderation. It is recommended that dietary protein comes from a variety of sources, especially the lower-fat options such as chicken, fish, lean meat, tofu, beans, pulses and soya. It is widely accepted now that most people eat too much protein, although some is important for energy, growth and repair of cells. Research has previously indicated that it is also a good idea to include two portions of oily fish per week as the omega-3 fatty acids present in oily fish are thought to protect against heart disease. Other useful forms of omega-3 include things such as chia seeds, flax seeds and walnuts. A review of the data has suggested that further research is required to prove the benefits of omega-3 (Rettner 2018), so once again you will need to keep updated in case the information changes.</li>
<li>Don't eat too much fat. This advice is currently under review. For many years, we have blamed fat for the obesity epidemic and other health problems because fat contains about twice as many calories as carbohydrate or protein per gram. However, we now know that the picture is more complex than this, and it seems likely that carbohydrate plays a larger role in weight gain than previously realised. Even so, it is probably still a good idea to eat less fat when trying to lose weight. A diet low in trans fats (unsaturated</li>
</ol>
<p>fatty acids) may also help reduce the chances of heart disease and stroke because trans fats increase LDL cholesterol and decrease HDL cholesterol, and both these factors are associated with heart disease. These trans fats occur in foods such as hard margarines, biscuits, pastries and cakes, fried foods, and processed foods. Saturated fats, including butter, have had something of a reprieve (much to my husband's delight!) following some research done by de Souza et al. (2015), although the recommendations are still to keep saturated and trans fats to a minimum. Choose fats labelled as monounsaturated or polyunsaturated, such as olive oil or sunflower oil, instead. Avoid frying food, choose lean cuts of meat, and watch out for hidden fats in foods such as pastries, cakes and biscuits.</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>
<p>Reduce the amount of sugary foods and drinks. Sugar might make some food taste better and does give an instant burst of energy, but apart from that it is of little value. It can cause weight gain and tooth decay, so cutting down on it makes sense. Try not to add sugar to tea, coffee and cereals. Use fruit as an alternative to add sweetness to recipes. Give children water or milk as their main drink. Keep chocolate or sweets for an occasional treat.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reduce salt. Too much salt may increase the risk of hypertension, and current guidelines advise a maximum of six grams a day, which is less than that consumed by most people. Try replacing salt with herbs and spices to add flavor, and look for foods that contain no added salt. Avoid processed foods, ready-made sauces and packet soups, which may be high in salt. Never add salt at the table and limit the amount added in cooking.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Watch portion sizes. It never ceases to amaze me how large the portions are in some restaurants! It seems that most people have become accustomed to eating portions that are much larger than required. Try to reduce portion sizes and fill up on more fruit and vegetables. Use a smaller plate and chew food more slowly. Order small portions when eating out. The child-size portion is often more than enough for the average adult.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Think about what you drink. Alcohol in excess can lead to a variety of problems (see Box 8.2). As alcohol has become more readily available in supermarkets, with prices much lower compared with those in pubs or restaurants, more people are choosing to drink alcohol at home on a regular basis. This has led to an increase in alcohol-related disease and addiction. Alcohol also contains calories. Choose healthier non-alcoholic drinks and keep alcohol within recommended limits. Men and women should drink no more than 14 units a week spread evenly over several days, and everyone should have at least two alcohol-free days a week. For information on units, check NHS Choices (2018a). Pregnant women should not drink any alcohol. Try to drink between six and eight glasses of water each day.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-8-2-problems-related-to-alcohol">Box 8.2 Problems related to alcohol<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-8-2-problems-related-to-alcohol" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Liver disease</li>
<li>Stomach ulcers</li>
<li>Obesity</li>
<li>Malnutrition</li>
<li>Dependence</li>
<li>Antisocial behaviour, aggression and violence</li>
<li>Accidents</li>
</ul>
<ol start="11">
<li>Try to sit down at a table when eating. If you eat in front of the television, you are less aware of how much you are eating. Food should be varied and most of all it should be enjoyed. It is often suggested that the Mediterranean diet is better for us because it contains olive oil, tomatoes and moderate amounts of red wine. Maybe it also has something to do with the fact that it is cooked from fresh ingredients and consumed in company, rather than as a solitary activity in front of the television or at a computer.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>How closely do you adhere to the principles of healthy eating? Try keeping a food diary for a week and write down everything you eat and drink. Be honest! Now compare your eating habits with the guidelines given above. Is there room for improvement? How will you make changes to improve your</p>
<p>How do we help our patients lose weight?</p>
<p>It seems as if the normalisation of ‘plus-size' body shapes may be leading to an increasing number of people underestimating their weight, which is undermining the efforts to tackle the problem of obesity in England and presumably elsewhere (Muttarak 2018). First, you need to establish if your patient needs to lose weight (and persuade them of this!), and if so how much they need to lose. Measure their height, weight and waist (see Chapter 11). It is also useful to work out the basal metabolic rate for each patient to determine how many calories they need. Although government guidelines suggest that men can have 2,500 calories and women 2,000 calories each day, this is a generalisation and cannot be applied to everyone. Obviously, people who have active jobs will need more calories than those in sedentary work.</p>
<p>When your patient says that they have tried everything but nothing works, this probably isn't true. Any diet that involves eating fewer calories will work if it is maintained, so the secret is finding the ‘diet', or better still finding the change in eating habits, that can be enjoyable and maintained. Nobody will stick to eating just cabbage for very long! Some top tips for helping your patients to lose weight are included in Box 8.3. These are taken from an information leaflet provided by the British Obesity Society. These are worth checking out as a useful resource for your patients. They are very readable and straightforward, with humorous and colourful diagrams, and they are available in different formats depending on the gender and ethnicity of your patient. (The leaflet has not been published, but copies can be obtained by contacting <a href="mailto:info@britishobesitysociety.org" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">info@britishobesitysociety.org</a>.) Don't forget that in the end, it is your patient's responsibility to lose weight and not yours! Sometimes I think we take too much on ourselves. I have certainly been guilty in the past of sometimes bringing patients back weekly for months on end to try to encourage them to lose weight. If they are not doing their part and losing some weight, this is not a cost-effective use of your valuable time! Be brave and stop following up these patients until they are really prepared to stick to their side of the agreement and reduce their food intake.</p>
<p>Box 8.3 Top tips for losing weight for your patients (adapted from information in the British Obesity Society leaflet)Weigh yourself — it is easy to convince yourself you're not getting bigger, but scales never lie!Think about joining a slimming club such as Slimming World. They are a great way to get support for men and women.Find out your body mass index, and if it tells you that you're obese, then don't try to convince yourself it's muscle — unless it really is!Remember, you don't have to diet — just make a few sensible changes.Become more aware of how many calories you are consuming.Track what you eat — a food diary is helpful, or you could try taking pictures with your phone of every bit of food you put in your mouth throughout the day. There are also many different apps available for this now, and these can make the process of tracking what you eat easy and fun. You will probably be surprised!Get competitive with your friends and get fitter to lose weight.Avoid fizzy drinks and stick to water instead, and moderate your alcohol intake.Eat smaller portions and try to stop eating when you are full.Avoid processed foods.Try to sit down when you're eating.Get the whole family involved in cooking.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="increasing-activity-levels">Increasing activity levels<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#increasing-activity-levels" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Every year, one in six deaths in the UK occurs as a direct result of physical inactivity, with an estimated cost of £7.4 billion. Physical activity can help prevent and manage over 20 chronic conditions, including heart disease, some cancers, depression and type 2 diabetes. Despite this fact, the population of the UK is</p>
<p>approximately 20 per cent less active than in the 1960s, and this is set to rise to 35 per cent by 2030 (PHE 2018a).</p>
<p>There are many benefits to be gained from exercise and increased levels of activity (see Box 8.4).</p>
<p>Public Health England has produced an evidence-based document outlining an approach to physical activity called <em>Everybody Active, Every Day</em> (PHE 2014a), and the information in this section is based on the Chief Medical Officer's guidelines contained in that document and in the NHS Choices fact sheets (NHS Choices 2011).</p>
<p>Everyone should engage in moderate or vigorous physical activity every day and should minimise sedentary behaviour, but individual physical and mental capabilities must be considered when interpreting the guidelines:</p>
<ul>
<li>Moderate-intensity activities include things that will make you breathe harder and increase your heart rate, such as brisk walking or cycling.</li>
<li>Vigorous activity will make you warmer, breathe much harder, and will make your heart beat rapidly, and includes things such as running, swimming, skipping, playing football and climbing stairs (see Figure 8.3).</li>
<li>Muscle- and bone-strengthening activities involve working against resistance or using body weight, and include things such as swinging, hopping, skipping, playing tennis, dancing, exercising with weights, carrying heavy loads and chair aerobics.</li>
<li>Minimising sedentary behaviour can be achieved by reducing the amount of time spent sitting down as much as possible. Reduce time spent watching TV, using the computer or playing video games. Take regular breaks away from your desk or couch, and try breaking up sedentary time by swapping long bus or car journeys for walking part of the way.</li>
<li>Activities should be enjoyable to the individual as they are then more likely to be sustained. Referral to an exercise specialist can lead to longer-term changes in physical activity, and many exercise referral schemes exist around the country based at local leisure centres.</li>
<li>Activities to improve balance and coordination include things such as tai chi, Pilates and yoga.</li>
<li>For the under-fives: Physical activity should be encouraged from birth through floor-based and water-based play. Once the child can walk unaided, they should be physically active daily for at least 180 minutes spread throughout the day.</li>
<li>For children and young people from 5 to 18 years: This age group should be engaging in moderate- to vigorous-intensity physical activity for at least 60 minutes and up to several hours every day. Activities should include muscle- and bone-strengthening activities at least three days a week.</li>
<li>For adults: Over a week, periods of activity should add up to at least 150 minutes of moderate-intensity activity in bouts of ten minutes or more. One way to achieve this would be to do 30 minutes on at least five days a week. Alternatively, 75 minutes of vigorous activity can be spread across the week, or moderate- and vigorous-intensity activity can be combined.</li>
<li>Include physical activity that will improve muscle strength at least two days a week.</li>
<li>Older adults should incorporate physical activity to improve balance and coordination at least two days a week.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-8-4-the-benefits-of-exercise-and-increased-activity">Box 8.4 The benefits of exercise and increased activity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-8-4-the-benefits-of-exercise-and-increased-activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Lowers blood pressure</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Lowers cholesterol</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reduces risk of heart disease and type 2 diabetes</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Improves bone density</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reduces symptoms of depression and anxiety</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Provides stress relief</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Improves weight control</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Improves self-image</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Builds stronger muscles</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Improves joint flexibility</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Increases stamina and energy</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Improves immunity</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reduces the risk of dementia</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reduces the risk of breast cancer and colon cancer</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>During the writing of this chapter, it occurred to me that I had been sitting at the computer for long periods of time, so I decided to take my own advice and go for a walk. I walked briskly for 30 minutes, and included a steep hill in my walk, so I was warm and puffing slightly by the time I got home. I saw my first primrose of the season and felt my cheeks glow as I walked. I must have had a rush of endorphins because I felt so good (and virtuous!), and felt re-energised when I sat back down to work. I had also topped up my UV light exposure, and thereby improved my synthesis of vitamin D and absorption of calcium as a result. Who could ask for more?</p>
<p>Try to practise what you preach, and you will find it's easier to be more enthusiastic when extolling the virtues of exercise and healthy eating because you know it really works. Patients might be more inclined to take your advice if they can see that you believe in what you say.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-e03aad25.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg">
Figure 8.3 HCA skipping</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="smoking-cessation">SMOKING CESSATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#smoking-cessation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Some interesting facts and figures:</p>
<ul>
<li>There are approximately 9 million adult smokers in the UK (ASH 2017b), but the good news is that the proportion of the population who smoke has decreased from 45.6 per cent in 1974 to 16.8 per cent in 2017 (Office for National Statistics 2018).</li>
<li>Smoking is the single most preventable cause of premature death. About 96,000 people in the UK die each year due to smoking, and about half of all lifelong smokers will die prematurely, losing on average about ten years of life (ASH 2017b).</li>
<li>The estimated cost to society in England alone is approximately £12.9 billion a year. This includes the cost to the NHS of treating diseases caused by smoking as well as lost productivity due to premature deaths, smoking breaks and absenteeism (ASH 2017a).</li>
<li>Smokers spend an estimated £3,600 each year on smoking 20 cigarettes a day (ASH 2017a).</li>
<li>Smoking is the main contributor to health inequalities in England. People in more deprived areas are more likely to smoke and less likely to quit, and these areas have more than double the death rate from</li>
</ul>
<p>lung cancer compared with other less deprived areas (PHE 2015a; ASH 2016a).</p>
<ul>
<li>People who have long-standing mental health problems are more than twice as likely to smoke (ASH 2016b).</li>
<li>There are over 4,000 chemicals in tobacco smoke, many of which are highly toxic, including things such as acetone, arsenic, benzene, cadmium, carbon monoxide, formaldehyde and hydrogen cyanide. It is hardly surprising, then, that smoking is the cause of so much ill health.</li>
<li>An estimated 2.9 million adults in Great Britain were using e-cigarettes (vapes) in 2017, up from 700,000 in 2012 (ASH 2017c).</li>
<li>Despite some concerns, nicotine replacement therapy (including e-cigarettes) is not as harmful as smoking, and is currently recommended by the NHS as a method of quitting.</li>
</ul>
<p>Some of the many benefits associated with stopping smoking are listed in Box 8.5.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-8-5-the-benefits-of-stopping-smoking-nhs-choices-2012">Box 8.5 The benefits of stopping smoking (NHS Choices 2012)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-8-5-the-benefits-of-stopping-smoking-nhs-choices-2012" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Improved sexual function</li>
<li>Improved fertility</li>
<li>Younger-looking skin</li>
<li>Whiter teeth</li>
<li>Better breathing</li>
<li>Longer life</li>
<li>Less stress</li>
<li>Improved smell and taste</li>
<li>More energy</li>
<li>Healthier loved ones</li>
<li>More money</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tips-for-encouraging-people-to-stop-smoking">TIPS FOR ENCOURAGING PEOPLE TO STOP SMOKING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tips-for-encouraging-people-to-stop-smoking" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pre-contemplation (not interested in changing behaviour):</p>
<ul>
<li>Accept their position and don't be judgemental.</li>
<li>Check on their long-term plans: 'Are you planning to continue smoking?'</li>
<li>Remind them that support is available if they decide to stop.</li>
</ul>
<p>Contemplation (thinking about making a change):</p>
<ul>
<li>Encourage the patient to talk about the pros and cons and find their own reasons for quitting.</li>
<li>Correct any myths, such as 'the damage is done', and remember it is never too late to quit.</li>
<li>Provide information about local smoking cessation provision.</li>
</ul>
<p>Preparing to change (ready to change and receptive to advice and information):</p>
<ul>
<li>Explain that smoking cessation advisors will support them throughout the process.</li>
<li>Provide a leaflet on stopping smoking.</li>
<li>Refer to the doctor for a prescription for nicotine replacement therapy or other medication if appropriate.</li>
<li>Reinforce the reasons they gave for quitting and give praise and encouragement.</li>
<li>Refer patients to the local smoking cessation organisation, where these are available.</li>
</ul>
<p>When I eat too much dessert, I don't post it on Facebook – because if it isn't charted, it didn't happen!</p>
<p>^{}[]
^{}[]</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="keeping-it-clean">Keeping it clean<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#keeping-it-clean" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hand-decontamination">Hand decontamination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hand-decontamination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Many thousands of years ago, a Greek philosopher and physician called Hippocrates laid down the moral code of conduct for medical practitioners called the ‘Hippocratic oath'. Within this oath are the words ‘all patients have the right to receive care and come to no harm'.</p>
<p>As healthcare workers, we all have a responsibility to ensure that we uphold this ideal wherever we work and to keep our patients safe. This chapter will focus on hand hygiene, but remember to think about infection prevention and control in everything you do when dealing with patients. Check your local guidelines for information on dealing with sharps, wearing of personal protective equipment (PPE) and disposal of clinical waste.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="setting-the-scene">SETTING THE SCENE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#setting-the-scene" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In the nineteenth century, a Hungarian obstetrician called Semmelweis discovered that the likely cause of infection and subsequent death in his obstetric patients originated from medical students who had performed autopsies, resulting in hand contamination. By contrast, patients cared for by the midwives who had not performed autopsies had a much lower rate of infection and death. Semmelweis hypothesised that there must be something on the hands of the medical students that could not be seen, but when passed on to the patient could cause infection. He demonstrated a significant reduction in mortality among his patients when medical students washed their hands in a chlorinated lime solution. Despite this important finding, his medical colleagues would not believe him. He was later committed to a sanatorium, where he died at the age of 47. Twenty years on, Louis Pasteur provided the theoretical explanation, or ‘germ theory', of disease.</p>
<p>Now, many years later, we are still guilty of causing infections in patients because of a lack of basic infection control procedures, the most important of which is hand hygiene.</p>
<p>Healthcare-associated infections (HCAIs) are infections that were not present before admission to hospital or contact with healthcare workers in the community. They can affect people of all ages, exacerbate existing or underlying conditions, delay recovery, and adversely affect quality of life (NICE 2014b). There has been a great deal of work done to raise awareness and to tackle the issues associated with infection prevention and control, and subsequently there has been some improvement in the numbers of some HCAIs, especially C. difficile infection, which has gone down by 42 per cent in the last ten years (RCN 2016b).</p>
<p>However, over 6 per cent of patients in hospital in England still acquire some form of HCAI (NICE 2016). Three hundred thousand patients a year acquire a HCAI in England, with 9,000 deaths thought to occur as a result, and the cost to the NHS is £1 billion a year (NICE 2014b).</p>
<p>Many of these HCAIs are preventable using simple measures, such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>good personal hygiene of health staff;</li>
<li>correct hand decontamination;</li>
<li>appropriate use of personal protective equipment such as gloves and aprons (when necessary);</li>
<li>correct disposal of sharps;</li>
<li>correct disposal of clinical waste;</li>
<li>keeping work areas and surfaces clean and free from clutter;</li>
<li>isolating patients with specific infections; and</li>
<li>appropriate use of antibiotics and avoidance of overuse.</li>
</ul>
<p>All nurses are responsible for ensuring that risks to patients are kept to a minimum and that their care is as</p>
<p>safe and effective as possible (NMC 2015), and this obviously applies to all healthcare providers (not just nurses). Everyone has a duty of care to their patients and should observe good infection prevention and control guidelines.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-infection">WHAT IS INFECTION?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Infection is caused by microorganisms, usually bacteria, viruses or fungi. These are too small to be seen with the naked eye, but if fully active and present in sufficient quantity and in the appropriate environment, such microorganisms can become pathogens, and are then capable of causing infection. All microorganisms can become pathogenic. An example of this is a bacterium called S. aureus. This is one of the major causes of HCAI or community-acquired infection (CAI). Approximately 30 per cent of the population may be long-term carriers of this bacterium. It is frequently found in the nose and on the skin, and usually causes no problems. However, if the person (or host) has a cut or abrasion, the bacteria can become pathogenic and cause an infection. It can cause pimples, impetigo or cellulitis. If it gets into the bloodstream, it can cause pneumonia, endocarditis or severe inflammation of the bones (osteomyelitis). Similarly, many people may have methicillin-resistant S. aureus (MRSA) bacteria on their skin or in their respiratory tract. When they are healthy and their skin is intact, it does not cause a problem. However, if they have an open wound or if their immune system is compromised in some way, the MRSA can become pathogenic and cause an infection that is resistant to many types of antibiotics (Henderson 2016).</p>
<p>S. aureus is a very resilient organism but can be removed by correct hand decontamination.</p>
<p>Many different types of microorganisms live on the skin and hands. Some are referred to as resident flora and others are called transient flora. Resident flora are microorganisms that tend to stay on the skin. They are more difficult to wash off the hands but are less likely to be harmful. Transient flora are microorganisms that are transferred very easily, and they tend to be more harmful and more likely to cause infection. They can be transferred by touch from person to person or from person to an object that may then harbour the organism. The good news is that these transient flora are also washed off more easily, so a good hand decontamination technique will remove or destroy them.</p>
<p>Infection prevention and control is not about destroying all microorganisms in the healthcare environment, as this is simply not possible. It is about preventing the transfer of those microorganisms that are potentially harmful and that may compromise the patient's safety and well-being.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-is-infection-spread">HOW IS INFECTION SPREAD?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-is-infection-spread" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For an infection to occur, the chain of infection (see Table 9.1) must be unbroken, and so the aim of infection control is to break the chain. Good hand hygiene, or ‘hand decontamination', is the most important way of breaking this chain and preventing the transmission of microorganisms.</p>
<p>If infection is to occur because of indirect contact via the healthcare worker's hands, the organism must have been present on the skin, it must survive on the hands for at least several minutes, hand-washing or antisepsis by the healthcare worker must be inadequate or omitted entirely, and the pathogens on the caregiver's hands must come into contact with another patient, either directly or by being shed into the patient's immediate environment (Teare et al. 2001).</p>
<p>Hand hygiene is the simplest and most important way in which we can reduce the spread of infection, and all healthcare workers — from cleaners through to consultants — have a personal responsibility to undertake adequate hand hygiene.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Chain of infection</th><th>Example</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Source of infection</td><td>• Bacteria</td></tr><tr><td>• Fungus</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Virus</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Route of transmission</td><td>Direct contact via: Body fluids</td></tr><tr><td>Or indirect contact via:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Hands</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Equipment</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Arthropods (e.g. flies, mosquitos)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Airborne (e.g. respiratory droplets)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Susceptible host</td><td>• Elderly or very young</td></tr><tr><td>• Immunosuppressed</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Open wound/recent surgery</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Chronic disease</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Point of entry</td><td>• Wound</td></tr><tr><td>• Mouth/nose/eyes/ears</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Vagina/rectum</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="activity-2">ACTIVITY 2<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When should you decontaminate your hands?</p>
<p>There are many occasions during a day at work when you should decontaminate your hands. How many can you think of?</p>
<p>Check your answers against those given in Box 9.1.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-9-1-five-key-moments-for-hand-hygiene">Box 9.1 Five key moments for hand hygiene<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-9-1-five-key-moments-for-hand-hygiene" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When should you decontaminate your hands? The WHO (2009) has defined the key moments when healthcare workers should perform hand hygiene. These include:</p>
<ol>
<li>before touching a patient;</li>
<li>before clean/aseptic procedures;</li>
<li>after body fluid exposure risk;</li>
<li>after touching a patient; and</li>
<li>after touching patient surroundings.</li>
</ol>
<p>You could also have included the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>at the beginning and end of each shift;</li>
<li>after the removal of gloves;</li>
<li>before eating;</li>
<li>after going to the toilet;</li>
<li>after touching your nose;</li>
<li>after handling potentially contaminated linen or equipment; and</li>
<li>whenever hands are visibly dirty.</li>
</ul>
<p>HAND-WASHING: THE PROCEDURE</p>
<p>Remember, nails should be short and clean, with no nail varnish. False nails should not be worn in work. Cover open cuts or lesions with a waterproof dressing, and report any skin conditions that are affecting the hands to your manager for advice.</p>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHIPC2 Perform Hand Hygiene to Prevent the Spread of Infection to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Health 2012):</p>
<ol>
<li>Remove wristwatches and bangles and any hand jewellery with ridges or stones that can harbour microorganisms before providing care. When wearing plain rings such as wedding bands, move them when carrying out hand hygiene to reach all microorganisms.</li>
<li>Wear short sleeves or roll up sleeves prior to hand hygiene.</li>
<li>Use either liquid soap or approved alcohol-based hand-rub products.</li>
<li>Hand-washing technique (see Figure 9.1):</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Wet hands under running water before applying soap.</li>
<li>Cover all areas of the hands during washing.</li>
<li>Rub hands palm to palm.</li>
<li>Right palm over the top of the left hand with fingers interlaced, and vice versa.</li>
<li>Palm to palm with fingers interlaced.</li>
<li>Rub the backs of the fingers to the opposing palms with fingers interlocked.</li>
<li>Rotational rubbing of left thumb clasped in right palm, and vice versa.</li>
<li>Rotational rubbing, backwards and forwards with clasped fingers of right hand in left palm, and vice versa.</li>
<li>Rinse well under running water.</li>
<li>When lever taps are not available, turn off the tap using a paper towel.</li>
<li>Dry hands thoroughly using soft disposable paper towels.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-0a16dd51.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg">
Figure 9.1 HCA washing hands</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>
<p>If your hands are not visibly contaminated with organic matter, it may be appropriate to use alcohol hand rub. In this instance, follow the manufacturer's guidelines and make sure all areas of your hands are in contact with the gel, using the technique as above for hand-washing. Allow the gel to dry naturally before contact with the patient.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Keep your fingernails short and clean. Do not use nail polish or wear artificial fingernails at work.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Before each shift, assess your hands for cuts, cracks and breaks in the skin that could harbour microorganisms.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cover any cuts and abrasions with a waterproof dressing, change the dressing when it appears soiled, and keep the area clean to reduce the risk of infection. Never perform wound dressings when you have a cut or abrasion on your hand.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Use hand cream to maintain skin integrity.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Report any skin problems to your line manager, occupational health or GP so that you can receive appropriate treatment.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Report any difficulties with facilities or supplies for hand hygiene to the appropriate person and ask them to take action to improve the situation.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>A video of the correct hand-washing technique is available online (NHS Choices 2017b).</p>
<p>Figure 9.2 indicates the most commonly missed areas after hand-washing. Many potentially harmful pathogens could still be present on these hands in the 'missed areas'. Make sure you never miss these areas when you decontaminate your hands.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-aa9473bb.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg">
Figure 9.2 Areas missed on washed hands</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-problem-with-gloves">THE PROBLEM WITH GLOVES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-problem-with-gloves" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>I have a bit of a thing about gloves! I get really annoyed when I see health workers typing on the computer keyboard or answering the phone with their gloves on. I think sometimes people think wearing gloves somehow makes them safer and eliminates the need to decontaminate the hands. Please remember that gloves are no substitute for effective hand decontamination and should only be used when necessary. Gloves should only be used when the user is at risk of exposure to blood and body fluids, non-intact skin or mucous membranes where there is the risk of exposure to blood-borne viruses. Gloves should also be worn when there are risks of exposure to chemicals such as cytotoxic drugs and other toxic substances such as NaDCC (commonly used to clean ear irrigation machines).</p>
<p>Wash your hands after removing gloves and discard the gloves in the clinical waste as soon as the procedure is completed. All medical gloves are single-use only.</p>
<p>LEGISLATION AND GUIDELINES YOU SHOULD KNOW ABOUT</p>
<ol>
<li>Control of Substances Hazardous to Health regulations – COSHH 2002 (Health and Safety Executive 2017). Under COSHH, all healthcare workers have a legal duty to assess the risk of infection for employees and patients and all others who may be affected by their work.</li>
<li>Infection Prevention and Control (NICE 2014b). This document provides evidence-based advice on hand decontamination, as well as information on safe management of long-term catheters and vascular access devices.</li>
<li>Surgical Site Infection (NICE 2013). This document includes information and advice on wound care and the treatment of surgical site infection.</li>
<li>Personal Protective Equipment at Work (Health and Safety Executive 2013). This document describes the use of PPE for protection of the worker in the workplace.</li>
<li>Guidelines for Hand Hygiene in Health Care (WHO 2009). These guidelines are a little old now but are still a useful guide for healthcare workers, administrators and health authorities, with a review of evidence on hand hygiene in healthcare. They make recommendations regarding how to improve practice and reduce the transmission of pathogenic microorganism to healthcare workers and patients.</li>
<li>Essential Practice for Infection Prevention and Control (RCN 2017b). This document provides evidence-based advice on best practice for prevention and control of HCAIs. It identifies the essential principles for hand decontamination and the use of personal protective equipment (PPE), as well as the safe use and disposal of sharps and waste disposal.</li>
<li>Aseptic Non-Touch Technique – ANTT (ASAP 2018). This is a framework for aseptic practice that has been developed to improve the standard of aseptic technique in clinical practice. This is discussed in more detail in Chapter 17.</li>
<li>Find your local trust or health board infection prevention and control policy and read it! This will include information on things such as hand hygiene, use of PPE, maintaining equipment and the environment, managing blood and body fluid spillages, safe disposal of waste, and sharps safety. Your place of work should also have their own policy on infection prevention and control.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Try using one of the frameworks for reflection discussed in Chapter 2 to do this.</p>
<p>We know that hand hygiene is the most important measure for reducing HCAIs, but studies on hand hygiene compliance among healthcare workers have repeatedly shown poor compliance with hand hygiene (Damani 2012).</p>
<ol>
<li>During your next shift in work, make a note of how many times in the day you decontaminate your hands. If you missed washing or decontaminating after dealing with a patient, why do you think this was?</li>
<li>Have a chat with your colleagues and mentor. How many of them decontaminate their hands after every patient encounter or procedure?</li>
<li>What would help you and your colleagues adhere to the guidelines more closely?</li>
<li>Identify any areas of weakness in your place of work where it is difficult to maintain infection control. Think about things such as not having lever taps, no sink in the treatment area, lack of alcohol hand gel, cluttered work surface, etc. Do any of these apply to your place of work? Or maybe you can think of other problems? Discuss this with the other members of the nursing team and identify an action plan to address these problems.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chaperoning">Chaperoning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chaperoning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="keywords">Keywords<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#keywords" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Chaperon</p>
<p>In recent years, the role of the chaperone in healthcare has been recognised as increasingly important for patients and healthcare providers alike. It is often performed badly, leaving the health professional and patient open to abuse, and so it is vital that all those who are to perform the role understand the reason for it and can do it properly. This chapter will outline the history behind the current situation and will provide guidelines for healthcare workers who may be asked to perform this role in the future.</p>
<p>All healthcare professionals have become acutely aware of the need to protect themselves from allegations of malpractice in today's litigious society. Furthermore, it has become clear that the title of ‘doctor' or ‘nurse' does not always guarantee protection for the patient. Consider the case of Harold Shipman, who was a GP who murdered more than 200 patients (Smith 2003). Consider also the case of Jimmy Savile, who so blatantly abused his position of trust as a hospital porter and used his status as a respected fundraising celebrity to abuse children and vulnerable patients for many decades. It is an unfortunate fact of life that in any sector of society, there will be people who will choose to abuse their positions of trust and respect to satisfy their own perverse needs. Baker (2004), writing about Shipman, argues that systems to protect the public and regulate the professions have been inadequate.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-ayling-inquiry">The Ayling Inquiry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-ayling-inquiry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In 2004, the Department of Health set up an independent investigation into how the NHS handled allegations about the conduct of GP Clifford Ayling (DoH 2004). He was convicted of 13 counts of indecent assault on patients between 1991 and 1998. The report describes a ‘long history of continuing unease' regarding the GP's behaviour, which was overfamiliar and unprofessional when performing intimate examinations. His colleagues appear to have ignored the warning signs and ‘reworked the truth' to make it more acceptable, and had ultimately failed their patients. This investigation culminated in the Ayling Inquiry of 2004, and it is this that has been the catalyst for the increased awareness of the importance of the role of the chaperone. The report recommended that all patients should be offered a chaperone for intimate examinations, and that each NHS trust should create a chaperone policy, make it explicit to patients and resource it accordingly. Despite this, research suggests that the use of chaperones is still afforded only a low priority, and many NHS trusts in England are still to implement a chaperone policy. Even where chaperones are used, many doctors are not recording this properly, and are therefore liable to medico-legal proceedings (MDDUS 2014).</p>
<p>Many staff performing the role of chaperone today have yet to receive any formal training in the role, and are unlikely to appreciate the full importance of it.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-a-chaperone">What is a chaperone?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-a-chaperone" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There is no established single definition of the term chaperone. It originates from the fifteenth century, when it meant ‘hood for a hawk', and later came to mean ‘a woman who protects a young single woman'. In French, the word ‘chaperonner' means to cover with a hood, and today the word is used in the sense of ‘protector' (Wai et al. 2008).</p>
<p>A Medical Protection fact sheet (Medical Protection 2016) defines the term ‘chaperone' as ‘an independent person, appropriately trained, whose role is to independently observe the examination/procedure undertaken by the doctor/health professional to assist the appropriate doctor--patient relationship'.</p>
<p>The chaperone can act as an advocate for the patient, assessing their understanding and offering an explanation when necessary. However, no matter how good the chaperone is, this does not negate the need for good communication and an adequate explanation of the procedure by the health professional performing the examination.</p>
<p>When is a chaperone needed?</p>
<p>The most common reasons for needing a chaperone are:</p>
<ul>
<li>To offer the patient protection against verbal, physical or sexual abuse and identify inappropriate behaviour.</li>
<li>To provide protection for the health worker against allegations of malpractice. A chaperone cannot be a guarantee of protection for either the examiner or examinee, but it is very rare for an allegation of assault to be made if a chaperone is present (Medical Protection 2016).</li>
<li>To support the patient by providing reassurance and physical and emotional comfort during an embarrassing ‘sensitive’ examination.</li>
<li>To maintain the patient’s dignity and self-respect.</li>
<li>When the patient requests a chaperone.</li>
<li>When the doctor feels that a chaperone is necessary.</li>
</ul>
<p>This list is not exhaustive, and the Medical Defence Union have produced fictional scenarios to demonstrate how a chaperone may be needed in other situations such as where dim lighting is required or where the health worker needs to get very close to the patient, for example during otoscopy (examining the ears with an otoscope) or ophthalmoscopy (examining the eyes) (Simpson 2010).</p>
<p>A ‘sensitive’ examination may denote one that involves examining the breasts, rectum or genitalia, but it could be used to describe any part of the body depending on the religion and cultural customs of the patient. Some authors argue that examination of the torso should also routinely be considered under the heading of intimate examination, as cases of inappropriate behaviour during such examinations have generated huge concern (Wai et al. 2008).</p>
<p>Other situations where chaperones may be beneficial have been outlined by Medical Protection (2016), and include those where there are vulnerable or anxious patients, patients with whom there may have been a difficulty or misunderstanding in the past, or patients being seen by trainee doctors or students.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-if-a-patient-declines-to-have-a-chaperone">What if a patient declines to have a chaperone?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-if-a-patient-declines-to-have-a-chaperone" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is important to document that the offer was made and declined. The health worker should explain that they would prefer a chaperone to be present and explain the role of the chaperone. They should explore the reasons why the patient doesn’t want a chaperone and address any concerns they may have. The healthcare professional may decline to examine the patient in this case if, in their clinical judgement, this would put them at risk of unjust or invalid accusation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chaperone-policy">Chaperone policy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chaperone-policy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There should be a chaperone policy in place so that all members of the team understand what the role entails. The policy should be clearly advertised in the surgery through patient information leaflets, websites and on notice boards. You, as the HCA, may be the best person to prepare an information poster for the waiting areas. The poster should outline the role and availability of chaperones and how patients can request one. Clear notices will reassure patients and increase understanding regarding the need of either doctor or patient to expect an extra degree of protection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-hca-as-chaperone">The HCA as chaperone<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-hca-as-chaperone" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Before the Ayling Inquiry (DoH 2004), there were very few, if any, recommendations as to who should act as a chaperone, and it would usually have been the nurse or female receptionist who were expected to perform the role. Friends or relatives may also have been used as chaperones, but are no longer considered appropriate. For patients with learning difficulties, a family member or carer may be present to support the patient, and in this instance a careful and sensitive explanation of the procedure is vital.</p>
<p>As a HCA, the role of chaperone will be one you can perform very competently, providing you have the appropriate training.</p>
<p>As a member of the clinical staff, you will usually have a Disclosure and Barring Service (DBS) check, but where non-clinical staff are used for this role, they will need to have a DBS check depending on their specific duties as chaperone and the contact they have with patients, especially children and vulnerable adults (CQC</p>
<p>2017a).</p>
<p>Remember that as a HCA, you might need to have a chaperone yourself for some procedures. For example, when performing an ECG, you may need a chaperone, particularly if you are a male HCA and your patient is female, or when performing a wound dressing where clothing is removed.</p>
<p>Men appear to be unfairly discriminated against when having an intimate examination because most chaperones are female. There should (ideally) be men available in the practice who can chaperone male patients if required, but obviously this won't always be possible.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chaperoning-the-gay-or-transgender-patient">Chaperoning the gay or transgender patient<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chaperoning-the-gay-or-transgender-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Remember, gay patients may prefer a chaperone of the opposite gender. All patients should be able to choose the gender of their chaperone if possible. Take care to use preferred gender titles with transgender patients.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chaperoning-the-procedure-see-figure-10-1">CHAPERONING: THE PROCEDURE (SEE FIGURE 10.1)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chaperoning-the-procedure-see-figure-10-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Introduce yourself to the patient.</li>
<li>Check that they have consented to your presence.</li>
<li>Give the patient privacy to undress and provide a dignity sheet to preserve their dignity.</li>
<li>Do not assist the patient with the removal of their clothes unless they indicate that they need assistance.</li>
<li>Assist the patient on to the examining couch only when necessary, and without putting yourself at risk of injury.</li>
<li>Only expose the area of the body required for the doctor to carry out the examination.</li>
<li>Maintain a position where you can observe the procedure throughout. This means you should always stand inside the curtains and usually at the head of the couch.</li>
<li>Check that the doctor has explained the procedure and that the patient understands what the examination will involve.</li>
<li>Keep the discussion relevant to the procedure and avoid unnecessary personal comments. Although it is sometimes tempting to discuss the weather or other topics to try to relax the patient, this can have the effect of distracting the patient and chaperone, and may make it more difficult for the patient to voice any concerns or discomfort.</li>
<li>Ensure the patient's dignity and privacy are respected as far as possible throughout the procedure.</li>
<li>Check for continuing consent if the patient appears to be uncomfortable or unhappy.</li>
<li>At the end of the examination, allow the patient privacy to redress fully, and only leave the room when they are fully dressed and before the doctor continues with the consultation.</li>
<li>Remember to observe infection control guidelines throughout.</li>
<li>Observe strict confidentiality, and do not discuss the consultation or examination with any other members of staff except on a 'need-to-know' basis. If you have any concerns about the procedure, always discuss these with your nurse mentor and document them clearly.</li>
<li>The healthcare professional should document details of the examination, including the presence (or absence) of a chaperone and their name and the information given. They should also document any reservations or concerns the patient or they themselves may have had.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-8beca05b.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg">
Figure 10.1 HCA chaperoning</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Consider the scenario below.</p>
<p>The doctor has asked you to act as chaperone for him to perform a breast examination. When you go into the room, you realise that the patient is someone you know quite well, and she appears quite nervous and flustered when she sees you.</p>
<p>Dr Jones quickly introduces you to the patient (Mrs X) and asks you to stand just inside the curtain at the foot of the bed. Mrs X takes off her blouse, as instructed by the doctor, but has some difficulty with the buttons, so you help her. You make some small talk to try to relax her as she seems very tense – you ask about her children and chat about the recent bad weather. You also admire her all-over tan, all the time trying to relax Mrs X.</p>
<p>Dr Jones comes behind the curtain and stands between you and the patient. He asks Mrs X to remove her bra and then proceeds to study her two breasts intently. He then asks Mrs X to raise her hands above her head and then to put her hands on her hips, all the while staring at her breasts. You're feeling a little embarrassed by now, so you look the other way and hope Mrs X will notice that you're not staring as well.</p>
<p>Dr Jones then proceeds to feel both Mrs X's breasts, as well as in the armpits and around the neck. Mrs X is looking very red and uncomfortable, and you do find yourself wondering why he needs to be so thorough when she only complained of a lump in one breast. Still, he's the doctor and must know what he's doing.</p>
<p>While examining Mrs X, Dr Jones talks to you, commenting on how busy the surgery is and asking if you will get the notes out for the next patient when you go out.</p>
<p>When Dr Jones has finished examining Mrs X, and before she puts her top back on, he thanks you for your help and suggests that you can now leave. You're glad to get out because you just find the whole thing so awkward.</p>
<p>How do you think the patient would have felt in this scenario, and why?</p>
<p>Using the information given in the chapter, suggest some ways this experience could have been improved for the patient and made safer in terms of protecting the doctor from allegations of malpractice.</p>
<p>The breast examination given in the scenario is exactly as it should be done, but if this is not explained to the patient it would seem a little odd.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Using a framework for reflection (see Chapter 2), try reflecting on a consultation where you were acting as a chaperone, or alternatively where you have been chaperoned as a patient yourself.</p>
<p>How did it go and what did you learn?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-iii">Section III<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-iii" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="core-skills">Core skills<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#core-skills" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li><strong>Chapter 11</strong> Physiological measurements</li>
<li><strong>Chapter 12</strong> Understanding and measuring blood pressure accurately</li>
<li><strong>Chapter 13</strong> Understanding the heart: how to perform the electrocardiograph (ECG)</li>
<li><strong>Chapter 14</strong> Venepuncture and capillary blood testing: best practice</li>
<li><strong>Chapter 15</strong> Kidney function and urine: performing accurate urinalysis</li>
</ul>
<p>11</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="physiological-measurements">Physiological measurements<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physiological-measurements" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Whenever performing any physiological measurement, always inform the patient fully, explaining what the procedure involves, and make sure that you have consent to proceed.</p>
<p>Decontaminate your hands before and after patient contact.</p>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS19 Undertake Routine Clinical Measurements to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Health 2011b).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="height-weight-body-mass-index-bmi-and-waist-measurement">HEIGHT, WEIGHT, BODY MASS INDEX (BMI) AND WAIST MEASUREMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#height-weight-body-mass-index-bmi-and-waist-measurement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>BMI = weight in kilograms / height in metres (most computers will calculate this automatically).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="why-do-we-record-weight-height-and-bmi">Why do we record weight, height and BMI?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-do-we-record-weight-height-and-bmi" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Recording a patient’s BMI can be useful to help identify those people who are overweight or underweight, and at increased risk of the associated health problems (see Box 11.1).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-11-1-why-check-the-bmi">Box 11.1 Why check the BMI?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-11-1-why-check-the-bmi" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To monitor normal growth in children.</li>
