<li>To check for abnormal loss of weight, for example in malnutrition, malignancy or alcoholism.</li>
<li>To check for abnormal loss of height, for example in osteoporosis where the spine crumbles.</li>
<li>To enable the doctor to prescribe certain medications or therapies where the dose is dependent on the patient’s weight.</li>
<li>To enable accurate measurement of lung function.</li>
<li>To encourage patients who are trying to lose or gain weight.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bmi-good-or-bad">BMI: good or bad?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bmi-good-or-bad" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>NICE (2014c) has produced a classification table to demonstrate the meaning of BMI measurements in relation to a healthy weight and obesity (see Table 11.1).</p>
<p>Table 11.1 Obesity: classification</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Classification</th><th>BMI</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Healthy weight</td><td>18.5–24.9</td></tr><tr><td>Overweight</td><td>25.0–29.9</td></tr><tr><td>Obesity 1</td><td>30.0–34.9</td></tr><tr><td>Obesity 2</td><td>35.0–39.9</td></tr><tr><td>Obesity 3</td><td>40.0 or more</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>While BMI is useful, it is not always an accurate indicator of health. The two men in Figure 11.1 have the same BMI, but which one do you think has the greatest health risk?</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-231ac5c9.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg">
Figure 11.1 BMI</p>
<p>Remember, muscle weighs more than fat, so if a person is very fit and has built up a lot of muscle, this can render the BMI useless as a determinant of health.</p>
<p>Measuring the waist in conjunction with the BMI is currently considered to be best practice, and is more useful to determine risk (see Table 11.2).</p>
<p>Table 11.2 Obesity: risk assessment with waist measurement</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>BMI classification</th><th>Waist circumference</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td>Low</td><td>High</td><td>Very high</td></tr><tr><td>Overweight</td><td>No increased risk</td><td>Increased risk</td><td>High risk</td></tr><tr><td>Obesity 1</td><td>Increased risk</td><td>High risk</td><td>Very high risk</td></tr><tr><td>For men, waist circumference of less than 94 cm is low, 94–102 cm is high, and more than 102 cm is very high.</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>For women, waist circumference of less than 80 cm is low, 80–88 cm is high, and more than 88 cm is very high.</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>It is generally accepted that people who lay down fat around their abdomen (referred to as central adiposity, or more commonly the 'apple shape') are at greater risk of health problems such as type 2 diabetes or heart disease than those people who have a 'pear shape', with larger buttocks and thighs. This is because the type of fat that tends to accumulate around the abdomen appears to block the effect of insulin on the cells, making it harder for the cells to take in glucose. This is thought to create insulin resistance so that the body responds by producing more glucose, which may eventually result in type 2 diabetes. This theory has previously been controversial, but more recent research has provided new evidence that supports the original theory and shows a clear correlation between the 'apple shape' and insulin resistance (Shungin et al. 2015), as well as a link with an increased risk of myocardial infarction, particularly in women (Peters et al. 2018).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recording-height-weight-and-waist-the-procedure">RECORDING HEIGHT, WEIGHT AND WAIST: THE PROCEDURE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-height-weight-and-waist-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="measuring-height-see-figure-11-2">Measuring height (see Figure 11.2)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measuring-height-see-figure-11-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Makes sure that the height chart/measure has been attached to the wall at the correct height.</p>
<p>Ask the patient to:</p>
<ul>
<li>remove their shoes;</li>
<li>stand upright and look straight ahead; and</li>
<li>keep heels together, and keep heels, calves, buttocks and back in contact with the wall if possible.</li>
</ul>
<p>Measure the patient's height to the nearest centimetre.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-40744cf2.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg">
Figure 11.2 HCA measuring height</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="measuring-weight">Measuring weight<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measuring-weight" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Use appropriate scales for the task. Recommendations for the types of scales to be used are available online (LACORS 2007; see Box 11.2).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-11-2-risks-associated-with-inaccurate-weight-measurement">Box 11.2 Risks associated with inaccurate weight measurement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-11-2-risks-associated-with-inaccurate-weight-measurement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>LACORS (2007) found that one hospital had used ordinary bathroom scales to weigh a girl with cancer to gauge how much radiation treatment she should receive.</p>
<p>The scales showed incorrectly that the 4-year-old had gained weight. Had the inaccurate reading been used, she could have been exposed to a potentially harmful dose of radiation.</p>
<p>Make sure the scales have been calibrated within the last year (or in line with local policy), are on a hard surface and set to zero. Ask the patient to:</p>
<p>remove outer clothing and shoes; and</p>
<ul>
<li>stand squarely on the scales.</li>
</ul>
<p>Record the patient's weight to the nearest 0.5 kilograms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="measuring-the-waist-see-figure-11-3">Measuring the waist (see Figure 11.3)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measuring-the-waist-see-figure-11-3" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Using a suitable measuring tape that is designed for the purpose, measure directly over skin or over only one item of light clothing.</li>
<li>Ask the patient to breathe out normally.</li>
<li>Hold the tape snugly (without compressing the skin) around the waist, halfway between the lowest rib and the top of the hip bone (roughly in line with or slightly above the belly button).</li>
</ul>
<p>Did you know?</p>
<p>Measurements on analogue scales can be increased by as much as 10-12 per cent if the scales are on a soft carpeted surface. Digital scales are less prone to this effect (Pendergast 2002).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-30dcc16c.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg">
Figure 11.3 HCA measuring waist</p>
<p>ACTIVITY</p>
<p>Now measure your own waist.</p>
<p>How do you score on the risk table (see Table 11.2)?</p>
<p>THE PULSE</p>
<p>When the heart contracts, it pushes blood out through the elastic walled arteries. This is felt at various points of the body as a pulse, and if the arteries are in good condition the pulse should usually be felt at the same time as the heartbeat. The normal pulse rate is between 60 and 80 beats per minute, but can vary enormously from as low as 40 during sleep up to 220 or more during strenuous exercise.</p>
<p>An arrhythmia is an abnormal rate or rhythm of the heartbeat. Some are more serious than others. Some are intermittent and some will become permanent unless treated.</p>
<p>What can affect the pulse?</p>
<p>It may be higher than normal (tachycardia = heart rate above 100 bpm). Tachycardia can be classified as sinus, ventricular or supraventricular, and there will be different causes for each type. It can occur:</p>
<p>if there is an infection or a high temperature (pyrexia);
when the patient is anxious;
after exercise;
after stimulants such as coffee or cigarettes;
in some medical conditions such as hyperthyroidism; and
with some medication, for example adrenaline and salbutamol.</p>
<p>It may also be lower than normal (bradycardia = heart rate below 50 bpm), and this may occur:</p>
<p>in athletes;
in some medical conditions, such as hypothyroidism, myocardial infarction, pericardial tamponade, adrenal insufficiency and sick sinus syndrome;
with some medication, such as beta blockers, digoxin, calcium channel blockers, amiodarone, clonidine and verapamil;
in hypoxia; and
in hypothermia.</p>
<p>Or it may be irregular, without normal rhythm. This can occur in normal healthy hearts, but may also occur:</p>
<p>in some types of heart disease, for example atrial fibrillation;
where there is an electrolyte imbalance;
if there are changes in heart muscle; and
if there is injury from a myocardial infarction (heart attack).</p>
<p>Pulse amplitude refers to the strength of the pulse and the elasticity of the artery wall. A pulse may be described as strong or 'bounding', which may indicate infection. Or it may be weak, faint or 'thready', which may signify shock or hypovolaemia (low fluid levels).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="recording-the-radial-or-brachial-pulse-the-procedure-see-figure-11-4">RECORDING THE RADIAL OR BRACHIAL PULSE: THE PROCEDURE (SEE FIGURE 11.4)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-the-radial-or-brachial-pulse-the-procedure-see-figure-11-4" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Use two fingers, and press quite firmly over the area at the base of the thumb for the radial pulse (see Figure 11.5) and on the inside of the elbow for the brachial pulse (see Figure 11.6).</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-03152723.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg">
Figure 11.4 HCA taking pulse at wrist</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Record the rate by counting how many beats there are in 30 seconds, then multiply this figure by two to record the beats per minute.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the pulse is irregular, you will need to measure it for a full minute.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Document the result immediately, and always check with the doctor or nurse if the pulse seems very fast or slow or is irregular. You may also notice if the pulse seems unusually weak or is very strong or bounding in nature. These may be important observations, so always report them.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Draw a body outline and see if you can mark where all the various pulses can be found. Check your answers against Figure 11.7 at the end of this chapter.</p>
<p>Using Figures 11.5 and 11.6 for guidance, see if you can find your own pulses in the wrist (radial pulse) and inside the elbow (brachial pulse).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-00dd4698.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg">
Figure 11.5 Brachial pulse</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-b851a5ca.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg">
Figure 11.6 Radial pulse</p>
<p>Measuring a pulse with a Doppler can be useful in patients who have or are at risk of arterial disease, but specific training is required to use the Doppler correctly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="respiratory-rate">RESPIRATORY RATE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#respiratory-rate" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The respiratory rate is a key vital sign, and it is used to detect early changes when monitoring very ill patients.</p>
<p>We breathe in oxygen (required to produce energy) and breathe out carbon dioxide (a waste product of metabolism), and most of us will breathe at a rate of about 12-20 breaths per minute. It is an essential but subconscious mechanism, and we only become aware of it when it becomes difficult or if we know we are being observed.</p>
<p>The respiratory rate may be affected by:</p>
<p>exercise;
stress and emotion;</p>
<ul>
<li>illness; and
some medication.</li>
</ul>
<p>A rate of less than 12 breaths per minute or more than 25 breaths per minute is considered abnormal (in adults).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="recording-the-respiratory-rate-the-procedure">Recording the respiratory rate: the procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-the-respiratory-rate-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Look. Is the patient distressed? Can they complete a sentence without stopping for breath? Are there any</li>
</ol>
<p>signs of cyanosis (for example, a blue colour around the lips)? Whenever you are unsure, always refer the patient back to the doctor or nurse.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Note how the patient breathes, as well as the rate. A person who has difficulty breathing will be using all their accessory muscles, sometimes lifting their shoulders up and down to help the lungs expand. There may be intercostal recession (sucking in of the skin between the ribs), and this is one of the key signs of respiratory distress. Some patients will also breathe out through pursed lips. This is very typical of patients with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), and is a method of trying to expel all the carbon dioxide slowly. Rapid expiration is more likely to force the diseased airways to collapse.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Listen to the breathing. Is there an audible wheeze or other abnormal noise?</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Recording a patient's respiratory rate can be difficult because the rate will change if the patient knows they are being observed. Because of this, it is better to count the breaths per minute when holding the wrist as if still checking the pulse rate, so that the patient is unaware. As with measuring the pulse, you should count for 30 seconds and multiply by two to achieve the rate per minute. If the patient is breathing irregularly, you will need to count for a full minute.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pulse-oximetry">PULSE OXIMETRY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pulse-oximetry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A pulse oximeter provides a non-invasive method of measuring the oxygenation of a patient's blood. It works by shining an infrared light through the tissues of the body and detecting the colour difference between oxygenated and unoxygenated blood.</p>
<p>A beam of infrared light is passed through the chosen area, and the degree of absorption will depend on the amount of oxygen in the blood. Pulse oximeters will often give a reading of the pulse rate as well.</p>
<p>Pulse oximetry is used routinely in intensive care, operating theatres and recovery rooms.</p>
<p>It may be used in a doctor's surgery to monitor a patient's oxygen levels when they are having respiratory difficulty, such as during an asthma attack or worsening COPD or during a heart attack. Some patients who are receiving medication that may depress their respiration, such as strong opiate analgesics, may also need to have their oxygen levels monitored.</p>
<p>Accuracy can be affected by the thickness and temperature of the skin, so it is important to place the sensor in the best possible place. This will normally be on an area where the skin is relatively thin, such as the fingertip or earlobe. Other factors, such as nail varnish, dirt, foreign objects or bright lighting, can affect the light transmission, and therefore give erroneous results. Pulse detection may be affected by excessive movement, shivering, poor circulation and atrial fibrillation.</p>
<p>The normal level for oxygen saturation of the blood is between 96 and 100 per cent. Levels below 90 per cent can be life-threatening.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recording-pulse-oximetry-the-procedure">Recording pulse oximetry: the procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-pulse-oximetry-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Ensure the patient is comfortable and warm enough.</li>
<li>Select a suitable site, such as the fingertip, and make sure that any nail varnish, dirt, etc. has been removed. Choose a finger where the skin is not callused or thickened.</li>
<li>Place the probe according to the manufacturer's instructions.</li>
<li>Make sure the sensor is also detecting the pulse.</li>
<li>Make a note of the oxygen level and remove the probe.</li>
<li>Clean and store the probe according to the manufacturer's instructions.</li>
<li>Document the reading and report any abnormal oxygen levels.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="temperature">TEMPERATURE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#temperature" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Words you need to know:</p>
<ul>
<li>Apyrexial: Normal temperature. Usually considered to be a range anywhere between <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>36.2</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">36.2^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">36.</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">2</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span> and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>37.7</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">37.7^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">37.</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">7</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>.</li>
<li>Pyrexia: High temperature. May also be referred to as a fever, and less commonly referred to as hyperthermia.</li>
<li>Hypothermia: Low temperature. We feel cold if our core temperature is <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>36</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">36^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>, start to shiver at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>35</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">35^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>, become clumsy and confused at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>34</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">34^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">4</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>, develop muscle stiffness at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>32</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">32^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">2</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>, and at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>28</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">28^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">8</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span> would be at risk of cardiac arrest.</li>
</ul>
<p>The temperature may be taken to check for an infection anywhere in the body when a patient is feeling unwell. It will also be checked routinely post-operatively to make sure a patient is not developing an infection. Patients with surgical wounds are more susceptible to infection, which can then delay healing.</p>
<p>When you consider the potential implications of an infection for any person, the importance of measuring the temperature accurately cannot be overemphasised. There are various devices for checking temperature, but the one used most commonly (and often incorrectly) is the tympanic thermometer. This uses an infrared light to detect the temperature at the tympanic membrane (eardrum).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recording-the-temperature-the-procedure">Recording the temperature: the procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-the-temperature-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Always check the manufacturer's instructions for use as the devices may vary slightly.</p>
<ol>
<li>Place a disposable cap over the nozzle of the thermometer.</li>
<li>Press the button to turn it on, and then place the nozzle just inside the ear canal and point it forwards.</li>
<li>Depress the button until a beep is heard, then gently withdraw the nozzle and note the temperature displayed.</li>
<li>Remember, the device must be placed just inside the ear canal, pointing towards the tympanic membrane, to take an accurate reading.</li>
<li>Report any temperature that falls outside the normal parameters to the doctor or nurse.</li>
</ol>
<p>Other devices to check the temperature may be used in some settings:</p>
<ul>
<li>A rectal thermometer provides the most reliable core temperature, but is obviously the most invasive and least 'user-friendly'!</li>
<li>Oral digital thermometers that can be placed inside the cheek (buccal) or under the tongue (sublingual) can be used, but must be held in place for long enough, and will give a reading that is slightly lower than core body temperature.</li>
<li>Body surface thermometers are placed in the axilla or groin, but again will need to be held there for long enough, and may give a significantly lower result than the actual core body temperature.</li>
<li>Forehead thermometers are the least intrusive, and can be useful for babies and toddlers.</li>
</ul>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="understanding-and-measuring-blood-pressure-accurately">Understanding and measuring blood pressure accurately<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#understanding-and-measuring-blood-pressure-accurately" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Measuring and recording blood pressure is an important task that is often delegated to the HCA. Before you learn how to perform this task, it is essential that you understand what you are doing and why. Only then can you learn how to make an accurate measurement and understand why it is so important.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-blood-pressure">WHAT IS BLOOD PRESSURE?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-blood-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Arteries are the blood vessels that carry blood away from the heart. Every time the heart contracts, it squeezes blood through the arteries and the blood exerts a force on the artery walls. This force is the blood pressure.</p>
<p>Human blood pressure was first recorded in 1847 by Carl Ludwig, who inserted a catheter into a patient's artery and connected the catheter to an invention called a kymograph. In 1896, the mercury-filled sphygmomanometer was developed by Scipione Riva-Rocci, and blood pressure was measured according to how high the pressure could lift a column of mercury (Welch Allyn 2008). We still refer to the measurement in millimetres of mercury (known by its chemical symbol of Hg).</p>
<p>The systolic pressure measures the force exerted by the blood on the artery walls at the peak of the left ventricular contraction. The left ventricle is the largest chamber in the heart (for more detail on heart anatomy, see Chapter 13). The systolic pressure is the higher of the two readings.</p>
<p>The diastolic pressure measures the force exerted on the artery walls when the heart is relaxed.</p>
<p>The pressure in the arteries will depend on how hard the heart is pumping and how much resistance there is in the arteries.</p>
<p>There are several things that will create an increase in the resistance in the artery:</p>
<p>The word diastolic sounds more relaxed than systolic – this is a good way to remember the difference. You could also think of diastolic as referring to the downbeat phase.</p>
<ul>
<li>If the diameter of the artery is reduced, as for example in atherosclerosis, where there are fatty deposits sticking to the artery walls</li>
<li>If the walls of the artery are stiff or rigid. Think of an old hosepipe compared with a new one. In the new piece of hose, the walls are more elastic and stretchy. The pressure can be absorbed as the wall stretches, whereas in the rigid hose the pressure will be greater. As arteries age, they become stiffer, so blood pressure will invariably increase a little with age.</li>
<li>If the blood is thicker or stickier than normal. This can happen when the number of red blood cells increases in a condition called polycythaemia. Red blood cells carry oxygen, so patients who have problems taking on enough oxygen (such as those with respiratory disease) may adapt by producing more red blood cells. Smokers may also have this problem.</li>
<li>If the volume of blood is increased. Again, imagine what happens when you turn up the tap and force more water through a hose. The volume of blood may be increased in patients who are having intravenous fluids or those who are retaining fluid, such as in kidney disease.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-hypertension-and-why-does-it-matter">WHAT IS HYPERTENSION AND WHY DOES IT MATTER?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-hypertension-and-why-does-it-matter" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are two types of hypertension:</p>
<ul>
<li>essential hypertension, where the cause is not known; and</li>
<li>secondary hypertension, which may be due to medication (e.g. combined contraceptive pill, steroids), kidney disease or hormone problems.</li>
</ul>
<p>A patient may be diagnosed with hypertension if the blood pressure measurements show an isolated high systolic pressure (e.g. 180/70) or an isolated high diastolic pressure (e.g. 130/100) or both (e.g. 170/110).</p>
<p>Pulse pressure refers to the difference between the systolic and diastolic blood pressures, and this is often increased in older people, who may exhibit normal diastolic readings with isolated systolic hypertension, thought to be due to age-related stiffness of the arteries.</p>
<p>Hypertension can only be diagnosed after several readings (usually a minimum of three) at different times of the day when the patient is relaxed. The phenomenon of white coat hypertension is well known, and refers to how some people will always have a higher blood pressure when they are in a clinical setting, probably due to anxiety (often without the patient realising). Because of this, the current guidelines now suggest that all patients should be offered ambulatory blood pressure monitoring (ABPM) or home blood pressure monitoring (HBPM) if they cannot tolerate ABPM (NICE 2011).</p>
<p>These patients will need to have a 24-hour monitor to make an accurate diagnosis or may be advised to purchase a home monitor to perform serial readings at home. In this case, they must be advised to buy a monitor that is validated by the British and Irish Hypertension Society (BIHS) or the British Heart Foundation (BHF) to ensure accuracy. For a list of blood pressure monitors validated for home and clinical use, see BIHS (2017).</p>
<p>The home monitor should be checked against the surgery monitor.</p>
<p>There are various grades of hypertension:</p>
<ul>
<li>grade 1 (mild hypertension): 140–159/90–99;</li>
<li>grade 2 (moderate hypertension): 160–179/100–109; and</li>
<li>grade 3 (severe hypertension): greater than or equal to 180/110.</li>
</ul>
<p>Do you know when to bring a patient back for further readings and when to refer them to the nurse or doctor? For HCAs who work in primary care, a flow chart based on the NICE guidelines (NICE 2011) is provided in Box 12.1 at the end of the chapter, but you may wish to discuss this with your nurse or doctor and amend it in line with your surgery protocol.</p>
<p>ACTIVITY</p>
<p>How many factors can you think of that might increase your risk of developing high blood pressure and heart disease (see Figure 12.1)?</p>
<p>Check your answers against those given in Box 12.2 at the end of the chapter.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-d32aec9b.jpeg" alt="img-26.jpeg">
Figure 12.1 An unhealthy lifestyle</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="so-what-s-all-the-fuss-about">SO, WHAT'S ALL THE FUSS ABOUT?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#so-what-s-all-the-fuss-about" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Hypertension is often referred to as 'the silent killer' because it usually has no symptoms, but over time - if left untreated - it can cause strain on the heart and the arteries. It is one of the most important preventable causes of premature heart disease and death in the UK (NICE 2011). There are many possible complications, including angina, heart attacks, heart failure, stroke, peripheral vascular disease, kidney damage and eye damage. It is often under-diagnosed and inadequately treated.</p>
<p>The role of the HCA in measuring and recording BP accurately is therefore vital. When a diagnosis is finally made, it has huge implications for the patient and the NHS:</p>
<p>There is no cure, and patients will need lifelong treatment.
It is often best controlled by using two or more medications.
Many treatments cause side effects.
More tablets mean more potential interactions with other medications.
There is increased cost to the individual and the NHS.
Patients require regular monitoring and blood tests.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="auscultatory-or-manual-method-for-checking-the-bp-what-are-you-listening-to">AUSCULTATORY OR MANUAL METHOD FOR CHECKING THE BP: WHAT ARE YOU LISTENING TO?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#auscultatory-or-manual-method-for-checking-the-bp-what-are-you-listening-to" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Although many surgeries and wards have now disposed of mercury sphygmomanometers in line with European regulations on mercury control, blood pressure is still recorded in mmHg. Many hospital wards and surgeries now use automatic monitors, but these cannot be used on patients who have an irregular heartbeat such as in atrial fibrillation. It is therefore important that all HCAs have the training to enable them to take a blood pressure using the auscultatory (listening) method. To do this, the cuff is wrapped around the upper arm and inflated until it compresses the brachial artery. The blood cannot flow through, so no sounds are heard through the stethoscope, which is placed over the artery. Gradually, the cuff is deflated until the blood can just force its way through the artery each time the heart contracts and pushes the blood out. This opening and closing of the artery causes a tapping sound that can be heard through the stethoscope, and this is the systolic pressure. As the cuff is deflated still further, the artery opens and blood flows more easily so that the forced tapping sound muffles or disappears altogether. This change in the sound represents the diastolic pressure when the heart is relaxed. These sounds are called the Korotkoff sounds after the man who discovered them, and there are five possible sounds that can be detected. You usually listen for Korotkoff 1 when the tapping sound begins to denote the systolic pressure, and then for Korotkoff 5 when the sound</p>
<p>disappears completely to denote the diastolic pressure. Sometimes the tapping sound may continue, in which case you record Korotkoff 4 – the point at which the tapping sound becomes muffled.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="measuring-blood-pressure-the-procedure-see-figure-12-2">MEASURING BLOOD PRESSURE: THE PROCEDURE (SEE FIGURE 12.2)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measuring-blood-pressure-the-procedure-see-figure-12-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS19 Undertake Routine Clinical Measurements to use as a check list for your competencies (Skills for Health 2011b).</p>
<ol>
<li>Explain what you are doing to your patient and why. Make sure you have their informed consent before you continue.</li>
<li>Wash or decontaminate your hands before and after the procedure.</li>
<li>Make sure the patient is seated comfortably (preferably sitting quietly for five minutes before proceeding). The arm should be free of any constrictive clothing and supported at the level of the heart.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-30fdacd3.jpeg" alt="img-27.jpeg">
Figure 12.2 Measuring blood pressure</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>
<p>Wrap the cuff around the upper arm with the centre of the bladder located over the brachial artery. The cuff must be large enough so that the bladder circles 80 per cent of the arm. In larger patients, a large cuff must be used to avoid the risk of inaccurate high readings.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Some teaching resources advocate the idea of positioning the cuff so that the tubing comes from the top. This avoids noise from the tubing bumping against the stethoscope from being confused with the arterial sounds.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is good practice to palpate the brachial or radial pulse and then inflate the cuff until the pulse disappears. Note the readings when the pulse disappears and add 30. Remember this reading. Now deflate the cuff.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Position the stethoscope over the brachial artery and reinflate the cuff to the level you noted previously. You should not be able to hear the tapping from the brachial artery. If you can still hear it, inflate the cuff a little more until it disappears.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Deflate the cuff very slowly (this takes practice). You should record the reading when you first hear the pulse come back in. This is the systolic reading (Korotkoff 1). This should be recorded to the nearest 2 mm, and never rounded up or down.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Keep deflating the cuff very slowly until the tapping sound disappears or becomes muffled (Korotkoff 4 or 5). This is the second (diastolic) reading.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Measure the BP in both arms initially and record the highest reading. Always use this arm in the future. If the difference in readings between the arms is <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>20</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">20\mathrm{mmHg}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8778em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">20</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mmHg</span></span></span></span></span></span> or more, always report this to the nurse or doctor. It may indicate a problem called subclavian stenosis, and should prompt further investigation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Take a second measurement from the chosen arm during the consultation. If this is very different from the first reading, then take a third reading. Record the lower of the last two measurements as the clinic reading.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Elderly and diabetic patients may need to have their blood pressure checked again after two minutes standing to check for <strong>postural hypotension</strong> (a common cause of falls in the elderly). In this case, you should still try to support the arm at the level of the heart so there is no muscular contraction in the arm, as this may interfere with the reading.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If using digital/automatic monitors, wrap the cuff around the upper arm as instructed in the monitor guidelines. Make sure the arm is free of restrictive clothing and is supported comfortably at the level of the heart. Press the record button and wait for the cuff to inflate and deflate before making a note of the recording. If the monitor indicates an error (e.g. if the patient has an arrhythmia), the manual method must be used and the nurse or doctor informed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Always discourage the patient from talking during the procedure.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Report the readings or recall the patient for further measurements according to your protocol.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>BP readings must always be recorded using the appropriate read code or template so that they can be easily accessed for future consultations and for audit purposes.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="blood-pressure-quiz">BLOOD PRESSURE QUIZ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-pressure-quiz" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-12-1-guidelines-for-hcas-for-the-referral-of-patients-according-to-bp">Box 12.1 Guidelines for HCAs for the referral of patients according to BP<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-12-1-guidelines-for-hcas-for-the-referral-of-patients-according-to-bp" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>&gt;179</th><th>160-179</th><th>140-159</th><th>135-139</th><th>&lt;135</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>&gt;109</td><td>100-109</td><td>90-99</td><td>85-89</td><td>&lt;85</td></tr><tr><td>Refer to the doctor on the same day.</td><td>Recheck three times over next two weeks, then make an appointment with the doctor.</td><td>Reassess three times weekly, then make an appointment with the doctor.</td><td>Lifestyle advice, and reassess annually if not on treatment, or in six months if on treatment.</td><td>Lifestyle advice, and reassess in five years if not on treatment, or in six months if on treatment.</td></tr><tr><td>All patients whose BP is equal to/above 140/90 should be offered ambulatory blood pressure monitoring (ABPM), or home blood pressure monitoring (HBPM) if ABPM is declined or not tolerated (NICE 2011).</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-12-2-risk-factors-for-developing-high-blood-pressure-and-heart-disease">Box 12.2 Risk factors for developing high blood pressure and heart disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-12-2-risk-factors-for-developing-high-blood-pressure-and-heart-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Poor diet, high in fat or salt</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>High cholesterol, high LDL, high triglycerides, low HDL</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Atheromatous arteries</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Male gender</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>High levels of alcohol</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Obesity</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Lack of exercise</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Increasing age</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Diabetes</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ethnic group</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Family history</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Smoking (although links with sustained hypertension have yet to be established)</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Using a framework for reflection (see Chapter 2), try reflecting on a consultation where you performed a blood pressure measurement.</p>
<p>How did it go and what did you learn?</p>
<p>How does a blood pressure monitor like to make jokes? Off the cuff!</p>
<p>13</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="understanding-the-heart">Understanding the heart<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#understanding-the-heart" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>How to perform the electrocardiograph (ECG)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="history-of-the-ecg">HISTORY OF THE ECG<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#history-of-the-ecg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In the nineteenth century, it became clear that the heart generated electricity. In 1924, Willem Einthoven discovered the electrocardiographic features of the heart and realised that these could be used to detect abnormalities. He assigned the letters PQRST to the various deflections, and was awarded the Nobel Prize for his discovery.</p>
<p>Before you can understand an ECG or how to perform the test accurately, you need to have a basic understanding of the heart and how it works.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-does-the-heart-do">WHAT DOES THE HEART DO?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-does-the-heart-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It is a dual muscular pump. The right and left side of the heart are separated by the septum.</li>
<li>It squeezes blood through one-way valves in a network of narrowing arteries to the lungs and the rest of the body (see Figure 13.1).</li>
<li>The heart rate for an average male is between 60 and 80 beats per minute.</li>
<li>It beats approximately 100,000 times per day (2,920,000,000 times in an average lifespan of 80 years).</li>
<li>If it stops for just a few seconds, all hell breaks loose!</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="heart-anatomy">HEART ANATOMY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#heart-anatomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The heart has four chambers, the right and left atria and the right and left ventricles (see Figure 13.2).</p>
<p>The arteries carry blood away from the heart, and the veins bring blood back to the heart (remember, artery begins with 'a' for 'away'). The arteries are therefore under greater pressure and have thicker walls. You will also feel a pulse in the arteries corresponding with the heartbeat, but you will not be able to feel a pulse in a vein. This is because the pressure has dropped by the time the blood has travelled through the circulatory system from the heart via arteries to arterioles, then to capillaries and then to venuoles, before being transported back to the heart via the veins.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-a433a12a.jpeg" alt="img-29.jpeg">
Figure 13.1 Circulatory system</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-65ef95c9.jpeg" alt="img-30.jpeg">
Figure 13.2 Heart anatomy showing blood flow</p>
<p>Blood that has been around the body and exhausted its supply of oxygen (deoxygenated blood) is transported back to the right side of the heart via the superior vena cava (coming from the upper body) and the inferior vena cava (from the lower body).</p>
<p>It enters the right atrium and then travels through the tricuspid valve to the right ventricle. From here, it leaves the heart via the right and left pulmonary arteries to the corresponding right or left lung.</p>
<p>In the lungs, the pulmonary arteries become smaller arterioles and then even smaller capillaries. The</p>
<p>capillaries have walls that are one cell thick, and they form a mesh around the lower end of the airways where there are tiny dilated sacs called alveoli (see Figure 13.3). This is where inhaled oxygen can be transferred across the very thin walls of the airways and surrounding capillaries, into the bloodstream. The alveoli are shaped like bunches of grapes to increase the surface area across which the gases can be exchanged and this makes the process more efficient.</p>
<p>Carbon Dioxide (the waste gas produced from metabolism in all the organs of the body) is also transferred from the blood back into the airways to be exhaled.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-80b029ef.jpeg" alt="img-31.jpeg">
Figure 13.3 Alveolus</p>
<p>The reoxygenated blood is now transported back through the pulmonary veins to the left atrium. From here, it travels through the mitral valve into the left ventricle. This is the largest chamber in the heart, and when it contracts it squeezes the blood with its vital oxygen out through the aorta.</p>
<p>The aorta divides into an ascending aorta, sending blood to the upper body, and the descending aorta, sending blood to the lower body.</p>
<p>This vital transport system can only be maintained if the heart beats at an appropriate rate.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="what-makes-the-heart-contract">WHAT MAKES THE HEART CONTRACT?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-makes-the-heart-contract" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The heart is a muscle. Inside the right atrium, there is an area of specialised heart tissue called the sinus node or sino-atrial (SA) node (see Figure 13.2). This node produces an electrical impulse that travels down through the right atrium to another area of specialised tissue called the atrioventricular node. From here, the electrical impulses travel down through the bundle of His and spread out around the heart muscle, causing it to contract. When the ventricles contract, the blood is pushed out through the arteries.</p>
<p>These electrical currents spread through the whole body. They can be picked up by applying electrodes to various parts of the body and connecting them to an electrocardiograph (ECG). The ECG is a graphic recording of electrical processes that make the heart muscle contract.</p>
<p>Abnormalities result in changes in the ECG, and can help with diagnosis depending on the extent and position of the changes.</p>
<p>The ECG detects the electrical current flowing through the heart, and depending on the size and state of the heart muscle, it will produce deflections on the ECG monitor.</p>
<p>It also shows the heart rate and rhythm.</p>
<p>PQRST</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-5577c73b.jpeg" alt="img-32.jpeg">
Figure 13.4 PQRST complex</p>
<ul>
<li>P represents the electrical activity during contraction of the smaller chambers (atria).</li>
<li>QRS represents the current that causes contraction of the left and right ventricles. Because the ventricles have a larger muscle mass than the atria, there is a larger deflection on the ECG recording.</li>
<li>T represents the repolarisation (like a resetting of the spring) of the ventricles. The repolarisation of the atria is hidden in the QRS complex (see Figure 13.4).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="heart-disease">HEART DISEASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#heart-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Various types of heart disease that affect the heart muscle or interfere with the rate or rhythm may be detected on an ECG.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ischaemic-heart-disease">Ischaemic heart disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ischaemic-heart-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Remember, the heart is a muscle, so it also needs an independent blood supply to obtain its oxygen and nutrients. This is delivered via the right and left coronary arteries.</p>
<p>In heart disease, these arteries may be narrow or blocked by fatty deposits called atheroma. This restricts the flow of blood to the heart muscle (ischaemia). The restriction is initially more of a problem when the heart muscle is working hard as in exercise or exertion. The heart needs more blood for its supply of oxygen and nutrients, and if the blood supply is compromised, the heart muscle will not be able to work properly and will become painful. This is what causes the pain of angina, which is typically felt during exertion or stress. The pain will go after rest unless there is a complete blockage or clot. In this case, the pain will not go with rest as the affected muscle is completely deprived of oxygen and it dies (heart attack or myocardial infarction; see Figure 13.5). When this happens, the outcome will depend on where the blockage has occurred. If it is at the end of one of the tiny branches of the coronary artery, it may only be experienced as a feeling of indigestion for a while (a silent heart attack), and this may only be detected on a subsequent ECG. If the blockage is further up the coronary artery, it will affect a larger area of heart muscle, and may cause the heart to stop (cardiac arrest) depending on the amount of heart muscle involved.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-b21e2bd9.jpeg" alt="img-33.jpeg">
Figure 13.5 Heart attack</p>
<p>The pain of angina or a heart attack may be felt in the chest, jaw or left arm. Occasionally, it may be felt in the right arm. This is because of the way in which the nerves are distributed in the chest wall. Other possible symptoms may include a feeling of pressure in the chest, indigestion, nausea, vomiting, sweating, shortness of breath, and feeling dizzy or faint.</p>
<p>The ECG recording may look different in someone who has ischaemic heart disease or who has had a heart attack. This is because the muscle may be damaged, so the flow of electric current through the muscle is altered and the deflections on the recording will be different.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="atrial-fibrillation-af">Atrial fibrillation (AF)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#atrial-fibrillation-af" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This occurs when the sinus node fires erratic impulses, causing the atria to 'fibrillate' or quiver when they beat very rapidly and irregularly. Because the atria are beating so fast (sometimes over 300 beats per minute), the blood is not pumped through to the ventricles effectively and it may pool in the left atria, where it is then more likely to form clots. If the clots break loose and are pumped around the body, they can then lodge in blood vessels in the brain and cause a stroke, or they may block an artery supplying the heart and cause a heart attack.</p>
<p>The ECG recording will look different in atrial fibrillation. Because the atria are not beating properly, the P wave does not show up clearly on the ECG recording. It may only appear as a fibrillatory line like a series of small bumps. This is very characteristic of AF.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="heart-failure">Heart failure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#heart-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is when the heart cannot pump the full amount of blood with each heartbeat, and is therefore unable to meet the needs of the body. It is classified as systolic and diastolic failure, and may affect only the right ventricle or the left ventricle, or both</p>
<p>It is commonly caused by heart disease, such as after a heart attack, or may occur because of uncontrolled hypertension. It may also be a complication of atrial fibrillation or valve disease.</p>
<p>It is estimated that about half a million people have heart failure in the UK (Townsend et al. 2015).</p>
<p>The incidence rises steeply with age and carries a poor prognosis that is worse than most cancers, with 30-40 per cent of patients diagnosed with heart failure dying within a year, usually because by the time it is diagnosed it is quite advanced (NICE 2010). Echocardiography is key to the diagnosis of heart failure, with</p>
<p>earlier diagnosis helping to ensure that the problem can be treated more quickly to prevent early complications.</p>
<p>There are many possible symptoms, including shortness of breath, swelling of the feet and legs, a chronic lack of energy, difficulty sleeping due to breathing problems, a swollen or tender abdomen and a loss of appetite, a cough with frothy sputum, increased urination at night, and confusion or an impaired memory.</p>
<p>The ECG recording may show the possible cause of heart failure, such as AF or a previous myocardial infarction, and it may demonstrate possible signs of heart failure such as an enlarged left ventricle (left ventricular hypertrophy). It can be useful to signal the need for further investigations but it cannot provide a definitive diagnosis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-12-lead-ecg">The 12-Lead ECG<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-12-lead-ecg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When detecting any abnormalities in the current flowing through the heart, the ECG can determine where the abnormality may be. It does this by viewing the heart from different directions. To record an ECG, there are six chest electrodes and four limb electrodes, but it becomes a little confusing because it is referred to as a 12-lead ECG (see Figure 13.6).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="so-how-does-6-4-a-12-lead-ecg">So how does 6 + 4 = a 12-lead ECG?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#so-how-does-6-4-a-12-lead-ecg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>A lead is a view of the electrical activity of the heart from a particular angle across the body, obtained by using signals from different combinations of electrodes.</li>
<li>To obtain a 12-lead ECG, you have one electrode attached to each of the limbs and six electrodes placed on the chest, ten in total, but you get 12 ‘leads', or pictures from the combinations of different electrodes.</li>
<li>For the chest, the six leads take their picture (or view) from an electrode each, and are referred to as unipolar (leads V1--V6).</li>
<li>Three of the limb leads are made up from the signals of two electrodes each, and are referred to as bipolar (leads I--III).</li>
<li>The other three limb leads are made up from signals of three electrodes each, and are called augmented leads (aVF, aVR, aVL).</li>
<li>The electrode placed on the right foot serves only as an earth, and does not play a part in the recording.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="some-reasons-for-performing-an-ecg">Some reasons for performing an ECG<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#some-reasons-for-performing-an-ecg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Chest pain? Always make sure you have professional help at hand in case of sudden deterioration in the patient.</li>
<li>History of chest pain or heart attack.</li>
<li>Hypertension.</li>
<li>Palpitations.</li>
<li>Patient at risk.</li>
<li>Prior to some drug treatments.</li>
<li>Shortness of breath.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-11923896.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg">
Figure 13.6 HCA performing an ECG</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="recording-an-ecg-the-procedure-adapted-from-british-cardiovascular-society-2017">RECORDING AN ECG: THE PROCEDURE (adapted from British Cardiovascular Society 2017)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-an-ecg-the-procedure-adapted-from-british-cardiovascular-society-2017" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS130 Perform Routine Electrocardiograph Procedures to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Health 2011b).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="preparing-the-patient">Preparing the patient<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparing-the-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Good preparation is an essential part of the process in the quest for the most accurate ECG recording:</p>
<ol>
<li>Confirm the patient's identity and make sure this correlates with the name on the computer screen.</li>
<li>Explain the process, including what will happen before, during and after the procedure. Ensure the patient understands and is happy to proceed (obtain informed consent).</li>
<li>Consider privacy and comfort in what can be a distressing and embarrassing procedure for some patients. A trained chaperone should be offered according to the local policy.</li>
<li>The patient should be in a semi-recumbent position of approximately 45 degrees, and any significant variation from this should be recorded. The limbs should be supported.</li>
<li>There should be unrestricted access to the chest, arms and legs. While respecting the cultural sensitivities of the patient, ask them to remove all clothing that may impede access to the electrode positions. Remember to offer a 'dignity blanket' for female patients to minimise embarrassment.</li>
<li>Reassure the anxious patient. Anxiety will make the heart beat faster, so the result will not be accurate. Make sure they understand that the ECG is a recording of electrical activity from them and is not a current being passed through them!</li>
<li>If the skin is sweaty or oily, clean it with a mild detergent or alcohol wipe to ensure a good contact. Similarly, if the patient has a lot of chest hair, it is sometimes necessary to shave those areas where the electrodes are to be stuck on. Always use a disposable single-use razor. If exfoliation is required, rub the skin lightly with a paper towel, gauze swab or abrasive tape designed for the purpose.</li>
<li>Discourage any talking or movement as this involves the use of other muscles. Make sure the patient's fists are not clenched. All muscles contract because of electrical stimulus, so there is the potential for this to interfere with the recording from the heart.</li>
<li>It is important to place the electrodes consistently and in the recommended positions to achieve the most accurate recording. Studies have shown that electrodes are frequently placed incorrectly, producing significant diagnostic differences (Kligfield et al. 2007; Kania et al. 2014). Because of this, there are</li>
</ol>
<p>recommended positions that should be adhered to whenever possible, or – if not possible – then the alternative positions used should be documented. Electrodes on the limbs and chest should be placed as in Table 13.1 (see also Figure 13.7).</p>
<p>Table 13.1 Recommended positions for the electrodes</p>
