<table><thead><tr><th>Electrode</th><th>Recommended position</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Red</td><td>Inside the right wrist</td></tr><tr><td>Yellow</td><td>Inside the left wrist</td></tr><tr><td>Green</td><td>Inside the left ankle</td></tr><tr><td>Black</td><td>Inside the right ankle</td></tr><tr><td>V1</td><td>Fourth right intercostal space at sternal border</td></tr><tr><td>V2</td><td>Fourth left intercostal space at sternal border</td></tr><tr><td>V3</td><td>Midway between V2 and V4</td></tr><tr><td>V4</td><td>Fifth left intercostal space in middavicular line</td></tr><tr><td>V5</td><td>Left anterior axillary line at same horizontal level as V4</td></tr><tr><td>V6</td><td>Left mid axillary line at same horizontal level as V4</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tips-for-remembering-the-limb-leads">Tips for remembering the limb leads<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tips-for-remembering-the-limb-leads" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It may be helpful to remember the mnemonic 'ride your green bike' (RYGB) as a memory aid for the sequence of limb electrode colours. The red electrode is applied to the inside of the right wrist, the yellow electrode to the inside of the left wrist, the green electrode to the left foot, and the black electrode to the right foot.</p>
<p>When a patient has a tremor or is an amputee, it may be necessary to position the limb lead on the corresponding shoulder or lower torso. Always document any variation from the regular position.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tips-for-placing-the-chest-leads">Tips for placing the chest leads<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tips-for-placing-the-chest-leads" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>To identify the fourth intercostal space for the chest leads, it may be helpful to slide a finger from the base of the throat downwards as far as a bony lump (angle of Louis). Then slide the finger to the left or right to identify the second intercostal space. From this position, run your fingers downward across the next rib and the next one. You are now in the fourth intercostal space.</p>
<p>Alternatively, count the dips in between the ribs, starting at the clavicle and working downwards to the fourth dip, but take care not to count the very first small dip between the clavicle and first rib.</p>
<p>Chest leads 3-5 can be placed under or over breast tissue.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-3207eff7.jpeg" alt="img-35.jpeg">
Figure 13.7 ECG electrodes on the chest</p>
<ol start="10">
<li>Once the electrodes have been applied and good contact ensured, press the button to start the machine and record a 12-lead ECG at  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>25</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">25\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">25</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  /second with a gain setting of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10\mathrm{mm / mv}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mm/mv</span></span></span></span></span></span> . All filters should be off for the first attempt because use of the filter may distort the ECG. The filter setting should only be used if there is interference from other muscle movement, and should be clearly marked on the recording. If the rhythm is irregular, an additional rhythm strip should be recorded, usually on lead II for a minimum of 10 seconds. This must be done manually.</li>
<li>When the recording is done, ensure correct labelling and show it to the requesting practitioner before the patient leaves the surgery (or proceed according to the local protocol).</li>
<li>Scan it immediately on to the computer and make sure the paper recording is stored away from the clinical area to preserve confidentiality.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="other-factors-to-consider">Other factors to consider<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#other-factors-to-consider" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="infection-control">Infection control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#infection-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Observe correct hand decontamination before and after the procedure, and clean the couch between patients. Dispose of couch roll and disposable razors in line with your local policy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="health-and-safety">Health and safety<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#health-and-safety" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Make sure the couch is at the correct height. Ideally, couches in the treatment room should be adjustable so that the patient can get on and off safely and the practitioner can attend to the patient without bending. Take care when helping patients on or off the couch.</p>
<p>All electrical equipment should be checked annually and the date recorded on the equipment. It should also be calibrated annually or as recommended by the manufacturer.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="dextrocardia">Dextrocardia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dextrocardia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is a common form of cardiac malposition where the heart is positioned on the right side rather than the left side. In this case, V1 and V2 can stay in the same position, but V3-V6 should be positioned on the right side of the chest using the same intercostal spacing and anatomical landmarks as previously described.</p>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers.</p>
<p>HEART AND ECG QUIZ</p>
<ol>
<li>List three reasons why a patient might need to have an ECG.</li>
<li>What are the four chambers of the heart called?</li>
<li>Which blood vessels carry blood away from the heart?</li>
<li>Which vein carries blood back to the heart from the upper body?</li>
<li>Where do the pulmonary arteries carry the blood to, and why?</li>
<li>Which area of the heart normally produces the electrical impulse that makes the heart muscle contract?</li>
<li>How should the patient be positioned to have an ECG?</li>
<li>Where would you place the electrode V2?</li>
<li>Where would you place the red electrode?</li>
<li>How do you get 12 leads (pictures) from ten electrodes?</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Using a framework for reflection (see Chapter 2), try reflecting on a consultation where you performed an ECG recording.</p>
<p>How did it go and what did you learn?</p>
<p>The ups and downs in life are important to keep us going because a straight line, even in an ECG, means we're not alive!</p>
<p>14</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="venepuncture-and-capillary-blood-testing">Venepuncture and capillary blood testing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#venepuncture-and-capillary-blood-testing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Best practice</p>
<p>Venepuncture is a task that is often one of the first to be taken on by a HCA, and many will be extremely proficient in the procedure. It is, however, another one of those tasks where it is so essential to get it right to achieve the best and most accurate sample possible. This may sound obvious, but it is surprising how many errors 'competent' phlebotomists can make that can have significant implications for the patient. This chapter will provide some of the necessary underpinning knowledge in the physiological process and will outline the correct procedure to achieve accurate sampling.</p>
<p>Before you consider performing venepuncture, make sure you are up to date with your hepatitis B protection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-clotting-process-in-venepuncture">THE CLOTTING PROCESS IN VENEPUNCTURE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-clotting-process-in-venepuncture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When a hole is made in the vein by a needle, platelets (small fragments of blood cells) become sticky and clump around the injury. Activated platelets and injured tissue produce chemicals that react with clotting factors in the blood. These clotting factors are known by the Roman numerals I-XIII. Factor I (fibrinogen) is converted into thin strands of solid protein called fibrin. These strands trap the platelets and blood cells to form a solid clot or plug. This process may take several hours, so it is important to remind the patient not to do anything after the procedure that may interfere with this or they risk further bleeding and subsequent bruising.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-that-may-interfere-with-the-clotting-process">Factors that may interfere with the clotting process<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-that-may-interfere-with-the-clotting-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Abnormal liver function (the liver produces the clotting factors).</li>
<li>Vitamin K levels (because factors II, VII, IX and X are dependent on Vitamin K).</li>
<li>Clotting disorders such as haemophilia.</li>
<li>Anticoagulants such as aspirin, warfarin and heparin.</li>
<li>Hypercoagulability disorders (where the blood clots too easily).</li>
<li>Low platelet counts such as in immunosuppression or leukaemia.</li>
<li>Platelet function disorders.</li>
<li>Heavy lifting or excessive movement within two hours of venepuncture.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="order-of-draw">ORDER OF DRAW<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#order-of-draw" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Blood samples should be drawn in the correct order to avoid cross-contamination of additives between tubes (WHO 2010).</p>
<p>Check with your local laboratory for current guidelines regarding which colour sample bottles to use for which test and the order of draw. A general rule is that blood glucose samples should be taken last in the order of draw as these bottles contain an enzyme that may adversely affect other tests if there is any cross-contamination.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="factors-affecting-the-quality-of-the-sample">FACTORS AFFECTING THE QUALITY OF THE SAMPLE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-affecting-the-quality-of-the-sample" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are some factors that may affect the results of a blood sample that the blood-taker cannot do anything</p>
<p>about, such as the patient's age, gender, race, pregnancy and so on.</p>
<p>There are other factors that may affect the quality that will depend very much on the competence of the blood-taker.</p>
<p>When a blood sample is analysed in the laboratory, the levels of various substances that are present in the fluid part of the blood are measured. Haemolysis is the term used to describe the damage of red blood cells, which results in leakage of their contents into the blood serum. Where a blood sample is haemolysed, the test may give falsely high reading for substances such as potassium. This is because most of the potassium in the body is intracellular (contained within the cells). When the cell is damaged, the potassium leaks out into the serum, so the blood sample will give a false high reading. Potassium is very important for muscle contraction, particularly of the heart muscle, so patients who have a high potassium level will always need to be retested promptly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity">Activity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>List as many things as you can think of that may cause damage to the blood cells during and after venepuncture, or that may give inaccurate results for other reasons.</p>
<p>How could this affect patient care?</p>
<p>Check your answers in Box 14.1 at the end of the chapter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-bit-about-tourniquets">A BIT ABOUT TOURNIQUETS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-bit-about-tourniquets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some areas now recommend the use of single-use tourniquets, but if these are not available then you should use tourniquets with a quick and slow release mechanism. Position the tourniquet ten centimetres (a hand width) above the intended puncture site. It should be loosened or removed when the first tube starts to fill, and never left on for longer than is necessary.</p>
<p>Always remove the tourniquet before removing the needle from the vein.</p>
<p>If the vein is clearly visible and palpable, consider taking the blood without using a tourniquet. Prolonged use may be uncomfortable and can cause haemolysis, adversely affecting the quality of the sample.</p>
<p>Some research has identified contamination with MRSA on multiple-use tourniquets (Elhassan and Dixon 2011). There is an ongoing debate about the need for disposable tourniquets. The problem with these is that many of them do not incorporate a slow release mechanism, so they are either on or off. There is therefore no way to easily retighten the tourniquet if the blood flow stops.</p>
<p>If reusable tourniquets are used, then consider washing them at the end of every session and never use them if they are visibly contaminated. You must always use the type of tourniquet as stated in your local policy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="venepuncture-the-procedure-see-figure-14-2">VENEPUNCTURE: THE PROCEDURE (SEE FIGURE 14.2)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#venepuncture-the-procedure-see-figure-14-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS 132 Obtain Venous Blood Samples to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Care and Development 2011).</p>
<ol>
<li>Identify the patient by asking them to say their name and address and date of birth (you must have at least three points of reference).</li>
<li>Obtain informed verbal consent before proceeding. Advise the patient of the risk of possible bruising following the procedure.</li>
<li>Check the history. Has the patient had any previous problems with the procedure or any surgery to one side where use of the opposite arm may be advised (e.g. lymph node clearance)? Have they fasted if necessary?</li>
<li>Position the patient appropriately. Always lie them down if they have fainted before or if they are unsure. The chair should ideally be designed for the purpose, but failing this it should – as a minimum requirement – have arms to prevent the patient falling to the side.</li>
<li>Decontaminate your hands and put on gloves (WHO 2010). The wearing of gloves is not required in all</li>
</ol>
<p>areas. Observe your local policy.</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Prepare the equipment:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Blood collection bottles according to the tests to be collected (charts with blood bottle colours are available from all hospital pathology laboratories). Always put a spare set of bottles on the tray in case there are problems obtaining the blood. Check that bottles are in date.</li>
<li>Two needles (one spare); 20-, 21- and 22-gauge needles are all suitable.</li>
<li>Cotton wool ball.</li>
<li>Alcohol wipe.</li>
<li>Tourniquet.</li>
<li>Plaster or hypoallergenic tape.</li>
<li>Sharps box.</li>
<li>Bottle holder (if using the vacuette system).</li>
</ul>
<ol start="7">
<li>Identify the best arm and expose the antecubital fossa (i.e. inside the elbow; see Figure 14.1).</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-d117f612.jpeg" alt="img-36.jpeg">
Figure 14.1 Antecubital fossa</p>
<p>Avoid using the weak or paralysed arm if the patient has had a stroke. Any tight clothing should be removed. Support the patient's arm on a pad and advise the patient to keep the arm straight throughout the procedure. Look for the most visible and palpable or 'bouncy' vein. The median cubital vein is usually the easiest to puncture. The basilic vein may be used, but as it usually runs above the brachial artery and nerve there is a greater risk of an arterial stab or nerve damage, so it is best avoided if possible. Where there isn't a good visible vein, apply the tourniquet and take time to carefully palpate the area to identify the best vein. Time taken at this stage is time well spent! Try to avoid choosing a site where the veins are diverging as there is more risk of a bruise forming. Loosen the tourniquet once the vein is located.</p>
<ol start="8">
<li>Now clean the area using an alcohol wipe, working with a firm downward and outward motion across the whole area for 30 seconds (WHO 2010). In some areas, this step is omitted from the policy as there is no good evidence to support the cleansing of skin prior to the procedure. If the skin is visibly dirty, it should be washed with soap and water.</li>
<li>While the alcohol dries, prepare the equipment.</li>
<li>Retighten the tourniquet if necessary.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-d01b787f.jpeg" alt="img-37.jpeg">
Figure 14.2 HCA taking blood</p>
<p>Vacuette system (used in most areas in England and Wales):</p>
<ul>
<li>Attach the needle to the holder (vacuette).</li>
<li>Drop the sheath off the needle at 90 degrees to avoid any spur on the needle that might occur when the sheath is pulled off horizontally.</li>
<li>Warn the patient to expect a sharp scratch. Insert the needle, bevel upwards, into the vein at a 30-degree angle to about half the length of the needle.</li>
<li>Push on the first bottle (observing the order of draw) while firmly anchoring the holder so that the needle doesn't move in the vein.</li>
<li>When the blood starts to flow, loosen the tourniquet if it has been tightened. Allow the blood to finish filling the tube completely before gently removing and inverting the tube twice.</li>
<li>Apply the next bottle and repeat the procedure.</li>
</ul>
<p>Monovette system (used in some areas):</p>
<ul>
<li>Attach the needle to the first bottle (observing the order of draw) and twist to lock. Drop the sheath off the needle at 90 degrees to avoid any spur on the needle that might occur if the sheath is pulled off horizontally.</li>
<li>Warn the patient to expect a sharp scratch.</li>
<li>Insert the needle, bevel upwards, into the vein at a 30-degree angle to about half the length of the needle.</li>
<li>Pull out the plunger of the bottle until it clicks, taking care not to move the needle in the vein.</li>
<li>When the blood starts to flow, loosen the tourniquet if it has been tightened.</li>
<li>Allow the bottle to finish filling the tube completely before removing and inverting twice.</li>
<li>Attach the next bottle and repeat. Second and subsequent bottles can be prepared by pulling out the plunger until it clicks and then snapping it off.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="finishing-up">Finishing up:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#finishing-up" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Before taking the needle out, remember to remove the tourniquet.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove the needle and then apply the cotton wool, advising the patient to press firmly for a few minutes (they may need to press for longer if on anticoagulant therapy).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pick up all the bottles and invert fully again a further four times (so six in total). Check your laboratory chart, as many will now specify how many times each bottle should be inverted.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Label the bottles, checking details with the patient, and put into the pathology bag/form.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Apply an appropriate dressing to the venepuncture site (always check for allergies first).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dispose of sharps and equipment immediately, according to local policy.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove gloves and decontaminate hands.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Advise the patient about aftercare and follow-up.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Document the procedure. Include consent obtained, which blood tests were taken, who the requesting practitioner was, which arm was used and any problems that were encountered</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="troubleshooting">TROUBLESHOOTING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#troubleshooting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>What will you do if any of the following occur?</p>
<ul>
<li>The patient faints during the procedure. This depends on if they are safe or not. If they are lying down and quite safe, continue with the procedure. It is much better to do this than to have to go through the whole procedure again if it makes them faint! If they are not safe, take off the tourniquet and remove the needle. Advise the patient to lean forward with their head down or to lie down. Apply a dressing to the venepuncture site. Support the patient gently to the floor if necessary and elevate the legs. Recovery from a simple faint is usually quite quick, but they may need a glass of water and observation for a short while. If the blood has not been taken at this stage, refer them to a registered practitioner for further tests. Always have the patient checked over by a registered practitioner before they leave the surgery if they have been unwell.</li>
<li>An artery is punctured instead of a vein. This is an uncommon event but can happen, especially when choosing a vein on the inner edge of the antecubital fossa. This is where the brachial artery is usually situated, but it is deeper and can be distinguished from a vein because the artery will have a pulse and will not 'plump up' when the tourniquet is applied. Blood from an artery will pump into the collection bottle in spurts and will be a brighter red colour than that from a vein. If this happens, take the tourniquet off and remove the needle. You should apply pressure for a minimum of five minutes and apply a pressure dressing when bleeding has stopped. Ask a registered practitioner to assess the patient before attempting venepuncture again or before the patient leaves the surgery.</li>
<li>The vein 'collapses'. This usually means that the vacuum has sucked the wall of the vein on to the needle and stopped the flow of blood. This can sometimes be overcome by taking the bottle off and then reapplying or by gently tilting the needle in the vein.</li>
<li>There is no blood flow. The needle may have gone through the vein or missed it completely. Gently and very slowly withdraw or manoeuvre the needle until the blood starts to flow. If there is still no success, slowly take the needle out, checking all the time for any indication of blood flow. Repeat the procedure with a fresh needle and bottle. Do not make more than two attempts on each arm.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="an-overview-of-some-common-blood-tests">AN OVERVIEW OF SOME COMMON BLOOD TESTS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#an-overview-of-some-common-blood-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="full-blood-count-fbc">Full blood count (FBC)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#full-blood-count-fbc" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This tells us how many red blood cells, white blood cells and platelets there are. It also identifies the different types of white blood cells. The shape and size of the cells can be determined. Red blood cells carry essential oxygen attached to haemoglobin. Because of this, a reduction in the number of red blood cells or a problem with them can result in a shortage of oxygen in the body. White blood cells (made up of neutrophils, lymphocytes, monocytes, basophils and eosinophils) fight different types of infection and are essential in the immune system. Platelets are small fragments of cells that are important in the clotting process.</p>
<p>The FBC is a haematological test, and may be done for several reasons, some of which are listed here:</p>
<ul>
<li>hypertension (because too many red blood cells can make the blood thicker and increase the blood pressure);</li>
<li>heart disease;</li>
<li>COPD;</li>
<li>diabetes;</li>
<li>any prolonged or recurrent infection;</li>
<li>cancer – undergoing chemotherapy;</li>
<li>inflammatory diseases requiring disease-modifying antirheumatic drugs (DMARDs) such as methotrexate; and</li>
<li>anaemia.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="erythrocyte-sedimentation-rate-esr">Erythrocyte sedimentation rate (ESR)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#erythrocyte-sedimentation-rate-esr" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Inflammatory processes affect the red blood cells and prolong the time it takes to spin them down in a centrifuge. The erythrocyte sedimentation rate (ESR) can indicate if there is any inflammation going on in the body. It will also be influenced by age and gender.</p>
<p>The ESR should be measured within two hours of the blood being taken to achieve the most accurate result.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="kidney-or-renal-function-tests-rfts-also-referred-to-as-urea-and-electrolytes-u-es">Kidney or renal function tests (RFTs), also referred to as urea and electrolytes (U&amp;Es)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#kidney-or-renal-function-tests-rfts-also-referred-to-as-urea-and-electrolytes-u-es" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The kidneys are two small organs positioned either side of the back in the left and right loin. They are essential organs and act as filters for the blood, sieving out waste products and excreting them in the urine. When the kidneys are not functioning properly, toxic waste products can build up in the blood and interfere with the normal functioning of the body.</p>
<p>Kidney function tests are performed to measure the levels of urea, creatinine and electrolytes, including sodium, potassium, chloride and bicarbonate. Some of the reasons for checking kidney function include:</p>
<ul>
<li>kidney disease;</li>
<li>hypertension;</li>
<li>diabetes; and</li>
<li>when the patient is taking specific medications such as some antihypertensives.</li>
</ul>
<p>The kidney function test will also identify the glomerular filtration rate (GFR), which indicates the rate at which the kidney is filtering the blood, and can therefore give information about the health and efficiency of the kidney.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="liver-function-tests-lfts">Liver function tests (LFTs)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#liver-function-tests-lfts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The liver is a vital organ in the right upper part of the abdomen. It has several essential functions:</p>
<ul>
<li>storage of glycogen (made from sugars – this is the fuel for the body);</li>
<li>metabolising (breaking down) fats and proteins from food;</li>
<li>processing and metabolising many toxins and drugs;</li>
<li>production of vitamin K and other chemicals needed to help the blood clot; and</li>
<li>production of bile (stored in the gall bladder), which is necessary for digesting fats.</li>
</ul>
<p>LFTs measure the levels of liver enzymes, such as alkaline phosphatase (ALP), alanine transaminase (ALT) and aspartate aminotransferase (AST), and abnormal levels may indicate liver disease. LFTs also measure albumin (the main protein produced by the liver), total protein and bilirubin, which gives the bile its green-yellow colour and may make the patient look yellow (jaundiced) when there are high levels in the blood. Another test of the liver that may be done if requested is the gamma-glutamyl transferase (GGT). High levels may indicate alcohol abuse as well as other types of liver disease.</p>
<p>Some reasons for checking liver function tests are:</p>
<ul>
<li>to help diagnose and monitor liver disease; and</li>
<li>as a precaution after starting some drugs, such as statins, to check that they are not causing liver damage.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="calcium">Calcium<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#calcium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Calcium is an essential mineral, and about 99 per cent is found in the bones and teeth, with the rest found in the blood and soft tissues. It is vital for healthy bones and teeth, and to help with muscle contractions, blood clotting and nerve function.</p>
<p>Calcium levels may be checked in patients with:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>kidney stones;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>bone disease;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>neurological (nerve) disease;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>kidney disease;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>symptoms of high calcium (e.g. fatigue, weakness, anorexia, vomiting, constipation, thirst, urinary frequency);</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>symptoms of low calcium (e.g. abdominal cramps, muscle cramps, tingling fingers); and</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>other diseases that may be associated with abnormal calcium levels, such as thyroid disease, cancer, malnutrition and intestinal disease.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Some literature advises against the use of the tourniquet when checking calcium levels as it has been suggested that prolonged use of the tourniquet gives a falsely high reading. However, this is controversial and will depend on laboratory testing methods. Check with your local laboratory.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="blood-glucose">Blood glucose<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-glucose" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Diabetes is a disease where the body either does not produce any insulin, or it may produce some but the body is resistant to it. Insulin is a chemical that is produced by the pancreas, and it is essential to enable glucose circulating in the blood to enter the cells of the body for them to function properly. When the blood glucose cannot enter the cells, the levels in the blood will rise, and subsequent high levels over time can cause many problems, such as heart disease and strokes, eye damage, kidney damage, and damage to the nerves, causing numbness or pain in the feet and hands.</p>
<p>Diabetes can be diagnosed by measuring blood glucose levels, but it is now usually diagnosed by measuring the HbA1C.</p>
<p>A random blood glucose level of 11.1 mmol/L or more indicates diabetes. This will usually be confirmed with a fasting test.</p>
<p>A fasting blood glucose level of 7.0 mmol/L or more indicates diabetes but will usually be repeated to confirm the diagnosis.</p>
<p>When the levels are on the borderline and the diagnosis is in doubt, the patient will sometimes be referred for an oral glucose tolerance test. For this test, the patient must fast overnight and then have a blood sample taken. They are then given a glucose drink. A blood sample taken again two hours later showing a glucose level of greater than 11 mmol/L indicates that the body is unable to deal efficiently with the glucose and confirms the diagnosis of diabetes. This test is also done routinely in pregnancy when the expectant mother is larger than expected as a large baby can sometimes occur when the mother has gestational diabetes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hba1c-glycated-or-glycosylated-haemoglobin">HbA1C (glycated or glycosylated haemoglobin)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hba1c-glycated-or-glycosylated-haemoglobin" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When there is an excess of glucose in the blood system, it will attach freely to haemoglobin in the red blood cells. Red blood cells live for about 120 days, so measuring the haemoglobin that has glucose attached to it (glycated haemoglobin) indicates the levels of circulating glucose in the bloodstream over the past three to four months. It is much more useful to determine the overall diabetic control as a fasting glucose level will only indicate the immediate glucose level. The HbA1C is now also used to diagnose diabetes, and a level of 6.5 per cent or greater is a positive result.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="thyroid-function-tests">Thyroid function tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#thyroid-function-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The thyroid gland is situated in the neck, and it produces hormones that increase the body's metabolic rate and help to control the level of calcium in the blood.</p>
<p>Thyroid function tests are done to diagnose thyroid disorders and to monitor people who have an underactive thyroid gland (hypothyroidism) and who take thyroid replacement medication (thyroxine). They are also done to monitor people who have an overactive thyroid (hyperthyroidism) and who take other types of drugs to counter the effects of this.</p>
<p>Newborn babies are checked for inherited thyroid problems.</p>
<p>The first test will usually be for the thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH), which is responsible for stimulating the gland to produce the hormones T4 and T3. Levels of these hormones will be checked if the TSH levels are abnormal.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lipid-profile-test">Lipid profile test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lipid-profile-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Lipid is basically fat, and it is stored in the body as a source of energy. A lipid profile includes measurements of cholesterol, triglycerides, low-density lipoprotein (LDL) and high-density lipoprotein (HDL):</p>
<ul>
<li>Cholesterol is a fat made in the liver from fats in the food we eat. We all need some cholesterol as it forms an important component of the cell walls in our body. It is carried in the bloodstream by the lipoproteins HDL and LDL.</li>
<li>HDL is the transporter of 'good cholesterol', and higher levels are associated with reduced fatty deposits in the arteries (atheroma).</li>
<li>LDL is the transporter of 'bad cholesterol', and it is the LDL that is implicated in the formation of atheroma.</li>
<li>Triglycerides are the end product of the metabolism of the bulky fats present in food. Triglycerides are stored in adipose (fat) cells to be used as energy if food is unavailable.</li>
</ul>
<p>Hyperlipidaemia can occur in people who have an unhealthy diet but can also be inherited. It may be secondary to other conditions, such as diabetes, thyroid disease and some liver and kidney disorders. It is an important risk factor for heart disease and strokes.</p>
<p>Historically, diagnosis has been made after a fasting blood test (fasting for 12 hours with only water to drink), but this is no longer considered appropriate or necessary (NICE 2014e). Occasionally, a doctor may still order a fasting test of triglycerides if non-fasting values have been significantly elevated because they can be affected for several hours by a high-fat meal.</p>
<p>High LDL levels are associated with a higher risk of heart disease, and should be less than 3.0 mmol/L. However, the ratio of total cholesterol to HDL (TC/HDL ratio) is also an important indicator, and should be 4.5 mmol/L or less.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="international-normalised-ratio-inr">International normalised ratio (INR)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#international-normalised-ratio-inr" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is a measure of how much longer it takes the blood to clot than normal, so if the INR is measured as 2.0, the blood is taking twice as long as normal to clot. For most of us, assuming we are not taking any anticoagulant medication, our INR would be 1.0. The INR is a test used most commonly for patients who are taking warfarin.</p>
<p>Warfarin blocks the effects of vitamin K and depletes the clotting factors in the body, so it inhibits the formation of clots. This is useful in patients with certain types of medical conditions, such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>atrial fibrillation;</li>
<li>deep-vein thrombosis (DVT) or pulmonary embolism; and</li>
<li>mechanical heart valves or heart valve disease.</li>
</ul>
<p>Each patient who takes warfarin will need an individualised, specific dose that is titrated according to the INR result. For this reason, it is essential to make sure you achieve a good-quality sample so that the patient receives an appropriate therapeutic dose. An overdose of warfarin can have catastrophic effects, resulting in the patient bleeding to death, and conversely – if the warfarin dose is too low for that patient – they are at greater risk of complications, including a stroke.</p>
<p>Blood sample bottles for INR testing contain heparin, which stops the blood from clotting so it can be tested accurately.</p>
<p>This principle applies to other blood tests as well – additives in the various blood sample tubes are measured for a specific amount of blood.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="capillary-blood-testing">CAPILLARY BLOOD TESTING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#capillary-blood-testing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is useful when only a very small amount of blood is needed or when venepuncture is difficult. It is a convenient way of monitoring blood glucose, INR and cholesterol levels.</p>
<p>It is not without potential pitfalls, however, and the person performing the test must ensure that it is done correctly to provide as accurate a result as possible.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="capillary-blood-testing-the-procedure">Capillary blood testing: the procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#capillary-blood-testing-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS 131 Obtain and Test Capillary Blood Samples to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Care and Development 2011).</p>
<ol>
<li>Confirm the identity of the patient.</li>
<li>Obtain verbal informed consent.</li>
<li>Decontaminate your hands, put on your gloves and prepare the necessary equipment:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>glucose meter;</li>
<li>cotton wool or gauze;</li>
<li>appropriate test strip (check that they are in date);</li>
<li>control solution;</li>
<li>appropriate device for puncturing the skin;</li>
<li>clean tray to hold the equipment; and</li>
<li>sharps box.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Make sure the patient's hands are clean, warm and dry before starting.</li>
<li>Remove the strip from the foil and insert into the meter with the three black lines at the end of the strip in the meter. The meter will turn on. This will vary according to the device used. Check the manufacturer's instructions if you are unsure.</li>
</ol>
<p>Always make sure that the blood sample bottle is filled to the mark on the tube. If the ratio of blood in the tube is not enough for the heparin additive in the tube, the INR will be recorded as much higher than it is. If you have difficulty 'bleeding' a patient, always document this on the form so that the laboratory staff can make allowances for this.</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Check that the LOT number matches the strip being used (if necessary).</li>
<li>Always use a fresh disposable lancet for each patient.</li>
<li>Select an appropriate area for puncture.</li>
<li>Use the middle, ring or little finger. Puncture the sides of the finger, parallel to the side edges of the nail. Try not to use the tip or pad of the finger because there are more nerve endings there and it will hurt more.</li>
<li>If performing the test for blood glucose, wipe away the first drop of blood and discard. Do not do this if checking the INR.</li>
<li>Squeeze gently until a rounded bead of blood is obtained. There must be sufficient blood for the meter to function accurately. Do not squeeze the finger too hard. The sample should flow freely from the puncture site. Compress the area, then release for a few seconds and repeat. Take your time! If the flow is too slow, you will need to apply a second puncture.</li>
<li>Touch the drop of blood to the white area at the end of the strip until the meter begins the test. Do not remove the test strip or disturb it during the countdown.</li>
</ol>
<p>Finishing up:</p>
<ol start="13">
<li>Once the sample has been obtained, ask the patient to apply pressure to the site with gauze and maintain pressure until the bleeding has stopped.</li>
<li>Dispose of all contaminated items in an approved container.</li>
<li>Remove your gloves and decontaminate your hands.</li>
<li>Document the result immediately and refer to the doctor or nurse as necessary.</li>
<li>Ensure the meter is checked regularly with the local internal quality control tests and external quality control tests (contact your local laboratory for more information).</li>
</ol>
<p>Perform an internal quality control test:</p>
<ul>
<li>daily on meters in use;</li>
<li>weekly if meters not used often;</li>
<li>whenever results seem odd; and</li>
<li>to make sure the meter and strips are working properly.</li>
</ul>
<p>Do not use the quality control solution if the expiry date has passed.</p>
<p>Common sources of error:</p>
<ul>
<li>not puncturing the skin deeply enough;</li>
<li>not wiping away the first drop of blood (for testing glucose levels);</li>
<li>squeezing too hard or milking the finger excessively;</li>
<li>using test strips that are past their expiry date; and</li>
<li>using test strips that have been stored incorrectly and that are damp or damaged.</li>
</ul>
<p>Accuracy can also be affected by:</p>
<ul>
<li>poor peripheral circulation (i.e. poor blood flow to the fingers);</li>
<li>dehydration;</li>
<li>hypotension;</li>
<li>renal dialysis; and</li>
<li>very high cholesterol levels greater than 13 mmol/L.</li>
</ul>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="venepuncture-quiz">VENEPUNCTURE QUIZ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#venepuncture-quiz" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>List two factors that might interfere with the clotting process</li>
<li>Which blood bottle should be left until last in the order of draw?</li>
<li>What is haemolysis?</li>
<li>Assuming you have needed to use a tourniquet, when should you loosen it?</li>
<li>When should the tourniquet be removed completely?</li>
<li>What will you do if you puncture an artery in error?</li>
<li>How many points of reference do you need for labelling the bottles and form?</li>
<li>Why do we need red blood cells?</li>
<li>List two reasons why a patient may have a kidney function test.</li>
<li>Why is it important to fill the blood bottle to the correct level?</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-14-1-poor-practice-in-venepuncture-answers-to-activity">Box 14.1 Poor practice in venepuncture: answers to activity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-14-1-poor-practice-in-venepuncture-answers-to-activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Poor practice in venepuncture that may give inaccurate results:</p>
<ul>
<li>Using needles that are too small or too big.</li>
<li>Prolonged use of the tourniquet.</li>
<li>Delays in transit time after sampling.</li>
<li>Samples stored at the wrong temperature (i.e. in an area that is too warm or too cold). Ideally, samples should be stored in a cool box.</li>
<li>Using a thin or ‘thready’ vein.</li>
<li>Taking blood from a bruised area.</li>
<li>Not allowing antiseptic skin cleanser to dry.</li>
<li>Mixing samples incorrectly or not at all.</li>
<li>Bending needles.</li>
<li>Filling sample bottles from a syringe and needle.</li>
<li>Under-filling the sample.</li>
<li>Labelling the sample incorrectly.</li>
</ul>
<p>Patient care may be compromised as inaccurate or haemolysed results may result in inappropriate or</p>
<p>delayed treatment. Repeat testing is uncomfortable and time-consuming for patients and staff, and is also costly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Using a framework for reflection (see Chapter 2), try reflecting on a consultation where you took blood from a patient.</p>
<p>How did it go and what did you learn?</p>
<p>You know you're a phlebotomist when you recognise their median antecubital vein rather than their face!</p>
<p>15</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="kidney-function-and-urine">Kidney function and urine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#kidney-function-and-urine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Performing accurate urinalysis</p>
<p>Why write a chapter on urinalysis? Surely it just involves dipping a test strip into a pot of urine and reading the result? What could be difficult about that? Of course, there is much more to it as an important diagnostic tool that is all too often very badly performed, resulting in inadequate or inaccurate information and a potentially incorrect diagnosis. As with any other task the HCA performs, it is always easier to perform it well and accurately if there is an understanding of what is being tested for and why.</p>
<p>Urine has historically been used for many different things and it has had many magical qualities attributed to it. Some tribes in Siberia drink the urine of people who have eaten magic mushrooms in the belief that this will help them communicate with the spirits. Ancient Romans used urine to bleach clothes. It has been used as an antiseptic and as a teeth whitener, and is thought by many different cultures to cure any number of ailments, from acne, warts, hair loss, wrinkles and gastric upset, to name but a few!</p>
<p>Most importantly, though, is the fact that urine testing can tell us a great deal about a person's general health.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-do-the-kidneys-do">WHAT DO THE KIDNEYS DO?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-do-the-kidneys-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>We have two kidneys, both weighing about 160 g and measuring 10–15 cm long. They filter the toxins and waste products from the blood, and these are then excreted in the urine.</p>
<p>Each kidney contains approximately 2 million microscopic sieves or filtering systems called nephrons, which consist of networks of capillaries called glomeruli, where the first phase of filtration occurs. The Bowman's capsule surrounds each glomerulus and is connected to a long tube or 'tubule'. The kidney receives about one litre of blood every minute. This blood is filtered through the nephrons and the resulting fluid is sent through the long tubule, where most of the water, essential salts, such as potassium, sodium and other substances such as glucose, amino acids and vitamins are reabsorbed back into the bloodstream. The remaining water, urea and other waste substances make up the urine, and this is passed down the ureter to the bladder. Urine is normally made up of 5 per cent salts and ammonia and 95 per cent water.</p>
<p>Approximately two litres of urine are produced in 24 hours, but this can vary greatly, and depends on factors such as fluid intake, sweating and general health.</p>
<p>The kidneys are essential for maintaining homeostasis – the normal balance of fluids and salts or electrolytes upon which the proper functioning of the body depends.</p>
<p>The kidneys also secrete some essential hormones such as erythropoietin, which is needed to make red blood cells. For this reason, patients with kidney failure may become anaemic (lacking in oxygen) if they fail to produce enough of this hormone, and therefore lack red blood cells to carry the oxygen.</p>
<p>The nephrons are very delicate structures and easily damaged, especially when pressures are increased, such as in hypertension, or where the renal artery supplying the kidney with blood is narrowed (renal artery stenosis). When they are damaged, the sieves or filters become leaky, and larger molecules such as proteins (not normally seen in urine) may become detectable.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-can-urine-testing-tell-us">WHAT CAN URINE TESTING TELL US?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-can-urine-testing-tell-us" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="look-at-it">Look at it<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#look-at-it" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Normal urine is straw coloured and transparent. So, to begin with, have a good look at it and assess if the</p>
<p>sample looks normal.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-76adf878.jpeg" alt="img-38.jpeg">
Figure 15.1 HCA testing urine</p>
<p>Is it dark or pale? If it is very pale, it indicates a good level of hydration. If very dark, it may indicate dehydration.</p>
<p>What colour is it? The colour of the urine can also be altered by medication, foodstuff and disease. Red or pink urine may indicate blood, but may also be caused by eating beetroot! Bright yellow urine can be caused by some vitamins and other medications. Brown urine may indicate the presence of bilirubin if, for example, there is gall bladder disease, but it may also be caused by iron supplements. Turbid or cloudy urine may indicate infection. Frothy urine can be due to the presence of glucose or protein.</p>
<p>So, before you have even opened the urine bottle, you may already have some idea of the health, hydration or even diet of the patient.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="smell-it">Smell it<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#smell-it" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Once you've opened the urine bottle, notice if there is any offensive smell.</p>
<p>A faecal smell could mean that there is a fistula, or direct connection between the bowel and bladder where faeces is able to contaminate the urine. A fruity, sweet smell could be significant in a diabetic patient who may have ketoacidosis. If the urine smells of asparagus, it is probably because the patient has been eating asparagus! An unpleasant strong smell of ammonia could be due to a bacterial infection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dip-it">Dip it<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dip-it" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The presence of various substances in the urine that can be detected on the dip test can be very useful to the clinician in making a diagnosis.</p>
<p>Protein should not normally be present in any significant amount, and anything greater than a trace could be significant, so you must take care to observe the colour on the dip test carefully and record the result as accurately as possible. An early morning sample is the best when testing for protein.</p>
<p>Protein may be present in:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>hypertension;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>kidney disease;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>infection;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>inflammation and malignancy;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>diabetes;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>pregnancy – indicating possible pre-eclampsia, which is a medical emergency;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>pyrexia;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>dehydration;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>vigorous exercise; and</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>'orthostatic proteinuria', which is usually harmless, and can sometimes be seen in children later in the day.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Blood is not normally present (haematuria). Take care when reading the test strip that you allow long enough for the reaction on the strip to occur. For blood, this can take up to two minutes.</p>
<p>Blood may be present in:</p>
<ul>
<li>infection;</li>
<li>trauma or injury to the urinary tract or kidneys;</li>
<li>kidney stones; and</li>
<li>malignancy.</li>
</ul>
<p>It may also be present:</p>
<ul>
<li>if the patient is menstruating;</li>
<li>after vigorous exercise;</li>
<li>after smoking or toxic chemical exposure; and</li>
<li>with no known cause – but it should always be reported and investigated.</li>
</ul>
<p>Ketones are produced by the breakdown of fatty acids.</p>
<p>Ketones may be present in:</p>
<ul>
<li>uncontrolled diabetes – so the nurse or doctor should always be informed;</li>
<li>anorexia or starvation;</li>
<li>diarrhoea and vomiting;</li>
<li>pregnancy;</li>
<li>eclampsia; and</li>
<li>alcoholism.</li>
</ul>
<p>Nitrites can be caused by the reaction of some bacteria on urinary nitrates. The pink colour must be uniform to indicate a positive result.</p>
<p>Nitrites may be present when there is infection. However, the test is highly sensitive to air exposure, and will commonly give a false positive result, so some laboratories no longer consider it relevant when deciding if further culture and sensitivity testing is required.</p>
<p>Leucocytes are white blood cells that are usually produced in response to infection.</p>
<p>Leucocytes may be present when there is infection or inflammation in the genitourinary tract. There may be false positive results if the test is contaminated by vaginal discharge.</p>
<p>Specific gravity (SG) is often overlooked as a useful test, but can demonstrate the concentration of solutes in the urine.</p>
<p>A low SG might indicate:</p>
<ul>
<li>excessive fluid intake;</li>
<li>renal failure;</li>
<li>pyelonephritis; and</li>
<li>diabetes insipidus (this is a condition where there is a problem with the adrenal glands, resulting in an inability to control the fluid balance – it is not to be confused with diabetes mellitus).</li>
</ul>
<p>A high SG shows that the urine is very concentrated with a high level of solutes, and could occur in:</p>
<ul>
<li>dehydration;</li>
<li>renal artery stenosis;</li>
<li>heart failure; and</li>
<li>liver failure.</li>
</ul>
<p>The pH of the urine stands for the 'potential of hydrogen', and demonstrates the concentration of hydrogen ions. This determines whether the urine is alkaline or acidic. Tap water and normal urine will usually be neutral, with a pH of approximately 7.</p>
<p>A pH lower than 7 (acidic urine) may be found:</p>
<ul>
<li>in acute starvation;</li>
<li>in diabetic ketoacidosis;</li>
<li>when potassium levels are low; and</li>
<li>when the diet is very acidic.</li>
</ul>
<p>A pH higher than 7 (alkaline urine) may be found:</p>
<ul>
<li>when potassium levels are high; and</li>
<li>in a vegetarian diet.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bilirubin is a by-product of the breakdown of red blood cells, and it is normally excreted in the bile. It may be present in urine in liver disease or obstruction of the gall bladder.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="urinalysis-the-procedure-see-figure-15-1">URINALYSIS: THE PROCEDURE (SEE FIGURE 15.1)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#urinalysis-the-procedure-see-figure-15-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS7 Obtain and Test Specimens from Individuals to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Care and Development 2011).</p>
<ol>
<li>Always wear gloves.</li>
<li>Consider eye protection.</li>
<li>Check the expiry date on strips.</li>
<li>Immerse the test strip completely in the urine.</li>
<li>After dipping in urine, remove any excess urine by tapping the test strip gently on the top of the bottle.</li>
<li>Lay the test strip flat on a dry surface.</li>
<li>Always check the time and be patient. Some reactions can take up to two minutes. If you do not observe this simple rule, you may miss potentially important results.</li>
<li>Do not discard urine until you are sure it will not be needed again.</li>
<li>Follow infection control procedures and wash your hands thoroughly after removing gloves.</li>
<li>Document results accurately.</li>
<li>Inform requesting clinician as necessary.</li>
</ol>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-iv">Section IV<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-iv" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
