<p>Examining the feet of people with diabetes</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="keywords">Keywords<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#keywords" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Diabetes, Health care, Health care workers, Health care workers</p>
<p>Examining people's feet may not sound like a very pleasant job, but for people who have diabetes it is vital that their feet are checked regularly. This can be a very suitable role for the HCA or AP who has completed appropriate training. The examination may be performed by the HCA in conjunction with other tests such as BMI, BP and relevant blood tests, and the patient can then be seen in a follow-up diabetic clinic with the appropriately trained nurse.</p>
<p>As with everything else you do, it is always useful to have a clear understanding of why the problems might occur and what you are looking for when you perform the task. This chapter will explain some of the underlying reasons for problems in the feet of diabetic patients and will identify how you can check for these to determine the patient's risk of ulceration.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="an-overview-of-diabetes">AN OVERVIEW OF DIABETES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#an-overview-of-diabetes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Diabetes mellitus is the medical term used to encompass Type 1 and Type 2 diabetes. The word Diabetes is Greek in origin, and means siphon (to pass through), referring to the glucose passing into the blood and urine. Mellitus is Latin in origin, and means honeyed or sweet. In medieval times, diabetes would be diagnosed by tasting the patient's urine to see if it was sweet. Fortunately, we have moved on a little in our testing techniques!</p>
<p>Diabetes is a rapidly increasing problem and is associated with many complications, including heart disease, blindness and amputation, so the implications for the health services are huge, and urgent action is needed to try to halt the epidemic.</p>
<p>There are an estimated 4.6 million people with diabetes in the UK. This figure includes 1 million people who are yet to be diagnosed and are unaware that they have it (Diabetes UK 2017).</p>
<p>People without diabetes have a pancreas that produces a hormone called insulin. We need glucose in its simplest form to provide our cells with energy so that they can work efficiently. When we ingest glucose or more complex carbohydrates that are broken down into glucose, the insulin acts like a key to facilitate the entry of glucose through the cell wall and into the cell.</p>
<p>While we currently still refer to type 1 and type 2 diabetes, there may be as many as five different types, and more accurate diagnosis at an earlier stage could give doctors a better idea of how it will develop over time, allowing them to predict and treat complications before they develop (Ahlqvist et al. 2018). More research is needed to clearly differentiate between these types, so for now we'll just consider the two types we are familiar with.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="type-1-diabetes">TYPE 1 DIABETES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#type-1-diabetes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the less common form of diabetes, accounting for only about 10 per cent of cases. In people with this condition, the specialised cells in the pancreas are destroyed by the body's own immune system. This is called autoimmune disease, where the body produces immunity to its own cells. The pancreas is then unable to produce insulin. The onset of this type of diabetes usually occurs in younger people and symptoms tend to appear suddenly. At present, the cause of type 1 diabetes is not clear, and there is no way to prevent it. The only treatment is to take insulin by injection or via an insulin pump for life, but there is a lot of very promising research in this area, and developments such as cell transplantation and the artificial pancreas look promising for the future (Diabetes UK 2018a).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="type-2-diabetes">TYPE 2 DIABETES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#type-2-diabetes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this situation, the pancreas may not produce enough insulin so that not all the glucose can get into the cells. Alternatively, the pancreas may produce enough insulin but it appears to be resisted by the effect of excess body fat. When there is not enough insulin, the body cannot cope with the glucose ('impaired glucose tolerance'). The result of this is that the specialised cells in the pancreas (beta cells) work harder to produce more insulin and eventually become worn out so they are no longer able to produce enough insulin. Factors such as obesity, sedentary lifestyle, ageing, genetics and smoking can all predispose to type 2 diabetes. The onset usually occurs in older people, but is increasingly seen in much younger people, presumably due to the increase in obesity in that age group. Symptoms tend to appear gradually, and the patient can be treated with diet, tablets and/or insulin.</p>
<p>Three in five cases of type 2 diabetes can be prevented or delayed by maintaining a healthy weight, eating well and being active (Diabetes UK 2018b). This is quite a mind-blowing statistic!</p>
<p>Obesity is the single biggest risk factor for diabetes, accounting for 80-85 per cent of the overall risk of developing type 2 diabetes, and it is the global rise in obesity that appears to be fuelling the current epidemic. The good news is that this type of diabetes can be reversed in some patients who are able to lose weight, with the two most promising areas being low-carbohydrate diets and very low-calorie diets (Moseley 2016; Newcastle University Press Office 2017; Diabetes UK 2018c).</p>
<p>The main problem with diabetes is that it results in a greater amount of circulating glucose in the arteries and less in the body cells, where it is really needed. This can result in various symptoms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>List as many things as you can think of that may be symptoms of diabetes. You may need to look these up.</p>
<p>Check your list against the list given in Box 16.1 at the end of the chapter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-does-diabetes-cause-foot-problems">HOW DOES DIABETES CAUSE FOOT PROBLEMS?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-does-diabetes-cause-foot-problems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Diabetic foot disease is still the most common cause of hospital admission in diabetic patients (see Figure 16.1).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-bdcc08df.jpeg" alt="img-41.jpeg"></p>
<p>This is because of two main factors: peripheral neuropathy and peripheral vascular disease.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="peripheral-neuropathy">Peripheral neuropathy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#peripheral-neuropathy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>About half of patients with diabetes are likely to have some degree of peripheral neuropathy (Boulton 2005). This is nerve damage that is caused by increased glucose circulating in the blood. The glucose injures the walls of the arteries and prevents essential nutrients from reaching the nerves. Nerves carry messages of sensation and pain. If the nerves are damaged, patients may experience symptoms such as numbness, tingling, burning or stabbing pain, extreme sensitivity to touch, skin, hair or nail changes, and a lack of coordination. The patient may lose sensation in the feet and may not know if they have an injury.</p>
<p>This loss of protective sensation (LOPS) is the single most important risk factor in the development of a foot ulcer.</p>
<p>Severe neuropathy can also cause a rare deformity called Charcot foot. The bones in the foot become weak and can fracture, and if the patient continues to walk on these weakened or fractured bones, the foot will change shape and will develop the characteristic 'rocker bottom'. Sometimes there may be a deformity on the side of the foot as well (see Figure 16.2). The protrusion forms a point of increased pressure, and can ulcerate and become infected easily.</p>
<p>Suspect acute Charcot arthropathy if the foot is abnormally red, warm, swollen or deformed.</p>
<p>If Charcot foot is suspected, it should be treated as an active risk. Early diagnosis and management are important to avoid rapid progressive deterioration (Tidy 2016b).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-6b8fcd13.jpeg" alt="img-42.jpeg">
Figure 16.1 Screaming feet
Figure 16.2 Charcot foot</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="peripheral-vascular-disease">Peripheral vascular disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#peripheral-vascular-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Diabetes is strongly associated with atherosclerosis, which is the narrowing of the arteries due to fatty deposits (atheroma). Associated risk factors include hypertension, smoking and hyperlipidaemia. Blood flow is reduced, and skin with a poor blood supply is more prone to damage and does not heal well. It is estimated that 10 per cent of people with diabetes will have a diabetic foot ulcer at some point in their lives (NICE 2015).</p>
<p>Some shocking statistics</p>
<p>In 2016, diabetes-related amputations in England were at an all-time high of 20 a day, which equates to 7,370 a year (Diabetes UK 2016a). Eighty per cent of these are potentially preventable because they begin as foot ulcers, which are avoidable and can be treated if picked up early.</p>
<p>Approximately £650 million is spent each year on foot ulcers and amputations, and of people who do develop an ulcer, 50 per cent will die within five years, while of those who have an amputation, 70 per cent will die within five years (NICE 2015).</p>
<p>These figures are a shocking indictment of the care of people with diabetes in the UK, and every effort must be made to reverse this situation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="so-what-can-we-do-about-it">So, what can we do about it?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#so-what-can-we-do-about-it" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The NICE guidance (NICE 2015) recommends that adults who have diabetes should have their risk of developing a foot problem assessed on diagnosis, and then at least annually, when any foot problems arise and on admission to hospital.</p>
<p>As part of their ‘Putting Feet First' campaign, Diabetes UK has produced some useful literature for patients and health professionals (Diabetes UK 2018d). These provide background information and an effective foot care pathway. There is a clear flow chart in which risk is identified as low, moderate, high or active, and each risk level is defined and accompanied by an action plan. Diabetes UK has coined the phrase ‘Fast Track for a Foot Attack' to emphasise the seriousness of new or increasing symptoms, and to promote the concept of rapid access for treatment by a specialist multidisciplinary team to promote faster healing and fewer amputations.</p>
<p>All nurses and HCAs who are involved in the management of patients with diabetes should be able to perform a diabetic foot check and should receive training for this from a recognised organisation or qualified podiatrist.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="checking-the-feet-of-the-diabetic-patient-the-procedure">Checking the Feet of the Diabetic Patient: The Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#checking-the-feet-of-the-diabetic-patient-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard HWB63 from the NHS Knowledge and Skills Framework to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Health 2011b).</p>
<p>The aims of the diabetic foot examination are to:</p>
<ul>
<li>detect risk factors for ulceration;</li>
<li>classify the risk as low, increased or high;</li>
<li>refer appropriately; and</li>
<li>feed back to the nurse or doctor.</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Confirm the identity of the patient.</li>
<li>Obtain informed verbal consent.</li>
<li>Prior to the patient removing their footwear, have a good look at it. Does it fit well? Is it enclosed? Does it support the foot well? Is it ‘breathable'?</li>
<li>Check the history – ask the following questions:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Have you ever had a foot ulcer?</li>
<li>Have you seen any cuts or blisters that you didn't feel?</li>
<li>Do you have any discomfort or altered sensation in your feet or legs, either at rest or when walking?</li>
<li>Do you have any problems looking after your feet?</li>
</ul>
<ol start="5">
<li>The patient should be seated comfortably on an examination couch and legs should be bare from the knees down. Any socks, stockings or bandages must be removed.</li>
<li>Wash your hands.</li>
<li>Inspection:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Inspect the shape of the foot. Is there any evidence of deformity or a Charcot foot (see Figure 16.2)?</li>
<li>Inspect the skin on all areas of the lower legs and feet. Check behind the heels and over the balls of the feet for dry, cracking skin and fissures. Check between the toes and examine each toenail for length, colour, thickness, debris, odour and separation of the nail bed. Note the character and colour of the skin on the</li>
</ul>
<p>legs and the feet. Record any changes in colour and texture of the skin. Check for the presence of varicose veins or hemosiderosis (brown staining over the ankle area). This can indicate poor venous return and lead to oedema in the lower legs, with an increased risk of venous ulcers.</p>
<ul>
<li>Note any bunions or calluses, and record their location, size and colour, as these could indicate areas of mechanical stress or pre-ulcer formation.</li>
<li>Check the skin temperature by using the back of the hand on both legs, working down from the tibia (underneath the knee) to the toes. Normally, the leg is warmer at the tibia and gets cooler at the toes. Patients with neuropathy have no change in temperature or the toes may feel abnormally warm to touch, and may look red and shiny because of dilation of the capillaries in the toes.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="8">
<li>Vascular assessment:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Check the capillary filling time by pressing the pulp of the big toe until it blanches, and then release. It will normally take between 0 and 5 seconds for reperfusion to occur, when the toe will become pink again. Delayed refill can be an indicator of arterial narrowing and ischaemia, and should be documented.</li>
<li>Feel for the dorsalis pedis pulse on the top of the foot and the posterior tibial pulse behind the inside ankle bone (see Figure 16.3).</li>
</ul>
<p>Vascular Assessment: Pulse examination</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-c4e8e872.jpeg" alt="img-43.jpeg">
Dorsalis Pedis</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-cf533a2b.jpeg" alt="img-44.jpeg">
Posterior Tibial
Figure 16.3 Foot pulses</p>
<p>It is helpful to use two or three fingers to feel for the pulses. For the dorsalis pedis pulse, press lightly on top of the foot, starting just below the gap between the big toe and second toe. Work your way up the foot in line with the normal route of the artery (see Figure 16.4). For the posterior tibial pulse, flex the patient's foot and cup your three fingers around the inside ankle bone. Take your time doing this. Foot pulses can be difficult to feel!</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-93cb7501.jpeg" alt="img-45.jpeg">
Figure 16.4 Arteries in the foot</p>
<ol start="9">
<li>If you are unable to feel the pulses, use a Doppler probe (8 Mhz) and ultrasound gel to try to locate them. Apply the probe at about 45-70 degrees and find the area that provides the clearest audible pulsatile sound (see Figure 16.5).</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-66e5b0cf.jpeg" alt="img-46.jpeg">
Figure 16.5 Using the Doppler to locate the posterior tibial pulse</p>
<ol start="10">
<li>Sensory assessment:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Use a  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10\mathrm{g}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">g</span></span></span></span></span>  monofilament. Show the patient it is not sharp by applying it to the patient's hand first.</li>
<li>Ask the patient to shut their eyes and then gently apply the monofilament to the foot in at least five places as indicated (see Figure 16.6). Try to do this randomly so that the patient is not able to predict where you will touch the foot. You should press the filament on the foot until it just bends, and then release. Ask the patient to tell you every time they feel the filament. Avoid callused areas or hard skin.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-b49dde31.jpeg" alt="img-47.jpeg">
Figure 16.6 Checking for sensory perception using a monofilament</p>
<ul>
<li>Ideally, use disposable monofilaments, but if these are not available make sure the filament is cleaned with a disinfectant wipe between each patient, and do not use for more than ten patients in one session. Monofilaments should be left for at least 24 hours between sessions to recover their 'buckling strength' and should be replaced every six months.</li>
<li>Record how many times the patient feels the monofilament. If they feel three out of five touches, record this as 3/5 on the template (see Table 16.1).</li>
<li>If the patient is unable to feel the filament at one or more sites, the diagnosis of loss of protective sensation (LOPS) is made, and must be recorded appropriately on the diabetic foot clinic template.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="11">
<li>Wash hands.</li>
<li>Identify the risk of ulceration and inform the patient. Explain the follow-up or referral procedure according to the identified risk (see Table 16.2 at the end of the chapter).</li>
<li>Advise the patient on home foot care. Provide Diabetes UK leaflets on foot care (available to order on the Diabetes UK website) and give contact numbers for the patient to use in case of any changes or concerns.</li>
<li>Document all results in the diabetic clinic foot examination template. Emis and Vision will have specific templates for this, but if you need to develop one of your own, you may find the example in Table 16.1 useful as a guide).</li>
</ol>
<p>Table 16.1 Example of diabetic foot examination template for documentation</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Right</th><th>Left</th><th>Comments</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Previous foot ulcer</td><td>Yes/No</td><td>Yes/No</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Amputation</td><td>Yes/No</td><td>Yes/No</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Deformity</td><td>Yes/No</td><td>Yes/No</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Presence of callus</td><td>Yes/No</td><td>Yes/No</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Skin condition</td><td>Normal/Abnormal</td><td>Normal/Abnormal</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Skin colour</td><td>Normal/Abnormal</td><td>Normal/Abnormal</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Hair growth</td><td>Normal/Abnormal</td><td>Normal/Abnormal</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Right</td><td></td><td>Left</td></tr><tr><td>---</td><td>---</td><td>---</td><td>---</td></tr><tr><td>Nail disorders</td><td>Yes/No</td><td></td><td>Yes/No</td></tr><tr><td>Footwear</td><td>Suitable/ Unsuitable</td><td></td><td>Suitable/ Unsuitable</td></tr><tr><td>Dorsalis pedis pulse</td><td>Present/Absent</td><td></td><td>Present/Absent</td></tr><tr><td>Posterior tibial pulse</td><td>Present/Absent</td><td></td><td>Present/Absent</td></tr><tr><td>Positive monofilament test</td><td>/ 5</td><td></td><td>/ 5</td></tr><tr><td>Self-care capacity</td><td></td><td>Good Moderate Poor</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Risk category</td><td></td><td>Low Moderate High Active</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Action taken</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Advice given</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Review date</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There are other tools available that can be used to detect loss of sensation:</p>
<ul>
<li>VibraTip is a small battery-operated vibrating tool that may provide an alternative to the monofilament in the future, but NICE medical technology guidance (NICE 2014d) advises that further research is needed before it can be used more widely in the NHS.</li>
<li>Tuning fork (128Hz): NICE (2015) only advises the use of monofilaments, so tuning forks are not included in the procedure above, but there are many useful videos available on YouTube that show how to use the tuning fork to test for neuropathy if you need to know this.</li>
</ul>
<p>Any doubt? Not sure about your findings? Unable to feel or hear the pulses?</p>
<p>If you have any concerns about your patient, however small, always discuss them with the registered nurse or doctor.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="encouraging-patients-to-look-after-their-feet">ENCOURAGING PATIENTS TO LOOK AFTER THEIR FEET<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#encouraging-patients-to-look-after-their-feet" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>We know that people with diabetes who take good care of their feet and protect their feet from injury are much less likely to develop foot ulcers. Work in partnership with your patient to provide them with the appropriate information. Involve them (and their partner or carer) in any decision-making, provide contact numbers for them to ring in case of any problems, and always back up any verbal information with written information.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aftercare-advice-for-your-patient">AFTERCARE ADVICE FOR YOUR PATIENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aftercare-advice-for-your-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You can type out this advice and make it into a leaflet for your patient or use a leaflet from Diabetes UK (Diabetes UK 2016b):</p>
<ul>
<li>Look for and report any changes.</li>
<li>Never try to deal with corns, calluses or verrucae yourself.</li>
<li>Use moisturiser regularly (but not between the toes).</li>
<li>Always cut nails straight across and never down at the sides.</li>
<li>Wash feet daily and dry carefully.</li>
<li>Never walk barefoot.</li>
<li>Always wear socks with shoes (and sandals!).</li>
<li>Shoes should fit well and be wide enough to reduce the risk of rubbing.</li>
<li>Always feel inside shoes before putting them on.</li>
<li>Check bath temperature before stepping in.</li>
<li>Avoid the use of hot water bottles or electric blankets.</li>
<li>Never sit too close to a fire.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-16-1-common-symptoms-of-diabetes">Box 16.1 Common symptoms of diabetes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-16-1-common-symptoms-of-diabetes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Urinary frequency – usually worse at night</li>
<li>Increased thirst</li>
<li>Increased hunger</li>
<li>Weight loss</li>
<li>Tiredness</li>
<li>Recurrent infections</li>
<li>Candidiasis (thrush) infection</li>
<li>Blurred vision</li>
<li>Erectile dysfunction</li>
<li>Wounds that won't heal</li>
<li>Numbness, tingling or pain in the feet or hands</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Using a framework for reflection (see Chapter 2), try reflecting on a consultation where you watched or performed a diabetic foot examination.</p>
<p>How did it go? What did you learn?</p>
<p>How does a frog feel when he has a bad toe? Unhappy!</p>
<p>Table 16.2 Classifying the risk of ulceration (NICE 2015)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Risk</th><th>Action</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Low risk</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>No risk factors present except callus alone (e.g. no loss of sensation, no signs of peripheral vascular disease and no other risk factors).</td><td>Annual foot assessments.</td></tr><tr><td>Emphasise the risk of developing problems and the importance of foot care. Provide appropriate leaflets and emergency contact numbers, and encourage the patient to return if they experience any changes or problems with their feet.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Moderate risk</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>One risk factor present</td><td>Refer to podiatrist – patient should be seen within six to eight weeks.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table><thead><tr><th>Risk</th><th>Action</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>(e.g. loss of sensation or signs of peripheral vascular disease without callus or deformity).</td><td>Emphasise the risk of developing problems and the importance of foot care. Provide appropriate leaflets and emergency contact numbers, and encourage the patient to return if they experience any changes or problems with their feet.</td></tr><tr><td>Review in three to six months.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>High risk</td><td></td></tr><tr><td><em>Previous ulceration or amputation, or an renal replacement therapy, or more than one risk factor present</em></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>(e.g. loss of sensation or signs of peripheral vascular disease with callus or deformity).</td><td>Seek advice from diabetic nurse or GP.</td></tr><tr><td>Refer to podiatrist – to be seen in one to three months, depending on need.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Emphasise the risk of developing problems and the importance of foot care. Provide appropriate leaflets and emergency contact numbers, and encourage the patient to return if they experience any changes or problems with their feet.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Review with GP or diabetic nurse every one to two months if there is no immediate concern, or every one to two weeks if there is immediate concern.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Active</td><td></td></tr><tr><td><em>Presence of active ulceration, spreading infection</em> (e.g. critical ischaemia, gangrene or unexplained hot, red, swollen foot with or without pain, painful peripheral neuropathy, acute Charcot foot).</td><td>Seek immediate advice from diabetic nurse or GP.</td></tr><tr><td>Patient should be referred within one working day to the multidisciplinary foot care service according to local protocols and pathways.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Provide appropriate leaflets and emergency contact numbers.</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The skin and the healing process</p>
<p>Basic wound care</p>
<p>As with all other clinical procedures, before you can embark on basic wound care, you must attend a recognised training course, have written evidence of your competence and have a clear protocol in place with care plans that have been drawn up by the registered nurse for each patient. A care plan does not need to be a long, complicated document, but can be a simple set of instructions on the computer notes, identifying things such as the duration of treatment, the type of dressing to be used and when to refer to the nurse for advice.</p>
<p>You should have knowledge of the skin and how it heals, and you must understand the principles of the aseptic non-touch technique (ANTT), which is discussed later in this chapter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="skin-anatomy">Skin Anatomy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#skin-anatomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Skin is the largest organ of the body — about two square metres in volume, weighing about six pounds. It basically keeps the outside out and the inside in! There are three layers in skin, and each layer has a specific function.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="epidermis">Epidermis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidermis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is the outermost layer that protects the body from the elements. It prevents the entry of harmful microorganisms and can dispose of harmful pathogens. It prevents loss of fluid and contains melanin cells. These cells give the skin its colour and protect the body from ultraviolet light.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="dermis">Dermis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dermis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is the layer of skin under the epidermis containing the nerve endings, blood vessels, oil glands and sweat glands. It also contains collagen and elastin, which give skin its strength and elasticity, sadly lacking as we get older! The dermis regulates the body temperature through sweating and vasodilation (widening) and constriction (narrowing) of the capillaries near the surface. Pre-vitamin D_{3} is formed in the dermis during exposure to sunlight, and this is then rearranged to form vitamin D_{3}.This is necessary to regulate the concentration of calcium and phosphate in the bloodstream, promoting healthy bones.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="subcutaneous-fat">Subcutaneous fat<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#subcutaneous-fat" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is the loose connective tissue largely made up of fat cells called adipocytes. It stores energy as lipids, helps keep the body warm, acts as a shock absorber, holds the skin to the underlying tissues and houses the hair follicles.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-physiology-of-wound-healing">The Physiology of Wound Healing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-physiology-of-wound-healing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is worth stopping to think sometimes about everything that is going on in your body without you even realising. When you cut a finger or if you sustain a much larger wound, the body sets to work immediately to clean and heal the wound. It is quite amazing how efficient the body can be at looking after us, even when we may not be very good at it ourselves!</p>
<p>Haemostasis (meaning the stopping of blood flow) is the body's natural response to injury. The damaged</p>
<p>blood vessels temporarily constrict to stop the bleeding. Activation of the clotting mechanism then occurs, resulting in the release of chemicals called clotting factors, which are identified by the Roman numerals I-XIII. Factor I, called fibrinogen, is converted to solid strands of protein called fibrin. The platelets become sticky and clump around the injured tissue. These are then reinforced by the strands of fibrin, forming platelet plugs, sealing the injury and initiating healing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stage-1-the-inflammatory-phase">Stage 1: the inflammatory phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-1-the-inflammatory-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This lasts between zero and five days, and begins the healing response. There may be heat, redness, swelling and pain at this stage. White blood cells collect in large numbers at the site of injury, and proceed to remove dead tissue, foreign material and pathogens from the wound. Pathogens can be bacteria, viruses or fungal organisms that are present in sufficient quantity and in the right environment to be able to cause infection. In wounds, the pathogens are usually bacteria, and only rarely include fungi or viruses (Collier 2004).</p>
<p>At this stage, yellow <strong>slough</strong> may appear on the wound surface and there may be redness around the wound. This is normal at this stage, and should not be confused with infection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stage-2-proliferation-granulation-phase">Stage 2: proliferation/granulation phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-2-proliferation-granulation-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This usually goes on between days 3 and 14, and may overlap with the first phase. This is an important phase in wounds where there is skin loss. Tiny new blood vessels (capillaries) form in the dermis to supply blood with essential nutrients and oxygen, and the wound bed granulation can then begin. The healing 'granulating' tissue should appear to be bright red, 'beefy' and moist. If the wound bed is moist, new pale pink skin cells (epithelial cells) can multiply and migrate across the wound bed in a process called <strong>epithelialisation</strong>. This process is delicate and easily hindered if the wound bed is not moist or if it is exposed to any further trauma during wound cleansing or during the removal of old dressings.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stage-3-maturation-phase">Stage 3: maturation phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-3-maturation-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This usually begins at about day 7 and can last up to 12 months. There is a progressive reduction in the blood supply to the scar. Collagen fibres become bigger and reoriented to improve the strength of the new skin. The scar becomes paler and should eventually flatten and soften.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-healing">TYPES OF HEALING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-healing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="primary-healing">Primary healing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-healing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is also called <strong>first intention healing</strong>, and this occurs when the wound closes by the coming together of the wound edges, either spontaneously or with intervention such as with sutures or clips.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="secondary-healing">Secondary healing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#secondary-healing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This occurs where there has been tissue loss. The wound heals by the process of epithelialisation (see under stage 2 above) and wound contraction. There is no surgical intervention.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are many different factors that can affect the healing process.</p>
<p>List as many things as you can you think of that may delay healing.</p>
<p>How could this affect patient care?</p>
<p>Check your answers in Box 17.1 at the end of the chapter.</p>
<p>PROBLEMS IN THE HEALING PROCESS</p>
<p>An essential part of basic wound care is the ability to recognise what is normal and when there may be problems.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-signs-of-infection">Clinical signs of infection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-signs-of-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>All wounds will have microorganisms present, but this does not necessarily mean that the wound is infected. Routine wound swabbing may therefore provide erroneous and misleading results.</p>
<p>Wound infection occurs when there are sufficient pathogens present in the wound so that they cause damage to the body tissue, and ultimately provoke a host reaction (Garner 2016). Wound swabbing should only be carried out when there are clinical signs of infection.</p>
<p>There are some important signs to look out for that may indicate problems in the healing process or possible infection, and these should always be reported to the registered nurse or doctor immediately.</p>
<p>Signs indicating possible infection:</p>
<ul>
<li>Wound increasing or not reducing in size.</li>
<li>Increasing pain.</li>
<li>Heat.</li>
<li>Erythema (redness).</li>
<li>Cellulitis.</li>
<li>Oedema (swelling).</li>
<li>Wound breakdown.</li>
<li>Malodour (bad smell).</li>
<li>Increased exudate (fluid leaking from the wound).</li>
<li>Fragile granulation tissue – wound bleeds easily.</li>
<li>Systemic signs of fever and malaise, pyrexia.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="taking-a-wound-swab-the-procedure">TAKING A WOUND SWAB: THE PROCEDURE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#taking-a-wound-swab-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Swabbing appears to be a straightforward task, but if done badly it can lead to an incorrect diagnosis and inappropriate treatment, delayed healing, and all the subsequent effects that may have on the patient.</p>
<p>The decision on whether to perform a swab should be made by the registered nurse or doctor, but the task may be performed by the HCA. Always collect the swab before any topical or systemic antibiotics are started. If the patient has had an antimicrobial dressing in place (see Table 17.1 at the end of the chapter), delay taking the swab because the microbial count will be reduced and you will have a meaningless result.</p>
<p>Follow these simple steps to ensure accurate sampling:</p>
<ol>
<li>Explain the procedure to the patient and obtain informed consent.</li>
<li>Decontaminate your hands and put on gloves.</li>
<li>Irrigate the wound to remove slough, necrotic tissue or dried exudate using tap water or sterile saline. Irrigating may also reduce the risk of introducing erroneous organisms into the specimen. The wound must be moistened if it appears dry as this will improve the 'catch' of the swab. There is little to be gained from swabbing a dry wound. Alternatively, the swab tip can be moistened with sterile saline prior to performing the swab. Use a sterile cotton or rayon-tipped swab in a charcoal medium.</li>
<li>Move the swab across the wound bed in a zigzag motion at the same time as rotating the swab between the fingers, covering as large an area of the wound as possible, working away from the area that is cleanest. Include material from the wound bed and wound margin (Cooper 2010).</li>
<li>Immediately place the swab back into the container with the transport medium.</li>
<li>Remove your gloves and decontaminate your hands.</li>
<li>Label the sample correctly and complete the necessary documentation to send with it. Include any relevant comments about the patient's general condition, type of wound, etc.</li>
<li>Record in the patient notes.</li>
<li>Ensure transport of the swab to the laboratory as soon as possible (ideally within four hours).</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="choosing-the-wound-dressing">Choosing the wound dressing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#choosing-the-wound-dressing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Each patient should have a clear care plan drawn up by the registered nurse at the initial assessment of the patient and their wound. The care plan will specify the type of dressing to be used and the frequency of dressing change. Most patients should be reviewed by the registered nurse regularly throughout the course of their treatment, but the frequency will depend on the severity of the wound. Always refer to the nurse if there are any problems or changes in the patient's condition.</p>
<p>Before deciding on the wound dressing, the nurse should consider:</p>
<ul>
<li>the patient, their health status, environment and mobility;</li>
<li>the wound type, size and cause;</li>
<li>allergies or intolerances;</li>
<li>dressing efficacy (based on evidence);</li>
<li>cost;</li>
<li>acceptability to patient and prescriber; and</li>
<li>local wound care formulary.</li>
</ul>
<p>Remember, dressings should be prescribed for individual patients and are single-use only. They should not be resealed and used again.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="activity-2">ACTIVITY 2<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Considering what you now know about the skin and the healing process, can you list the qualities of the ideal wound dressing?</p>
<p>Check your answers in Box 17.2 at the end of the chapter.</p>
<p>There are many different wound dressings, and intelligent use of the appropriate dressing can speed up the healing process. The choice of dressing should always be guided by the local wound care formulary, as this will be based on current, available, unbiased and robust evidence.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="activity-3">ACTIVITY 3<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity-3" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Look up your local wound care formulary or contact your local tissue viability nurse, who will tell you where you can obtain a copy from. You must be able to justify the use of dressings that are not included in the local formulary. Table 17.1 at the end of the chapter gives a list of dressings as listed in some wound care formularies, and outlines their qualities and suitability for various wounds.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-do-you-reduce-the-risk-of-infection-when-performing-a-simple-wound-dressing">How do you reduce the risk of infection when performing a simple wound dressing?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-do-you-reduce-the-risk-of-infection-when-performing-a-simple-wound-dressing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Most healthcare-acquired infections are entirely preventable (as we saw in Chapter 9).</p>
<p>The main infection risk to the patient is the health worker – that's you! The level of risk can be minimised by using an effective aseptic technique during any invasive procedure, such as when performing a wound dressing. The aseptic non-touch technique, referred to as ANTT, is a framework for aseptic practice that was designed in 2013, and which has been developed and disseminated by the Association for Safe Aseptic Practice (ASAP 2018) to improve the standard of aseptic technique in clinical practice. ANTT is now the national standard aseptic technique in England, Wales and Australia, and is used in over 30 countries.</p>
<p>Asepsis is usually defined as the 'absence of microorganisms', but this is not truly achievable in typical</p>
<p>healthcare settings. On this basis, the ANTT principle defines asepsis as 'free from pathogenic organisms in sufficient numbers to cause infection'.</p>
<p>Asepsis is achieved by 'key part' and 'key site' protection, where key parts refer to things such as the tip of a syringe or the wound side of a dressing, and key sites refer to things such as open wounds and surgical incisions or any portal of entry on a patient.</p>
<p>ANTT is described as 'standard ANTT' (suitable for uncomplicated, short procedures with small key parts and small key sites) and 'surgical ANTT' (suitable for procedures that are technically complex, lasting more than 20 minutes and involving large open key sites and many key parts). Standard ANTT is suitable for the type of dressings most HCAs will be performing. The aseptic field is an important component of this to ensure a controlled safe working space and help maintain asepsis. The other important components of ANTT are the non-touch technique and the use of appropriate infective precautions, such as effective hand decontamination and appropriate use of personal protective equipment. Use non-sterilised gloves if you can perform the procedure without touching key parts or key sites directly, and use sterilised gloves if you are likely to touch key parts or sites.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="wound-dressing-the-procedure-see-figure-17-1">WOUND DRESSING: THE PROCEDURE (SEE FIGURE 17.1)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#wound-dressing-the-procedure-see-figure-17-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS12 Undertake Treatments and Dressing Related to the Care of Lesions and Wounds to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Health 2011b).</p>
<p>Remember:</p>
<ul>
<li>Always use a clean trolley.</li>
<li>Wash your hands before, after and at any point during the procedure if they become contaminated. Put on a disposable plastic apron.</li>
<li>Use sterile equipment, fluids and dressing materials. Discard any with broken packaging or any that have passed their expiry date.</li>
<li>Do not do wound dressings if you have any hand infections, boils, a sore throat or a runny nose.</li>
<li>Never attempt complex dressings when you have not had appropriate training and been assessed as competent. If you are in any doubt, refer to the nurse.</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Confirm the patient's identity and obtain informed consent before proceeding.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Check the care plan and patient notes from the previous appointment.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Prepare the patient so that they are comfortable and the area to be dressed is adequately exposed. If this involves taking off clothing, make sure the treatment area is private and the patient is provided with a dignity cover where necessary. Offer a chaperone.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Put on gloves to remove the dressing. Alternatively, you may ask the patient to remove the dressing themselves and offer the facility to decontaminate their hands afterwards. Another way to remove a dressing is by inserting your hand inside the bag (provided in some dressing packs) to remove the dressing, and then inverting the bag so that the dressing is contained inside. This can then be attached to the side of the trolley nearest to the patient and used as the bag to collect the dirty swabs, gloves, etc.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove gloves. Decontaminate your hands.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Note the condition of the wound. Seek advice from the registered nurse if the wound shows signs of any problems or if the patient reports any concerns.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Prepare the clean trolley. Select the appropriate dressing pack if required. Peel open and gently drop the inside pack on to the upper surface of the trolley. Open the inside pack by touching the corners only so that your hands never come into direct contact with the inside of the pack.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Once the sterile field is opened out on to the upper level of the trolley, other sterile items such as the dressing can be opened and gently dropped on to the field. Non-sterile items, such as irrigation pods, adhesive tape, etc., can be placed on the lower level of the trolley so that they are within reach when needed. If using saline in plastic pouches, you will need a sterile gallipot for the fluid, a syringe to irrigate the wound, and an alcohol swab to clean the pouch.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Decontaminate your hands. Put on gloves.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Clean the wound if necessary by gently irrigating with warm tap water (Fernandez and Griffiths 2012) or sterile saline. Do not routinely clean wounds – they should only be cleaned if they are contaminated with foreign particles, slough or necrotic tissue. Never use gauze or cotton wool on wounds as these may leave fibres in the wound and delay the healing process. Only ever use these to clean around the wound.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Apply the dressing(s) according to the manufacturer's instructions, without touching the side that will be in contact with the wound.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Apply adhesive tape if necessary (check for allergies).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dispose of dressing packs and contaminated equipment in the clinical waste bin in line with your local policy</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove gloves and decontaminate your hands.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Make sure the patient has a new appointment if they are to be seen again, and always advise them to return sooner if they are having any problems.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Document your actions and observations in the patient notes.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-86d4a2bb.jpeg" alt="img-48.jpeg">
Figure 17.1 Performing a wound dressing</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="removing-wound-closures-the-procedure">REMOVING WOUND CLOSURES: THE PROCEDURE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#removing-wound-closures-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS14 Remove Wound Closure Materials from Individuals to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Health 2011b).</p>
<p>You can purchase suture removal packs, which include a sterile field, suture cutter, forceps, gauze and bag for used dressings, swabs, etc., but these may be more expensive than using individual items. Check your local wound care formulary.</p>
<p>Sharps should be disposed of in the sharps bin in accordance with local policy.</p>
<p>Steps 1-9 as above. Then proceed as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>The nurse should assess the wound prior to suture removal.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove alternate sutures to begin with in case the wound has not healed well, so that if it starts to open, there are still some sutures left in situ.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove the suture by pulling the knot up with forceps, and then cutting underneath the knot closest to the skin and pulling the suture through.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If there are any problems during the procedure or the patient has any discomfort, always check with the registered nurse before proceeding.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dispose of the sutures in the clinical waste bag and the stitch cutter in the sharps bin.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Apply a dry dressing if required.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove gloves and decontaminate your hands.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Advise the patient to come back if they have any problems.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Document your actions and observations on the patient notes.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-17-1-factors-that-may-delay-healing">Box 17.1 Factors that may delay healing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-17-1-factors-that-may-delay-healing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Infection</li>
<li>Presence of foreign object</li>
<li>Inappropriate dressing/wrong size</li>
<li>Poor dressing technique</li>
<li>Poor nutrition</li>
<li>Smoking</li>
<li>Chronic disease such as diabetes, COPD, anaemia, depression, heart disease</li>
<li>Poor circulation</li>
<li>Poor mobility</li>
<li>Social factors and environment</li>
<li>Radiotherapy/chemotherapy</li>
<li>Immunosuppression</li>
<li>Pressure</li>
<li>Medication</li>
<li>Age</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-17-2-characteristics-of-the-ideal-wound-dressing">Box 17.2 Characteristics of the ideal wound dressing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-17-2-characteristics-of-the-ideal-wound-dressing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Comfortable and acceptable for the patient.</li>
<li>Keep pain to a minimum.</li>
<li>Maintain a moist environment where there is an open wound bed, when the wound is healing by secondary intention.</li>
<li>Remove excess exudate.</li>
<li>Allow the exchange of gases such as oxygen in from the air to promote healing and carbon dioxide out (waste product).</li>
<li>Maintain the correct temperature to facilitate healing (not too hot or cold).</li>
<li>Provide protection from further trauma.</li>
<li>Prevent the entry of microorganisms.</li>
<li>Leave no particles in the wound when it is removed.</li>
<li>Be easily removed without causing trauma/hindering the epithelialisation of the wound.</li>
<li>Be non-allergenic and non-toxic.</li>
</ul>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-e842dae6.jpeg" alt="img-49.jpeg"></p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="understanding-lung-function-and-disease">Understanding lung function and disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#understanding-lung-function-and-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="keywords">Keywords<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#keywords" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cancer, Lung cancer, Lung cancer</p>
<p>Many HCAs are now being asked to perform lung function testing to assist the nurse in the chronic disease clinic managing patients with respiratory disease. This chapter will give an overview of the anatomy and physiology of the respiratory system and common respiratory diseases to enhance your understanding and enable you to perform lung function testing more efficiently and accurately. You will need to complete a recognised course in spirometry before performing this task. Following concerns that the spirometry technique employed in many places was substandard, NHS England has implemented a scheme whereby any health professional (GP, nurse or HCA) who is involved in lung function testing must be trained to a specific standard by 2021 (Price 2016). The Association for Respiratory Technology and Physiology (ARTP) is the only organisation to offer national, professionally recognised qualifications in respiratory function testing and spirometry in the UK, and they now maintain the national register, which has been created to identify all appropriately trained and/or certified practitioners. HCAs will need to complete the foundation certificate, and this will enable them to perform the procedure competently, but not to interpret results.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="why-do-we-need-to-breathe">Why do we need to breathe?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-do-we-need-to-breathe" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Breathing is something most of us take for granted and rarely think about unless we become short of breath for some reason.</p>
<p>Effective respiration provides us with essential oxygen, which must then be transferred from our airways into the bloodstream for distribution to all the organs and cells of the body. Oxygen is essential to help convert food into energy in our cells. This is called metabolism. Effective respiration also helps us to get rid of carbon dioxide, which is a waste product of metabolism and can become very toxic to the body if it accumulates.</p>
<p>When you inhale (breathe in), the diaphragm and muscles between the ribs (intercostal muscles) contract and make the chest cavity expand. As the chest expands, the pressure in the chest will drop, and because the outside air pressure is now greater, air flows into the lungs. As you exhale (breathe out), the diaphragm and intercostal muscles relax, reducing the size of the chest cavity. This increases the pressure inside the chest so that air from inside the lungs flows to the outside. The cycle is repeated every time you breathe.</p>
<p>Oxygen-enriched air that we breathe in travels down the trachea and is then sent via the left and right bronchus to the bronchioles in the lungs. The bronchioles become smaller and constantly branch so that if you looked at the inside of a healthy lung, it would look something like the branches of a very dense tree in miniature. At the end of every tiny branch, there is a tiny air sac called an alveolus (see Figure 13.3 in Chapter 13). This is designed to maximise surface area for the exchange of gases (oxygen and carbon dioxide). From the outside, it looks like a tiny bunch of grapes wrapped in capillaries, which are tiny blood vessels. These contain deoxygenated blood — in other words, blood that has been all around the body and used up its oxygen supply. It is sent back from the heart to the lungs, via the pulmonary artery, to collect more oxygen. The artery becomes smaller and smaller until it forms the mesh of tiny capillaries with very thin walls that are only one cell thick. The mesh of capillaries wraps intimately around the alveolus. Oxygen from the air in the alveoli can then diffuse across the cell walls and into the blood to be taken back to the left side of the heart and sent off around the body again. Carbon dioxide is transferred by the same mechanism in the opposite direction, back into the lungs and then exhaled.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="useful-definitions">Useful definitions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#useful-definitions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Tidal breathing: Normal breathing where the volume of air entering and leaving the body of a healthy</li>
</ul>
<p>adult will be about 500 ml.</p>
<ul>
<li>Dead space: The inhaled air in the airways where no gaseous exchange can take place (usually about 150 ml).</li>
<li>Total lung capacity: The volume of air in the lungs following maximum inspiration (usually about 6 litres).</li>
<li>Residual volume: The air remaining in the lungs after full expiration.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="lung-disease">LUNG DISEASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lung-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="asthma">Asthma<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#asthma" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Asthma is a very common disease. There are 5.4 million people currently receiving treatment for asthma in the UK, which equates to one in every 11 people, or one in five households.</p>
<p>In 2016, 1,410 people died from asthma, and in most cases their deaths were entirely preventable (Asthma UK 2018).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="so-what-is-it">So, what is it?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#so-what-is-it" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>There is no standardised definition of the type, severity or frequency of symptoms, nor of the findings on investigation, for asthma. There are various possible symptoms, including wheeziness, breathlessness, chest tightness, cough and variable airflow obstruction. There may also be airway hyper-responsiveness and airway inflammation as components of the disease (Scottish Intercollegiate Guidelines Network 2016). It can affect people of any age and can change throughout a person's life and it is characterised by attacks (or exacerbations), with the severity and frequency of attacks varying from person to person (NICE 2017).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Please don't try this if you already have asthma – you already know what it feels like!</p>
<p>For those who have never had problems breathing, try taking in a little breath. Don't breathe out, but take in another little breath, and keep doing this until you can't breathe in any more. This is what it feels like not to be able to get any more air into your lungs.</p>
<p>It's quite frightening, isn't it?</p>
