<p>Asthma is very common, often misunderstood and often poorly managed. It can be very variable, very distressing and disabling, and can be life-threatening, but most of the time it can be treated.</p>
<p>The airways may become twitchy and constrict easily. There might be swelling or inflammation or mucus in the airways as well, which will make the airflow difficult. When the airways get smaller, the person with asthma might cough or wheeze or have difficulty breathing. Sometimes they may feel as if their chest is tight or as if there is a band around the chest. This usually happens in response to a trigger, and there are many possible triggers.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>How many asthma triggers can you think of?</p>
<p>Check your answers against those given in Box 18.1 at the end of the chapter.</p>
<p>Asthma can occur at any age, and tends to occur most often in people with other allergies or those who have a family history of asthma. The evidence suggests that if inherited, it passes from mother to daughter and father to son (Hasan Arshad et al. 2012).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="diagnosis-and-treatment">Diagnosis and treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis-and-treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Various tests can be used to diagnose asthma, but there is no gold standard test available yet (NICE 2017). Tests available include serial peak flow measurements, spirometry and assessment of reversibility with bronchodilators such as salbutamol. The problem with all these tests is that they are unreliable in terms of diagnosis. Testing for airway inflammation is increasingly being used for diagnosis in clinical practice, and this has been endorsed in the NICE guidance (NICE 2017), but will take some time to implement everywhere. It involves measuring the amount of fractional exhaled nitric oxide (FeNO) because this will be raised in patients who have inflamed airways and who are more likely to respond well to steroid treatment. A FeNO level above 40 makes asthma and the response to an inhaled steroid very likely, while a level under 20 makes it unlikely (Thomas 2018).</p>
<p>Asthma can be treated with various inhalers and tablets, and the treatment follows a stepwise approach (Scottish Intercollegiate Guidelines Network 2016), stepping up from occasional use of a bronchodilator inhaler (this will widen the airways) to regular use of inhaled steroids that help to stop the swelling and inflammation in the airways. Some patients will need very strong doses of inhaled steroids to control their symptoms, and some may also have inhalers that give a long-acting bronchodilator action. Whichever type of treatment they have, they should all be instructed by a nurse who has training in respiratory disease (or a doctor) on how to recognise their symptoms and manage them quickly and effectively. Never underestimate the potential danger in an asthma attack. All patients should seek emergency help if their symptoms are getting worse as asthma can deteriorate very quickly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chronic-obstructive-pulmonary-disease-copd">Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chronic-obstructive-pulmonary-disease-copd" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An estimated 1.2 million people have been diagnosed with COPD in the UK, making this the second most common lung disease after asthma. The prevalence increases with age, and figures published by the British Lung Foundation (2018) indicate that 2 per cent of the whole population (4.5 per cent of people over the age of 40) have been diagnosed with COPD.</p>
<p>COPD is usually associated with smoking, and because smoking levels are much higher in some disadvantaged sectors of the community, especially in people who have mental health issues, these people tend to be disproportionately affected by COPD compared with the rest of the population (ASH 2016a, 2016b). COPD can also have occupational causes such as exposure to dust or toxins. A small percentage of people with COPD may have inherited a condition called alpha 1 antitrypsin deficiency, which results in destruction of the alveoli – the tiny air sacs at the end of the bronchioles.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="so-what-is-it">So, what is it?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#so-what-is-it" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>COPD is a term used to describe various conditions, including chronic bronchitis, emphysema, chronic obstructive airways disease and chronic airflow limitation. Patients with COPD experience progressive breathlessness, chronic productive cough and limited exercise capacity. This is caused by airflow obstruction that is not reversible in the way that asthma is. There is permanent damage to the airways and treatment options are limited.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="managing-copd">Managing COPD<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#managing-copd" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Smoking cessation is the most important treatment, and it is never too late to stop, despite what the patient may think. If the disease is in the early stages and symptoms are mild, no other treatment may be needed. Even if the patient has been a heavy smoker for most of their life, stopping smoking can still slow down the progression of the disease.</p>
<p>Treatments for stable COPD:</p>
<ul>
<li>Bronchodilator inhalers (e.g. salbutamol, terbutaline, salmeterol).</li>
<li>Antimuscarinic inhalers (e.g. ipratropium, tiotropium).</li>
<li>Steroid inhalers (e.g. beclometasone, fluticasone, budesonide).</li>
<li>Combination inhalers.</li>
<li>Bronchodilator tablets.</li>
<li>Mucolytic medicines, which make the sputum less sticky and easier to cough up.</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatments to manage exacerbations:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Steroid tablets to reduce the inflammation in the airways.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Antibiotics.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Admission to hospital.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatments-for-end-stage-copd">Treatments for end-stage COPD:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatments-for-end-stage-copd" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li><strong>Domiciliary</strong> oxygen – this can only be prescribed by a hospital specialist.</li>
<li><strong>Palliative</strong> care to keep the patient as comfortable as possible, including physiotherapy and rehabilitation.</li>
<li>Exercise training.</li>
<li>Support and advice.</li>
<li>Medication for depression and anxiety may also be required.</li>
</ul>
<p>The HCA role in caring for patients with COPD is an important one, and includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>promoting healthy living;</li>
<li>monitoring bloods, blood pressure, lung function testing and pulse oximetry;</li>
<li>encouraging influenza and pneumococcal vaccination;</li>
<li>supporting patients and carers;</li>
<li>referring patients with early signs or increasing problems;</li>
<li>recognising and referring patients with associated depression and anxiety; and</li>
<li>keeping up to date with current developments and guidelines.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="lung-function-testing">LUNG FUNCTION TESTING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lung-function-testing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="peak-flow-recording">Peak flow recording<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#peak-flow-recording" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The peak flow measures the fastest rate of air blown out of the lungs in litres per minute. It can be used in the diagnosis and monitoring of asthma. Readings are dependent on the height, age and gender of the patient, and a normal range can vary by up to 100 litres per minute more or less than the predicted level. A chart with normal peak flow values is available at <a href="http://www.peakflow.com/top_nav/normal_values/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.peakflow.com/top_nav/normal_values/</a>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="recording-the-peak-flow-measurement-the-procedure">Recording the peak flow measurement: the procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-the-peak-flow-measurement-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>You can download the National Occupational Standard SFHCHS19 Undertake Routine Clinical Measurements to use as a checklist for your competencies (Skills for Health 2011b).</p>
<ol>
<li>Check the patient's identity and obtain informed consent.</li>
<li>Decontaminate your hands.</li>
<li>The patient should be standing or sitting upright so that the lungs can fully expand.</li>
<li>Insert a disposable mouthpiece with a 'one-way' valve into the peak flow meter. Use the appropriate size mouthpiece according to the age of the patient.</li>
<li>Set the pointer on the meter to zero and ask the patient to hold the meter underneath so their hands do not interfere with movement of the pointer.</li>
<li>The patient should then take a full breath in, seal their mouth around the mouthpiece, and blow hard and fast over one to two seconds.</li>
<li>Try to ensure that the patient remains upright when blowing. The natural tendency is to lean forward when blowing, but this will interfere with the lung expansion and deflation, and the reading will not be accurate.</li>
<li>Always repeat the process three times and record the best of the readings. It is often useful to demonstrate the procedure to the patient using your own mouthpiece.</li>
<li>Sometimes patients cannot perform this procedure properly, and if this is the case you must document this. If in doubt, seek advice from the nurse.</li>
<li>Document the measurement and report any low readings.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="spirometry">Spirometry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#spirometry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Asthma and COPD can be diagnosed by performing spirometry to measure how quickly and effectively the lungs can be emptied and filled. Measurements include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The amount of air the patient can blow out quickly and forcibly (i.e. the forced expiratory volume in one second, FEV1).</li>
<li>The total amount blown out in one breath (i.e. forced vital capacity, FVC).</li>
<li>FEV1 / FVC (a low value indicates narrowed airways).</li>
<li>The degree of reversibility following administration of a bronchodilator (e.g. salbutamol).</li>
</ul>
<p>Age, height and gender will affect lung volumes.</p>
<p>COPD is categorised as mild, moderate or severe depending on the result:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mild (stage 1): FEV1 is at least 80 per cent of the predicted value.</li>
<li>Moderate (stage 2): FEV1 is 50–79 per cent of the predicted value.</li>
<li>Severe (stage 3): FEV1 is 30–49 per cent of the predicted value.</li>
<li>Very severe (stage 4): FEV1 is less than 30 per cent of the predicted value.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cleaning-the-equipment">Cleaning the equipment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cleaning-the-equipment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prior to the procedure, the equipment must be calibrated according to the manufacturer's instructions. Calibration may need to be done before every spirometry session and every four hours, or after every ten patients (whichever happens first). Results should be recorded in a calibration log. If the calibration shows any discrepancies, the equipment should be cleaned and rechecked. Do not continue with testing until the equipment has been successfully calibrated.</p>
<p>Cleaning should also be carried out in accordance with the manufacturer's instructions and local infection control policies. You should keep a cleaning log that must be signed and dated. Always use a one-way mouth piece or bacterial filters.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="performing-spirometry-the-procedure-see-figure-18-1">Performing spirometry: the procedure (see Figure 18.1)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#performing-spirometry-the-procedure-see-figure-18-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The patient should have been advised not to smoke for at least an hour before (preferably 24 hours if possible), not to have had a heavy meal for at least four hours, not to have had an alcoholic drink for at least four hours, not to have had any vigorous activity for 30 minutes, and not to wear restrictive clothing. Any deviations from these requirements should be documented.</p>
<p>If the patient is attending for routine monitoring, they can have their inhalers as usual. If they are attending for baseline reversibility testing, they should have no short-acting bronchodilator for four hours, no long-acting bronchodilator for eight hours, and no sustained-release bronchodilators for 36 hours (Primary Care Commissioning 2013).</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Check the patient's identity and obtain informed consent.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Decontaminate your hands before and after the procedure.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Check that there are no contraindications (see Box 18.2 at the end of the chapter).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Make sure they have an empty bladder!</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Record age, gender and height.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The patient should be seated upright in a chair with arms. Both their feet should be flat on the floor.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Advise the patient to loosen any tight clothing and remove any false teeth if they are loose-fitting.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Perform the slow vital capacity before the forced vital capacity. This is done first because the airways are likely to collapse prematurely when the patient with COPD tries to blow out hard. Place a nose clip on the patient or ask them to hold their nose. Ask the patient to breathe in as deeply as they can, place their lips around the mouthpiece to form a seal, and then blow out slowly and steadily for as long as they can. This is almost like sighing. Wait at least 30 seconds before repeating, and then repeat twice if possible, to ensure the results are consistent, accurate and reproducible.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Now perform the forced vital capacity. Remove the nose clip. Ask the patient to take a deep breath in, place their lips around the mouthpiece to form a seal, and then blow out forcibly and rapidly for as long as they can until they have no breath left to blow out. This should take between 6 and 15 seconds. Take a minimum of three readings if possible. Wait at least 30 seconds between each attempt. The best two readings should have the FVC within <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>100</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">100\mathrm{ml}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord">100</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ml</span></span></span></span></span></span> of each other.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The patient may need encouragement to keep blowing until they cannot blow any more, and it is helpful to place a hand lightly on their shoulder to stop them from leaning forward. This is a normal response to get all the breath out, but by doing this they will inadvertently compress the lungs a little and render the result inaccurate.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Advise the patient not to cough or take another breath during the procedure as this will invalidate the result. You can usually tell when this has happened as there will be a characteristic step in the flow chart. Watch out for anything else that may interfere with the results, such as poor effort, leakage around the mouth piece, a slow start or an abrupt stop. If any of these things happen, the results should be discarded and the test done again.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Stop the procedure if the patient feels unwell at all during the procedure and seek advice from the nurse or doctor. A maximum of eight blows is recommended. If the acceptability criteria are not achieved after this, the test should be stopped and rebooked for a later date if appropriate.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Record:</p>
</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>FEV1 and the percentage of the predicted FEV1;
FVC and the percentage of predicted FVC;</li>
<li>peak flow (PEF); and</li>
<li>FEV1 / FVC.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-e3d164e9.jpeg" alt="img-51.jpeg">
Figure 18.1 Performing spirometry</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="reversibility-testing">Reversibility testing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reversibility-testing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This only needs to be done if there is doubt over the diagnosis and to identify asthma.</p>
<ol>
<li>The patient should be prescribed a bronchodilator inhaler (usually salbutamol).</li>
<li>Measure the FEV1.</li>
<li>The patient should then take four puffs of the inhaler via a large volume spacer.</li>
<li>After 15 minutes, remeasure the FEV1. If there is an increase of more than 12 per cent from the baseline reading, this indicates reversible airflow obstruction and supports the diagnosis of asthma.</li>
</ol>
<p>You should not administer salbutamol unless it has been prescribed for the individual patient or there is a patient-specific direction (PSD) for salbutamol in place for that patient (for more information about PSDs, see Chapter 19).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-18-1-triggers-for-asthma">Box 18.1 Triggers for asthma<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-18-1-triggers-for-asthma" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Cigarette smoke (passive or active smoking)</li>
<li>Cold air</li>
<li>Pollen</li>
<li>Dust</li>
<li>Dust mite faeces</li>
<li>Animal hair</li>
<li>Chemicals such as solder fumes, glues, aerosols</li>
<li>Respiratory infection</li>
<li>Stress/emotion</li>
<li>Exercise</li>
<li>Laughter</li>
<li>Some medications, such as beta blockers and non-steroidal anti-inflammatories</li>
<li>Mould</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-18-2-contraindications-to-spirometry-procedure-should-not-be-performed">Box 18.2 Contraindications to spirometry (procedure should not be performed)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-18-2-contraindications-to-spirometry-procedure-should-not-be-performed" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Previous problems when performing spirometry.</li>
<li>Current active chest infection.</li>
<li>Current acute illness or any communication issues that could interfere with the ability to perform the test properly.</li>
<li>Patient has been coughing up blood.</li>
<li>Suspected or active TB.</li>
<li>Unstable heart disease/angina.</li>
<li>Recent pneumothorax (three months).</li>
<li>Recent eye surgery.</li>
<li>Recent abdominal or chest surgery (three months).</li>
<li>Diagnosed aneurysm (thoracic, abdominal or cerebral).</li>
<li>Refer to the registered nurse if the patient has ear problems, has a history of fainting, has a diagnosis of glaucoma or is pregnant.</li>
</ul>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-012191f1.jpeg" alt="img-52.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lung-function-quiz">LUNG FUNCTION QUIZ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lung-function-quiz" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Why do we need oxygen?</li>
<li>What is the purpose of the alveolus?</li>
<li>What is meant by total lung capacity?</li>
<li>List three possible symptoms of asthma.</li>
<li>How many readings should you take when performing a peak flow rate?</li>
<li>What is the most common cause of COPD?</li>
<li>What does FEV1 stand for?</li>
<li>What does a low value of FEV1 / FVC indicate?</li>
<li>Why should you perform a slow vital capacity before the forced vital capacity?</li>
<li>What should be in place before you administer salbutamol to the patient for reversibility testing?</li>
</ol>
<p>TIME TO REFLECT</p>
<p>^{}[]</p>
<p>Using a framework for reflection (see Chapter 2), try reflecting on a consultation where you performed lung function testing.</p>
<p>How did it go and what did you learn?</p>
<p>Administering injections such as influenza and pneumococcal pneumonia vaccines is something that HCAs have been doing successfully now for several years. However, it wasn't that long ago that I attended a nursing conference when the first discussions about this task being delegated to HCAs were occurring, and there was uproar! Many nurses thought that in the absence of regulation and recognised HCA training, it was a step too far and was putting patient safety on the line. Now, of course, HCAs are routinely and safely administering flu, pneumococcal and shingles vaccines, and some HCAs are giving vitamin B_{12}, vitamin D, heparin and insulin injections, as well as human papilloma virus vaccines. What you do will depend very much on the area you work in and your local policy, but should also be dependent on you having the appropriate training and documentation (e.g. patient-specific directions) in place (to be discussed later). You must also be up to date with resuscitation training and anaphylaxis management.</p>
<p>This chapter will discuss the HCA role in giving those vaccinations that are covered by the National Minimum Standards and Core Curriculum for Immunisation Training of Healthcare Support Workers (PHE 2015b). There will be an overview of the diseases and the vaccines, but you will need to refer to The Green Book (Gov.uk 2013) for more detailed and current information.</p>
<p>Vitamin B_{12} (in the form of hydroxocobalamin) is an injection (but not an immunisation) that is routinely administered by HCAs in primary care, so there is information about this at the end of the chapter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="why-do-we-need-the-minimum-standards">Why do we need the minimum standards?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-do-we-need-the-minimum-standards" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Immunisation programmes are one of the most successful public health measures to prevent disease, but it is essential to maintain public confidence in the programme to ensure its continued success. When confidence is dented, the uptake of vaccines goes down, with resulting peaks in disease levels. A good example of this is the measles epidemic that occurred following scare stories (based on discredited research) about the alleged adverse effects of the MMR vaccine. Vaccination levels went down and the number of measles and mumps cases rose dramatically, resulting in permanent injury and deaths (Pepys 2007; McIntyre and Leask 2008).</p>
<p>We must make sure that when people are given an injection, they can have confidence in the vaccination and in the person administering it.</p>
<p>The National Minimum Standards (PHE 2015b) were developed to support and facilitate high-quality, safe delivery of the influenza, pneumococcal and shingles vaccination programme. The standards are outlined in Box 19.1.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="box-19-1-standards-for-immunisation-training-of-hcsws">Box 19.1 Standards for immunisation training of HCSWs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-19-1-standards-for-immunisation-training-of-hcsws" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>HCSW must have completed relevant training and been assessed as competent.The practitioner who delegates the role of immunisation must be on a relevant professional register and must ensure HCSW has met the necessary standards of competency.Any HCSW who immunises should receive specific training and annual updates.The training content should include specific core areas of knowledge.The recommended duration of training is a minimum of two days with annual half-day updates.HCSWs with a role in immunisation should have access to all updates of national influenza, shingles and pneumococcal vaccination policy, as well as The Green Book: Immunisation against Infectious Disease (Gov.uk 2013).</p>
<ul>
<li>HCSWs actively involved in immunisation services must have an identified supervisor who should be a registered, appropriately trained, experienced and knowledgeable practitioner.</li>
<li>Staff training at all levels should be included in a regular audit of the immunisation service.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-can-you-keep-updated">How can you keep updated?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-can-you-keep-updated" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Once you agree to get involved in giving injections, you must make sure you keep updated as guidelines and vaccines are constantly changing. There are several ways to do this:</p>
<ol>
<li>Attend the annual flu updates that should be provided locally. Sometimes these may be in the form of online learning.</li>
<li>Look up The Green Book online (Gov.uk 2013) and keep it handy. It is an indispensable source of reference for all types of immunisation and includes chapters on every aspect. The individual chapters are regularly updated to reflect all current policy regarding specific vaccines and injection techniques.</li>
<li>Subscribe to vaccine updates. Type ‘gov.uk’ into your search engine and look for vaccine updates. If you subscribe to these, you will get a monthly newsletter with up-to-the-minute information on any changes in policy, programmes, etc. Not all the information will be relevant to you (as with The Green Book), but it is easy to sift out what you need.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-a-vaccination-and-how-does-it-work">WHAT IS A VACCINATION AND HOW DOES IT WORK?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-a-vaccination-and-how-does-it-work" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Immunity can be inborn or acquired:</p>
<ul>
<li>Inborn immunity is basically the immunity you are born with. It includes physical barriers, such as skin and mucous membranes, and chemical barriers, such as the acid in your stomach. Inborn immunity is useful for disposing of some pathogens before they can cause us any harm, but it is not as effective as acquired immunity.</li>
<li>Acquired immunity can be passive or active.</li>
</ul>
<p>Passive immunity occurs – as the name suggests – ‘passively’, and involves the transfer of antibodies across the placenta or via the breast milk. It can also be provided by an injection of immunoglobulin. This type of immunity is useful, but the antibodies transferred in this way will only last for a few days or weeks, and so only provide short-term protection. They do not stimulate the body to produce its own antibodies, so there is no ‘immunological memory’. Consequently, if the body is exposed to the disease again in the future, it will have no protection against it.</p>
<p>Active immunity is long-lasting and occurs when the body is exposed to the actual disease or to a vaccine that mimics the disease. The invading antigen (e.g. bacteria or virus) stimulates the body's immune system into producing chemicals called antibodies that neutralise the antigen. This is the best type of immunity because the antibodies will remain in the system as memory cells, and if the body is exposed to the same disease again in the future, the antibodies can be reactivated to provide protection.</p>
<p>Vaccines contain weakened (also referred to as attenuated) or inactivated parts of the disease-causing organism. They trick the body into thinking it has been exposed to the actual disease without producing the unpleasant effects. The immune system produces antibodies in response, and the immunological memory will then ‘recognise’ the antigen that causes the disease, so if the body is exposed to it again, it will produce the appropriate antibodies as and when required.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="different-types-of-vaccine">Different types of vaccine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#different-types-of-vaccine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Live attenuated vaccines: These vaccines are made of weakened or altered disease-causing organisms. They cannot cause the actual illness, but because the organism has not been killed, they can still cause unpleasant effects in somebody with an impaired immunity. Examples of these types of vaccines include the influenza nasal spray and shingles vaccines.</li>
<li>Inactivated/killed organisms or viruses: With these vaccines, the organism has been completely disabled, so they will never cause the disease they are designed to protect against. An example of an inactivated vaccine is the seasonal injectable influenza vaccination for adults.</li>
<li>Segments of the pathogen: These will not cause the disease they are designed to prevent, but will still be able</li>
</ul>
<p>to trick the immune system into thinking it has been invaded by the whole pathogen. An example of this type of vaccine is the pneumococcal pneumonia vaccine.</p>
<p>Vaccination will protect the individual who receives it, and that person is then less likely to pass infection to others. This reduces the infection risk for unvaccinated people, so it protects them as well. This is called herd immunity or population immunity. If vaccination coverage is high enough, this may eventually lead to the eradication of a specific disease (as has happened with smallpox).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="storage-of-vaccines-the-cold-chain">Storage of vaccines: the cold chain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#storage-of-vaccines-the-cold-chain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>All vaccines should be kept at 2–8°C from the moment they are manufactured to the moment they are administered. This is an essential feature of a vaccination programme because vaccines will lose efficacy if they are exposed to inappropriate temperatures for too long, so this can be very wasteful and expensive for the NHS, and the vaccine may no longer provide the required immune response.</p>
<p>Public Health England (PHE 2017) has issued a list of recommendations to ensure maintenance of the cold chain (see Box 19.2).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-19-2-recommendations-for-correct-storage-of-vaccines">Box 19.2 Recommendations for correct storage of vaccines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-19-2-recommendations-for-correct-storage-of-vaccines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Vaccines must be refrigerated immediately on receipt, maintaining the cold chain at all stages. Check delivered vaccines carefully to ensure there are no leakages or damage.</li>
<li>Store vaccines in a lockable validated vaccine fridge at 2–8°C (a mid range of 5°C is good practice), with space for the air to circulate. The fridge must only be used for vaccines and medicines.</li>
<li>Rotate all stock so that those with the shortest expiry date are used first. Avoid over-ordering.</li>
<li>Record type, brand, quantity, batch numbers, expiry dates, and date and time received.</li>
<li>Store vaccines in original packaging at 2–8°C, protected from light. Never freeze vaccines.</li>
<li>Use switchless sockets to avoid accidental disconnection.</li>
<li>Read and record fridge temperature at the same time every working day and sign the record sheet (minimum and maximum temperatures). Reset thermometer after each reading. React if temperature falls outside 2–8°C.</li>
<li>Use a validated medical-grade cool box for storing vaccines during transfer of stock.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="legal-issues-for-hcas-administering-injections">LEGAL ISSUES FOR HCAS ADMINISTERING INJECTIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#legal-issues-for-hcas-administering-injections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>HCAs should only administer injectable medication to people over the age of 16, and can also administer intranasal vaccines to children aged between 2 and 18 (for more on consent and record-keeping, see Chapter 7).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-patient-specific-direction">The patient-specific direction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-patient-specific-direction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the tricky bit! Every year, HCAs are signed up for immunisation courses in the mistaken belief that they will then be able to administer the injections in the same way as registered nurses. It comes as a bit of a blow to some students (and managers) to realise that this isn't the case! While registered nurses can work under legal frameworks called patient group directions (PGDs), there is a more stringent legal framework called the patient-specific direction (PSD) that must be in place before unregistered health workers can administer an injection. When I deliver updates every year, I am constantly amazed at how many HCAs have (unknowingly) acted illegally, because they have been giving injections without a PSD in place. It is essential that you understand what the PSD is to ensure patient safety and to make sure that you are acting within the law and are covered by your insurance provider.</p>
<p>So, what is the difference between the PSD and the PGD?</p>
<p>The patient-specific direction (PSD) is a written (manual or electronic) instruction, signed by a doctor, dentist or non-medical prescriber, for medicine to be supplied and/or administered to a named patient after the prescriber has assessed the patient on an individual basis.</p>
<p>The PSD can also be an instruction to administer a medicine to a list of named patients where each patient on the list has been individually assessed by the prescriber. The prescriber must have knowledge of the patient's health and be satisfied that the medicine serves the individual needs of each patient on that list (CQC 2017b). It is good practice for the PSD to include the surgery (or workplace) details, the prescriber's name and qualification, the HCA's name, and the name, dose, route and frequency of the injection. The PSD should also specify a start and finish date.</p>
<p>The prescriber has a duty of care and is legally and professionally accountable for delegating the task to the HCA, so they must be completely sure that the HCA has had the appropriate training as outlined in the National Minimum Standards (PHE 2015b) and is competent to administer injections.</p>
<p>Just to be clear, a PSD:</p>
<ul>
<li>is not the same as an FP10 (traditional prescription);</li>
<li>is not the same as a simple signature on the bottom of a list of names;</li>
<li>is not a verbal instruction;</li>
<li>is not the same as a PGD template that has been renamed a 'PSD'; and</li>
<li>is not the same as a generic instruction to be applied to any patient who fits the eligibility criteria and who happens to attend on a specific day.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="patient-group-directions">Patient group directions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#patient-group-directions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are suitable for registered and specified staff, and comprise written instructions for the supply or administration of medicines to groups of patients who may not have been individually identified before presenting for treatment. It is the responsibility of the person administering the medicine to assess the patient beforehand, and the PGD is therefore not suitable for HCAs working at levels 3 or 4.</p>
<p>Having read this, you might be feeling as if it is hardly worth taking on this task when the patient must be assessed before you can give the injection anyway. But the good news is that it is possible, and many thousands of HCAs are now giving injections safely and legally. It just takes a little bit of forward thinking and preparation.</p>
<p>An example of a PSD template is provided in Box 19.3, but there are others available online. Just be careful that the one you use adheres to the legal requirements!</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-19-3-example-template-for-a-patient-specific-direction-attach-this-to-the-clinic-list-of-patients-or-scan-on-to-individual-patient-s-notes">Box 19.3 Example template for a patient-specific direction (attach this to the clinic list of patients or scan on to individual patient's notes)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-19-3-example-template-for-a-patient-specific-direction-attach-this-to-the-clinic-list-of-patients-or-scan-on-to-individual-patient-s-notes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Administration of inactivated influenza vaccination by a HCA/AP</p>
<p>Name of practice:</p>
<p>Address:</p>
<p>Name of vaccine (please print):</p>
<p>Dose:</p>
<p>Route:</p>
<p>This PSD is valid from: to [insert dates as applicable]</p>
<p>Name of prescribing practitioner:</p>
<p>Status:</p>
<p>Signature: Date:</p>
<p>Name of supervising practitioner:</p>
<p>Status:</p>
<p>Signature: ... Date: ...
Name of HCA 1 (print): ...
Signature: ... Date: ...
Name of HCA 2 (print): ...
Signature: ... Date: ...</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-diseases-and-the-vaccines">THE DISEASES AND THE VACCINES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-diseases-and-the-vaccines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="influenza">Influenza<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#influenza" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Influenza is an acute viral infection of the respiratory tract. There are three types of influenza virus, known as A, B and C, with A and B responsible for most clinical illness. Virus A causes outbreaks and is the usual cause of epidemics. Virus B causes less severe disease and smaller outbreaks. Illness due to virus C is not as common and tends to be milder.</p>
<p>These viruses are subject to slight changes every year, and this is known as antigenic drift. Periodically, a major change in the virus occurs, and this is called antigenic shift. This usually results in a new subtype of virus that is likely to cause epidemics or even pandemics.</p>
<p>Symptoms of flu can be mild or severe, but it is not the same as a bad cold, and it usually has a longer recovery period.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Flu is a more severe illness than a cold and tends to come on more quickly.</p>
<p>How many symptoms of flu can you think of?</p>
<p>Check your answers in Box 19.7 at the end of the chapter, as well as in Figure 19.1.</p>
<p>It is highly infectious and spreads rapidly, especially in closed communities. The virus is spread in respiratory droplets when a person coughs or sneezes, or via contaminated surfaces such as door handles. Some research has also shown that flu can be passed between people by simply breathing (University of Maryland 2018)!</p>
<p>The flu virus can live for many hours on hard surfaces, so the advice from NHS Health Education England (2018c) is to catch coughs and sneezes in a paper tissue and then wash hands to kill the germs – ‘Catch It, Bin It, Kill It!’</p>
<p>An outbreak of flu can have a big impact, especially on vulnerable members of society, such as the very young or very old, pregnant women, morbidly obese, people with immunodeficiency or no spleen, and those who have a chronic disease. Each year, thousands of people die from complications after catching the flu. In the first seven weeks of 2018, over 10,000 more people died in England and Wales than is usual for the time of year, and it may be that flu was the driving force behind these extra deaths, although it is not easy to prove a definite link (Pebody et al. 2018).</p>
<p>Complications include things such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>bronchitis;</li>
<li>secondary bacterial pneumonia;</li>
<li>otitis media (middle ear infection);</li>
<li>meningitis; and</li>
<li>encephalitis.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-2b6f2bd1.jpeg" alt="img-53.jpeg">
Figure 19.1 Symptoms of flu</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-flu-vaccine">The flu vaccine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-flu-vaccine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Flu viruses are constantly changing, so the vaccine composition is reviewed annually and updated. In February every year, the World Health Organization (WHO) assesses the flu strains that are most likely to be circulating during the following winter in the northern hemisphere. Depending on which ones are most likely to spread and cause illness, the WHO then recommends which three or four flu strains (usually two A viruses and one or two B viruses) should be used in the vaccines for the next season. It is not easy to predict exactly which viruses are going to cause problems, and sometimes it goes wrong, such as happened in the 2014/2015 season when the A (H3N2) virus produced a subtype after the vaccine strain had been selected, so the vaccine was not as effective.</p>
<p>The flu vaccines used in the past have not been very effective at all in the older age groups (over 75), and in 2018 a flu vaccine was developed that is more effective against the H3N2 strain in this age group. The Joint Committee on Vaccination and Immunisation advised the use of the adjuvanted trivalent (containing three virus types, i.e. two A and one B) influenza vaccine (aTIV) in the over-65 age group as the only licensed cost-effective option for this group (Gov.uk 2013, influenza chapter updated August 2018). An adjuvant is a substance added to the vaccine that improves efficacy.</p>
<p>The quadrivalent vaccine (with four virus types, i.e. two A and two B) will be given to those aged 18-65 years in clinical risk groups and healthy children age 2 years or older in the eligible age groups, in line with the national programme.</p>
<p>A universal flu vaccine is also being developed, which may mean that people will only need one or two flu vaccines in their life, but this is not available yet (University of Oxford 2017).</p>
<p>If this is used in the future, it will put an end to flu clinics as we now know them!</p>
<p>There are two types of flu vaccine:</p>
<ul>
<li>the injectable flu vaccine for adults (and children under 2); and</li>
<li>the intranasal flu vaccine for children from the age of 2 up to the age of 18 (live attenuated influenza vaccine, or LAIV).</li>
</ul>
<p>Most of the flu viruses to be used for the vaccine are grown on hens' eggs and then deactivated for the injectable vaccine or weakened in the intranasal LAIV, and then purified before being made into the vaccine. Contrary to popular belief, they cannot cause flu in the vaccinated person!</p>
<p>Because most vaccines use viruses that are grown on hens' eggs, patients who have an egg allergy should be offered vaccines that are egg-free or that have a low ovalbumin (egg protein) content.</p>
<p>The vaccine is always aimed at the most vulnerable members in the population, as outlined previously. However, eligibility criteria will vary from year to year, so you will need to check the annual flu plan for England (or equivalent for Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland), which is usually available from May onwards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="injectable-inactivated-flu-vaccine">Injectable inactivated flu vaccine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#injectable-inactivated-flu-vaccine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This can be either trivalent or quadrivalent. It is usually supplied as a single dose of 0.5 ml suspension in a prefilled syringe with an integral needle. It should be administered intramuscularly into the deltoid muscle in the upper arm or the vastus lateralis muscle in the anterolateral aspect of the thigh.</p>
<p>Individuals who have a bleeding disorder should usually have their injections via the subcutaneous route when possible, although at least one of the adjuvanted trivalent vaccines can only be given via the IM route, so always check the information leaflet (summary of product characteristics, or SPC) first.</p>
<p>The adjuvanted trivalent influenza vaccine (aTIV) will be given to adults over 65, and the injectable quadrivalent vaccine will be given to those aged 18--65 years who are in a clinical risk group.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="contraindications">Contraindications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contraindications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Do not give to anyone who has had a confirmed anaphylactic reaction to a previous dose or to any component of the vaccine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="precautions">Precautions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#precautions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Postpone if the patient is acutely unwell with pyrexia.</p>
<p>Adverse reactions are common, but usually disappear within one to two days. They include things such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>pain, swelling or redness at the injection site;</li>
<li>low-grade flu-like symptoms; and</li>
<li>a small painless nodule at the injection site.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="live-attenuated-influenza-vaccine-laiv">Live attenuated influenza vaccine (LAIV)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#live-attenuated-influenza-vaccine-laiv" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is a quadrivalent vaccine and is supplied as a suspension in a special applicator that is designed for intranasal use only. In the UK, it is only to be used for children aged from 2 to 18. The dose is 0.2 ml and the applicator is designed to allow a dose of 0.1 ml per nostril. Current guidelines advise two doses for children aged 2 to 9 who have never had the vaccine before and who are in clinical risk groups. The two doses should be given at least four weeks apart (Gov.uk 2013, influenza chapter updated August 2018). If the LAIV is not available for the second dose, then the injectable quadrivalent vaccine may be used, but must then be given by the registered nurse.</p>
<p>The vaccine contains gelatine from pigs, which may make it unacceptable for some religious groups and vegetarians.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="contraindications-more-details-are-available-in-the-green-book">Contraindications (more details are available in The Green Book)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contraindications-more-details-are-available-in-the-green-book" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The LAIV is a live vaccine, which means that the virus used in the vaccine has been weakened but not killed. This will not be an issue for most children who have a healthy immune system, but may be for those who have any form of impaired immunity. For this reason, the list of contraindications is longer than for the inactivated injection. It is important to remember this!</p>
<p>Do not give to anyone who:</p>
<ul>
<li>has had a confirmed anaphylactic reaction to a previous dose or component of the vaccine;</li>
<li>has clinical severe immunodeficiency;</li>
<li>has severe asthma or is actively wheezing, or has been prescribed oral steroids in the last 14 days for respiratory disease;</li>
<li>is on salicylates; and</li>
<li>is known to be pregnant.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="precautions">Precautions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#precautions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Postpone the vaccine if the child is heavily congested or if they are unwell with a fever.</p>
<p>When you are unsure about anything, always check with the nurse or doctor before giving the vaccine. You might think that if the patient is covered by a PSD, then they have been assessed as fit to receive the vaccine by the prescriber (and hopefully they will have been), but remember you are accountable for giving this vaccine, and even if they are covered by the PSD, you must not give the vaccine if they have a contraindication or you suspect that they might have.</p>
<p>Adverse reactions are common, but usually settle quickly.</p>
<p>Common:</p>
<ul>
<li>nasal congestion or runny nose;</li>
<li>reduced appetite;</li>
<li>weakness; and</li>
<li>headache.</li>
</ul>
<p>Less common:</p>
<ul>
<li>pyrexia/malaise;</li>
<li>rash; and</li>
<li>nosebleed.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="pneumococcal-disease">Pneumococcal disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pneumococcal-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Pneumococcal disease is a term used to describe infections caused by the bacterium Streptococcus pneumoniae (also called pneumococcus). Over 90 different types have been identified. The bacteria can spread into the sinuses to cause sinusitis or into the middle ear cavity to cause otitis media (middle ear infection). It may also affect the lungs to cause pneumonia, and can cause systemic (invasive) infections such as bacteraemic pneumonia, bacteraemia and meningitis.</p>
<p>It is spread by respiratory droplets, but unlike with flu transmission usually requires either frequent or close contact. It can occur at any time of the year, but peaks in the winter months.</p>
<p>It is a major cause of morbidity and mortality, particularly affecting the very young, the elderly and those with impaired immunity. Recurrent infections can occur in association with skull defects, cerebrospinal fluid leaks, cochlear implants and skull fractures (Gov.uk 2013).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="pneumococcal-vaccine-for-adults">Pneumococcal vaccine for adults<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pneumococcal-vaccine-for-adults" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine (PPV23) is the vaccine that is most commonly given to adults, and it contains 23 of the different types of bacteria that account for approximately 96 per cent of serious infection in the UK. It is supplied as a single dose of 0.5 ml and administered intramuscularly into the deltoid muscle of the upper arm or vastus lateralis muscle in the anterolateral thigh. It can also be given subcutaneously if the patient has a bleeding disorder.</p>
<p>For most people, one dose will be enough, but in some groups, such as those with splenic dysfunction or severe kidney disease, the antibody levels decline more rapidly. For this reason, these groups will be offered the vaccine every five years.</p>
<p>The vaccine is aimed at vulnerable groups, such as the elderly and the very young, those with chronic disease, and those with a problem that may make them more prone to problems with the bacteria (e.g. those people with a skull fracture and those who have had cochlear implants). Current eligibility can be checked in The Green Book in the chapter on pneumonia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="contraindications">Contraindications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contraindications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Confirmed anaphylaxis to a previous dose or any component of the vaccine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="precautions">Precautions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#precautions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Postpone if the patient is acutely unwell with pyrexia.</p>
<p>Pregnant and breastfeeding women who need this vaccination should be referred to the registered nurse.</p>
<p>Adverse reactions are not common, but may include things such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>mild soreness and induration at the site of injection lasting one to three days;</li>
<li>low-grade fever; and</li>
<li>severe systemic reactions (rare).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="herpes-zoster-shingles">Herpes zoster (shingles)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#herpes-zoster-shingles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Shingles is also known as zoster or herpes zoster, and it is caused by the same virus that is responsible for chickenpox (the varicella zoster virus)</p>
<p>Most of us will have had chickenpox (or been exposed to it) as a child. The problem with this virus is that even after we have recovered from the acute illness, the virus stays in the body. It lies dormant in the nerve cells and can reactivate at a later stage, usually when the immune system is weakened. Anyone who has had chickenpox can get shingles.</p>
<p>The incidence and severity tends to increase with age. You have about a one in four chance of developing shingles at some time in your life.</p>
<p>When the virus is reactivated, it travels from the clusters of nerve cell bodies (ganglia) in the spinal nerve to the area supplied by that nerve in the skin. The nerves supply various areas called dermatomes, and the shingles rash will typically affect very specific areas on one or the other side of the body (see Figure 19.2).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-c792f11b.jpeg" alt="img-54.jpeg">
Figure 19.2 Shingles</p>
<p>While the virus is travelling along the nerve, it causes symptoms such as pain, tingling or itching and numbness. There may also be generalised malaise, headache and fever. When it reaches the skin, the virus produces a blistering rash, usually with intense pain and itching. The rash will typically last for two to four weeks (see Box 19.4)</p>
<p>Box 19.4 Symptoms of shingles</p>
<ul>
<li>Abnormal skin sensations or pain in the affected area.</li>
<li>Headache, photophobia, malaise and occasionally fever may occur before the rash appears.</li>
<li>Within days or weeks, a unilateral (one-sided) vesicular rash (fluid-filled blisters) appears in a dermatomal distribution.</li>
<li>The affected area may be intensely painful, with tingling, pricking or numbness and intense itching.</li>
<li>The rash typically lasts between two and four weeks.</li>
</ul>
<p>You cannot catch shingles from somebody else with shingles, but you can catch chickenpox from shingles if the lesions are still blistering and uncovered and you have close contact with that person (assuming you haven't had chickenpox before).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications-of-shingles">Complications of shingles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications-of-shingles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Post-herpetic neuralgia (PHN) is nerve pain that persists in the affected area, typically lasting for three to six months, and sometimes longer. The severity of the pain varies, and it may be triggered by stimulation, such as touch or wind on the face.</p>
<p>Other complications will depend on the area affected, and may include things such as facial palsy, eye problems (e.g. keratitis, retinitis and corneal ulceration), pneumonia, hepatitis and encephalitis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="shingles-vaccine">Shingles vaccine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shingles-vaccine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The complications of shingles often result in hospitalisation and sometimes death, especially in older people. Treatments for shingles tend to have side effects or simply don't work very well. The vaccine was developed with the aim of reducing the incidence and severity of shingles disease in older adults, where the risk and severity of disease is higher. It will also save a huge amount of money for the NHS because of saved hospital days, doctor appointments and prescriptions. Based on the findings of a cost-effectiveness analysis, the Joint Committee on Vaccination and Immunisation (JCVI) recommended a universal routine herpes zoster (shingles) vaccination programme for adults aged 70 years, with a catch-up programme for those aged 71-79 years (PHE 2018b).</p>
<p>The vaccine being used in the UK in 2018 is called Zostavax, and this is a live vaccine.</p>
<p>Shingrix is a non-live vaccine that is not currently (as of 2018) licensed in the UK, but it may be considered for use in future programmes.</p>
<p>Zostavax is provided as a white crystalline plug in a vial with a liquid diluent in a separate syringe. It is reconstituted with the diluent to produce 0.65 ml of an off-white/pale yellow, hazy liquid. The vaccine should only be reconstituted just prior to giving the injection, and will be wasted if not used after 30 minutes. When reconstituting the vaccine, it should only be agitated gently to avoid bubbles developing, which then make it difficult to draw up. YouTube tutorials are available to show you how to do this (e.g. <a href="http://www.merckvaccines.com/Products/Zostavax/shingles-videos/howto-reconstitute-and-administer-frozen-vaccine/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.merckvaccines.com/Products/Zostavax/shingles-videos/howto-reconstitute-and-administer-frozen-vaccine/</a>), and it is well worth spending a few minutes watching one of these before you embark on the procedure, or you may find yourself wasting £100 worth of precious vaccine!</p>
<p>Zostavax is given intramuscularly into the deltoid muscle in the upper arm or the vastus lateralis in the anterolateral thigh. It can also be given subcutaneously if the patient has a bleeding disorder.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="contraindications">Contraindications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contraindications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Zostavax is a live vaccine, and so should not be given to anyone who may have impaired immunity. As with the intranasal flu vaccine for children, it is important to remember this! Always keep a paper copy of the contraindications handy and always ask the appropriate questions before giving the vaccine (see Box 19.6).</p>
<p>The contraindications are as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>primary or acquired immunodeficiency;</li>
<li>patients receiving immunosuppressive or immunomodulating therapy;</li>
<li>pregnancy; and</li>
<li>confirmed anaphylactic reaction to a previous dose or any component.</li>
</ul>
<p>More details are available in The Green Book.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="precautions">Precautions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#precautions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
