<li>Acute illness with fever – postpone the injection.</li>
<li>Patients receiving antiviral medication – postpone the injection.</li>
<li>Active untreated TB – defer until resolved.</li>
<li>Immunosuppressed patients who have been advised that they need protection against shingles – should be referred to the nurse.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b12-deficiency">B12 DEFICIENCY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b12-deficiency" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>We all need vitamin B₁₂ to help us create red blood cells and to keep our nervous system healthy. It is also needed to absorb folic acid and release energy, and it is essential to lower the levels of a chemical called homocysteine. High levels of this chemical are associated with heart disease and stroke.</p>
<p>If we are lacking in B₁₂, we will eventually be unable to produce enough red blood cells, and will therefore be unable to carry enough oxygen around the body, resulting in anaemia, but don't get confused – this type of anaemia has nothing to do with iron deficiency! There are different types of anaemia with different causes, but ultimately they all interfere with our ability to carry oxygen, and so we exhibit the same sorts of symptoms.</p>
<p>&gt; Anaemia occurs when the body is unable to transport oxygen efficiently, usually because there are fewer red blood cells than normal or there is not enough haemoglobin in the red cells.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity">ACTIVITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>How many symptoms of anaemia can you think of?</p>
<p>Check your answers against the list in Box 19.5.</p>
<p>Because B₁₂ is also important for a properly functioning nervous system, a deficiency can also result in neurological symptoms such as tingling fingers and toes, confusion, memory loss, and muscle weakness.</p>
<p>Vitamin B₁₂ is a water-soluble vitamin that is naturally present in some foods such as meat, poultry and dairy produce. It is added to other foods and is available as a dietary supplement and a prescription medication. Vitamin B₁₂ is also known as the ‘extrinsic factor’ because it comes from outside the body. We produce a chemical in the parietal cells of the stomach lining called the ‘intrinsic factor’. This enhances the absorption of vitamin B₁₂, which then occurs further down in the gut at the end of the small intestine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-19-5-symptoms-of-anaemia-nhs-choices-2016b">Box 19.5 Symptoms of anaemia (NHS Choices 2016b)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-19-5-symptoms-of-anaemia-nhs-choices-2016b" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Extreme tiredness</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Lack of energy</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Breathlessness</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Feeling faint</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dizziness</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Palpitations</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Headaches</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pale skin</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Tinnitus</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Loss of appetite and weight loss</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Anaemia caused by B_{12} and folate deficiency may also cause further symptoms, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>pale yellow tinge to the skin;</li>
<li>sore red tongue (glossitis);</li>
<li>mouth ulcers;</li>
<li>pins and needles in the hands and feet;</li>
<li>disturbed vision;</li>
<li>irritability;</li>
<li>reduced sense of taste;</li>
<li>changes in movement;</li>
<li>muscle weakness;</li>
<li>changes in thoughts, feelings and behavior;</li>
<li>decline in mental abilities; and</li>
<li>diarrhoea.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="causes-of-b-12-deficiency">Causes of B_{12} deficiency<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes-of-b-12-deficiency" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Pernicious anaemia is the most common cause of B_{12} deficiency. It is an autoimmune disorder (where the body attacks itself) because the immune system makes antibodies that act against the parietal cells in the stomach or against the intrinsic factor produced by those cells. This means that the patient is unable to absorb enough B_{12}. Incidentally (if you take blood), this is the reason why you may be asked to do a blood sample for ‘intrinsic factor antibodies' or ‘parietal cell antibodies'. It is a check for pernicious anaemia.</li>
<li>Inflammatory bowel disease (e.g. Crohn's disease or ulcerative colitis). This interferes with absorption of B_{12} lower down in the intestine.</li>
<li>Some diets, such as a vegan diet, because B_{12} is not found in vegetables or fruit, but it is available in yeast extract, fortified cereals, soya milk and soya products.</li>
<li>Excessive alcohol because this causes inflammation or atrophy of the stomach lining, and may therefore interfere with the effect of the intrinsic factor.</li>
<li>Some drugs, such as metformin. A large percentage of patients who are on long-term metformin will develop B_{12} deficiency. This is an important point to remember because metformin is given to patients who have diabetes. These patients may often have a complication of their diabetes called peripheral neuropathy where the nerves are damaged. This results in tingling, pain or numbness of the toes, and this is also a symptom of severe B_{12} deficiency! It is quite possible that such symptoms will be attributed to the diabetes when in fact they may be due to B_{12} deficiency caused by their metformin. Such neurological damage is irreversible, and the importance of detecting this deficiency early is now well recognised. It is therefore important to monitor those diabetic patients on long-term metformin for B_{12} levels to check for B_{12} deficiency (Chapman et al. 2016).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-12-injection">B_{12} injection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-12-injection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>When the patient has B_{12} deficiency because they cannot absorb vitamin B_{12} from the gut, they will usually be prescribed replacement therapy with injectable hydroxocobalamin, which is a form of B_{12}. The injection is a clear red liquid presented as 1 mg/ml (or 1,000 mcg/ml) in a glass ampoule. It must be stored at a temperature of less than 25°C and it should be protected from light, in its box.</p>
<p>Breaking the neck of a glass ampoule and drawing up the fluid can be tricky and takes practice. I hope you'll be shown how to do this during your immunisation training, but failing that, ask your nurse to show you the first time. There are also plenty of YouTube video tutorials — watch a good one before you try it, and remember to protect your hands from glass fragments! If you do it properly, there should be a clean break with minimal glass fragments. It would be very embarrassing to be standing there with bloodied hands in front of your patient after breaking a glass ampoule incorrectly!</p>
<p>Hydroxocobalamin is given intramuscularly into the deltoid muscle of the upper outer arm, or into the</p>
<p>vastus lateralis in the anterolateral thigh. It can be given subcutaneously if the patient has a bleeding disorder.</p>
<p>Most patients will have a significant reduction in their levels of B_{12} by the time they are diagnosed, so they need a large dose initially to boost their levels This is called the loading dose, and usually comprises six injections, one every two to four days over two weeks. There is no reason why the HCA cannot give the loading doses, providing the PSD is specific about the dose and the dates that the injections should be given. After the loading doses, the usual maintenance dose is one injection every 12 weeks, but if the patient has developed neurological symptoms they may be offered it at eight weekly intervals.</p>
<p>Open a pre-injection swab and use the empty packet as a cover for the top of the ampoule. You can then break the top of the ampoule into the cover.</p>
<p>When designing a PSD for a hydroxocobalamin injection, it makes sense to prepare one that can then be inserted into the patient's notes and used each time the patient presents. There is no need to make out a new one each time as it is the same medication and same dose. It is useful to state in the PSD that the patient should be given the injection every 12 weeks (or eight weeks), or 3 days either side of the due date. This allows a little flexibility for those patients who are unable to attend on a specific day.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="contraindications">Contraindications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contraindications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Hypersensitivity to any ingredient (but this is rare).</p>
<p>Adverse reactions are rare, but may include symptoms such as a rash, nausea, diarrhoea, pain at injection site, headache or irregular heartbeat.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="giving-an-injection-the-procedure">Giving an Injection: The Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#giving-an-injection-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You can download the Royal College of Nursing/Public Health England document Immunisation Knowledge and Competence Skills Assessment Tool (for non-registered staff), which provides an excellent record for you to complete as evidence of your competence (RCN and PHE 2015).</p>
<p>You must attend an appropriate training course and have written evidence of your competence before giving injections.</p>
<ol>
<li>Prepare your patient (see Box 19.6).</li>
<li>Make sure the patient is sitting down (preferably) with their arm relaxed by their side or in their lap. The injection site should be fully exposed. If you are injecting into the deltoid muscle, make sure the upper arm is adequately exposed. If the sleeve is rolled up, it should not be too tight as this works as a tourniquet and may make the patient bleed. Only clean the skin with soap and water if the skin is visibly dirty. There is no need to use alcohol swabs.</li>
<li>Decontaminate your hands. There is no need to wear gloves.</li>
<li>Prepare the equipment. Some flu vaccinations are prefilled with an integral needle, but for other injections you may need to prepare the syringe, needle and injection.</li>
<li>Check the vaccine/injection to make sure you have the correct product, the correct dose, and that it is not past the expiry date. If you are giving a vaccine, check that the cold chain has been maintained.</li>
<li>Examine the colour and composition of the vaccine/injection and ensure it conforms with the summary of product characteristics (SPC). If you are unsure about this, it can usually be found in a leaflet inside the box.</li>
<li>If the injection is prefilled (e.g. inactivated flu vaccine), there is not usually any need to expel the air from the syringe (PHE 2014b).</li>
<li>Injections that are supplied as suspensions (e.g. inactivated influenza) will need to be shaken before administration.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="19-6-preparing-the-patient">19.6 Preparing the patient<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#19-6-preparing-the-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Check patient identity.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Check the PSD is in place for that patient.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Explain the procedure and make sure your patient (or person with parental responsibility, if giving LAIV to a patient under 16) gives informed consent. Include information about advantages, disadvantages, possible side effects and their management in your explanation, and offer a patient advice leaflet.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ask-the-following-questions">Ask the following questions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ask-the-following-questions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Do you feel unwell today?</li>
<li>Have you had (name of injection/vaccine) before?</li>
<li>Have you had any reaction to a vaccination/medication before?</li>
<li>Do you have an egg allergy? (prior to giving inactivated flu vaccine only)</li>
<li>Are you pregnant or breastfeeding? (prior to giving LAIV, shingles or pneumococcal vaccine)</li>
<li>Do you have lowered immunity due to disease or treatment? (prior to giving LAIV or shingles vaccine)</li>
<li>Do you have a bleeding disorder? (injections should be given subcutaneously when possible for these patients -check local guidelines and SPC for each vaccine) (Makris et al. 2003).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="intramuscular-im-injection-technique">Intramuscular (IM) injection technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intramuscular-im-injection-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>These are usually given with a blue needle (25 mm long, 23 gauge) for a person of average build. Larger patients with more subcutaneous fat will need a longer needle (green, 38 mm long) to ensure the medication is given into the muscle and not into the subcutaneous layer. An individual assessment must be made, and if you are unsure check with the registered nurse first.</p>
<ul>
<li>Locate the injection site over the deltoid muscle by drawing an imaginary line two fingers' breadth below the acromion process (shoulder tip). The injection should be given into an imaginary triangle whose base is formed by the line and whose apex will be in line with the axilla. This ensures that the injection is given into the belly of the muscle for optimum absorption and effect (see Figure 19.3).</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-97d188ad.jpeg" alt="img-55.jpeg">
Figure 19.3 Intramuscular injection site</p>
<ul>
<li>Stretch the skin over the injection site between the thumb and forefinger of your non-dominant hand. With your dominant hand, hold the syringe near the tip (like a pencil or dart) and insert the needle at a 90-degree angle to the skin, all the way in (see Figure 19.6). Make sure the hand holding the syringe is somehow in contact with the patient's arm, either by extending your little finger or by resting the edge of your little finger on the skin. This keeps you in control of the syringe and needle in case the patient moves</li>
</ul>
<p>(see Figure 19.4). Push the plunger in steadily (at about  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1\mathrm{ml}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ml</span></span></span></span></span></span>  per second) until all the medication has been given. Take the needle straight out and have cotton wool ready in case there is a spot of blood, but don't rub the area.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-3d3fd399.jpeg" alt="img-56.jpeg">
Figure 19.4 Giving an intramuscular injection</p>
<ul>
<li>Some local policies are promoting the Z-track technique. To do this, you need to locate the area as before, but instead of stretching the skin, push the skin to the side of the site before inserting the needle at 90 degrees. Push the plunger of the syringe steadily to administer the vaccine. Leave in position for 10 seconds, then remove the needle and allow the skin to move back into place. This makes a seal in the muscle and prevents leakage of the medication into the surrounding tissue.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="subcutaneous-sc-injection-technique">Subcutaneous (SC) injection technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#subcutaneous-sc-injection-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>These are usually given with an orange needle (16 mm long, 25 gauge).</p>
<ul>
<li>Locate the injection site, which is usually on the upper outer arm (see Figure 19.5).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="preferred-site-for-sc-injection-is-upper-outer-arm">Preferred site for SC injection is upper outer arm<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preferred-site-for-sc-injection-is-upper-outer-arm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-334cc500.jpeg" alt="img-57.jpeg">
Figure 19.5 Subcutaneous injection site</p>
<ul>
<li>Bunch the skin over the area using the thumb and forefinger of the non-dominant hand. With your dominant hand, hold the syringe comfortably so that you can insert the needle at a 45-degree angle to the skin, all the way in (see Figure 19.6). Try to keep the hand that is holding the syringe in contact with the patient's arm so if they move, you move with them! Push the plunger in steadily until all the medication has been given. Take the needle straight out and have cotton wool ready in case there is a spot of blood, but don't rub the area.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-568c1f64.jpeg" alt="img-58.jpeg">
The correct Angle and Way to Hold the Syringe</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-84f20dac.jpeg" alt="img-59.jpeg">
Figure 19.6 Needle angles for subcutaneous and intramuscular injections</p>
<ol start="9">
<li>
<p>Dispose of the needle and syringe immediately into a sharps bin, which should be close at hand.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Apply a small plaster or cotton wool and tape if necessary (but remember to check for allergies first).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Observe the patient for signs of immediate adverse reaction.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Decontaminate your hands.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Document the procedure, including the following information:</p>
</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>consent obtained;</li>
<li>no contraindications;
vaccine name, batch number, dose, route, site, expiry date;
any problems; and
name of immuniser and date of administration.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="giving-an-intranasal-vaccination-laiv-the-procedure-see-figure-19-7">GIVING AN INTRANASAL VACCINATION (LAIV): THE PROCEDURE (SEE FIGURE 19.7)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#giving-an-intranasal-vaccination-laiv-the-procedure-see-figure-19-7" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Prepare the patient (see Box 19.6).</li>
<li>Decontaminate your hands.</li>
<li>Make sure the child is sitting comfortably with their head slightly tilted back. Toddlers should be held securely on the accompanying adult's lap.</li>
<li>Remove the rubber tip protector. Do not remove the dose divider clip.</li>
<li>Place the tip just inside the nostril, and with a single rapid motion depress the plunger until the dose divider prevents you from going any further. This will deliver  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>0.1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">0.1\mathrm{ml}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord">0.1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ml</span></span></span></span></span></span>  into the nostril. Use a rapid motion when depressing the plunger to achieve a fine mist, which is better absorbed by the nasal mucosa. If you push the plunger too slowly, the liquid comes out in a dribble, and is likely to run out of the nose and not be absorbed as effectively.</li>
<li>Pinch and remove the dose divider clip and administer the remaining dose in the other nostril in the same way as before.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-e12e2670.jpeg" alt="img-60.jpeg">
Figure 19.7 Giving an intranasal vaccine</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>The patient should breathe normally and not sniff.</li>
<li>If their nose runs or they sneeze following the procedure, reassure them that the vaccine will still be effective, and they do not need to have it again.</li>
<li>Observe the patient for signs of immediate adverse reaction.</li>
<li>Decontaminate your hands.</li>
<li>Document the procedure as for the IM and SC techniques.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="box-19-7-symptoms-of-flu">Box 19.7 Symptoms of flu<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-19-7-symptoms-of-flu" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Sudden onset of fever (less likely with a simple cold) and chills or rigors</li>
<li>Headache</li>
<li>Myalgia (aching muscles)</li>
<li>Extreme fatigue</li>
<li>Dry cough</li>
<li>Sneezing and sore throat (sometimes)</li>
<li>Nasal congestion</li>
<li>May be vomiting and diarrhoea (e.g. as in swine flu)</li>
<li>Recovery in seven days – sometimes longer</li>
<li>May require hospitalisation</li>
</ul>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="838646a4">VACCINATION AND B₁₂ QUIZ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#838646a4" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Using a framework for reflection (see Chapter 2), try reflecting on a consultation where you watched or gave a vaccination or other injection.</p>
<p>How did it go and what did you learn?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ear-examination-and-irrigation">Ear examination and irrigation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ear-examination-and-irrigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Earl A. A. A. A.</p>
<p>In recent years, HCAs and APs have been taking on more complex tasks such as ear irrigation. Removal of earwax is one of those rare things you can do for your patient that can provide instant relief when their hearing has been compromised by a build-up of wax, but unfortunately it is a task that has provoked much controversy over the years. This is largely because the old method of syringing the ears using a metal syringe often resulted in problems afterwards, such as perforation or infection (Aung and Mullet 2002). This in turn resulted in litigation in many cases. By comparison, removal of wax using an electronic irrigator is a very safe procedure, providing it is done in accordance with the guidelines. However, although irrigation is relatively safe, there are risks associated with it, as with any procedure, so before agreeing to examine a patient's ear or irrigating it, you must complete a recognised ear irrigation course, such as the one offered by the Rotherham Primary Ear Care Centre (2017). These courses are available all over the UK and are delivered by trainers who are licensed to the Ear Care Centre. Other courses are available, but always make sure the course providers have a good reputation or can offer accredited training.</p>
<p>A good background understanding of ear anatomy and physiology, as well as a thorough practical training in the procedure, is essential if this task is to be carried out safely. This chapter will provide the background knowledge required and will describe the procedure in line with the Rotherham Primary Ear Care Centre guidelines.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ear-anatomy-and-the-hearing-process">EAR ANATOMY AND THE HEARING PROCESS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ear-anatomy-and-the-hearing-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The ear is divided into three parts, usually described as the outer, middle and inner ear (see Figure 20.1).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="the-outer-ear">The outer ear<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-outer-ear" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The outer ear is made up of the pinna (see Figure 20.2) and the external auditory meatus (ear canal). The pinna is the external structure, and is designed to collect and direct the sound waves into the ear canal. The ear canal is a skin-lined channel ending at the outer surface of the tympanic membrane (TM or eardrum).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-315c38cf.jpeg" alt="img-61.jpeg">
Figure 20.1 Ear anatomy</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-60342142.jpeg" alt="img-62.jpeg">
Figure 20.2 External ear</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-middle-ear">The middle ear<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-middle-ear" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The middle ear is the area behind the eardrum where three little bones are located. These tiny bones, known by their Latin names as the malleus, the incus and the stapes, are collectively called the ossicles. These bones</p>
<p>have joints between them in the same way as other groups of bones do, and they are the only bones in your body that should not grow from the moment you are born to the moment you die!</p>
<p>Sound waves are channelled through the pinna into the ear canal, and they hit the eardrum, making it vibrate. This vibration is transmitted through the ossicles into the inner ear.</p>
<p>The malleus is the bone that sits against the eardrum from behind and gives it the characteristic shape. It is the features of this bone that you look for during otoscopy. Remember, the eardrum is made up of a soft part - pars flaccida (seen above the short process of the malleus) and the taut part, which is stretched tightly over the long process of the malleus (also called the handle of the malleus), making it transparent and shiny - the pars tensa (seen below the short process of the malleus). The light reflex is created where the long process of the malleus creates a crease in the pars tensa, which picks up and reflects the light from the otoscope (see Figure 20.3).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-ff30591a.jpeg" alt="img-63.jpeg">
Figure 20.3 Eardrum features</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-inner-ear">The inner ear<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-inner-ear" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The inner ear consists of the cochlea (organ of hearing) and the semicircular canals (organ of balance).</p>
<p>The cochlea is a coiled structure with inner channels that are filled with fluid. Vibrations are transmitted from the ossicles, through the oval window (a membrane covered opening underneath the stapes) into the fluid filled channels, where they create ripples. The ripples pass over tiny hairs called cilia located deep within the cochlea, and the subsequent movement of the cilia sends a signal via the auditory nerve to the brain, where it is then interpreted as noise. The brain is usually able to determine where the noise is coming from and what is causing it. This whole process is very complex and delicate. If the sound waves are created by a very loud noise, then their amplitude will be greater, and this will cause bigger vibrations and bigger ripples. When the ripples become very big, instead of wafting the cilia gently, they will flatten them all, like a field of corn in a hurricane. Not all the cilia will recover from this, and those that do will recover at different rates. This can result in abnormal signals being sent to the brain, creating buzzing or ringing in the ears and a reduction in hearing for a while. Think of the last time you went to a music gig - what happened when you came out? You were probably shouting at each other for a while until your hearing recovered! If our ears are constantly exposed to loud noise, then early onset deafness is inevitable as more and more of those cilia are flattened and unable to recover.</p>
<p>The semicircular canals operate in a similar way, except that the signal sent back to the brain is interpreted as a sense of position instead of noise.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="wax">WAX<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#wax" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Many patients seem to think that wax is dirty and should be cleaned out of the ear regardless of whether it is causing a problem or not. Wax is actually very important for protection, lubrication and for slowing down the growth of bacteria. It is made up of cerumen (a sweat-like substance), sebum (an oily substance), dead skin</p>
<p>cells and debris. The composition of wax will vary from individual to individual, depending on age, diet and the environment. The ceruminous glands, where the cerumen is produced, are located just inside the ear canal. This is a fact worth pointing out to your patients when the wax is seen pushed up against their eardrum, usually because of poking cotton buds or other small items in the ear!</p>
<p>Sometimes it may be necessary to remove wax by irrigation, but this should always be a last resort because putting water inside the ear canal increases the risk of infection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="activity-1">ACTIVITY 1<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#activity-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Consider what wax is made up of and then list as many factors as you can think of that may make the patient more susceptible to increased or impacted earwax.</p>
<p>Check your answers in Box 20.1.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="softening-the-wax">Softening the wax<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#softening-the-wax" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Olive oil drops at room temperature are the cheapest and most suitable way of softening the wax. The patient should be instructed to instil the drops every morning and evening for three to five days. They should lie on the unaffected side for 10 minutes after instilling the drops and avoid the use of cotton wool in the ear. An olive oil ear spray is available on prescription and can be bought over the counter, and this may be very useful for your elderly patients who live alone and find it difficult to instil the drops.</p>
<p>It is always worth advising patients who have a frequent build-up of wax in their ears to instil olive oil drops regularly (once or twice a week) as this is sometimes all that is needed to soften the wax and facilitate the natural cleaning process of the ear without the need for irrigation.</p>
<p>Sodium bicarbonate can also be used (Allen 2016), but this can have a drying effect, especially in the elderly who tend to have a dryer and thinner meatal skin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-20-1-factors-that-may-increase-wax-accumulation">Box 20.1 Factors that may increase wax accumulation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-20-1-factors-that-may-increase-wax-accumulation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Fatty diet/high cholesterol</li>
<li>Excessive sweating</li>
<li>Narrow ear canals – Down’s syndrome patients tend to have narrower ear canals, which makes them more prone to problems with earwax (National Deaf Children’s Society 2018)</li>
<li>Noisy or dusty environment</li>
<li>Age</li>
<li>Genetics</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ear-irrigation-the-procedure">EAR IRRIGATION: THE PROCEDURE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ear-irrigation-the-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Only those patients who have had the procedure done previously with no complications are suitable for the HCA to irrigate. Patients must be assessed by the doctor or nurse first to determine the presence of wax and their suitability for irrigation. If most of the eardrum is visible, then irrigation will not normally be necessary. The only exception to this will be if the patient is to have a hearing aid fitted, in which case the ear canal must be completely clear.</p>
<p>The procedure outlined below is adapted from the Rotherham Primary Ear Care Centre (2017).</p>
<ul>
<li>Assemble the equipment (see Box 20.2).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-20-2-equipment-for-ear-irrigation">Box 20.2 Equipment for ear irrigation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-20-2-equipment-for-ear-irrigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Electronic irrigator (Projet or Propulse 2017 model).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Disposable jet tips.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Warm water (37°C).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Otoscope and disposable speculae (with good batteries).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Headlight (with good batteries).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Single-use Noots tank or equivalent. A disposable plastic bag liner in a metal Noots tank is a cheaper alternative to the disposable cardboard variety and takes up less space.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Single-use Jobson Horne Probe or equivalent.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cotton wool.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Disposable waterproof cape.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Tissues.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Disposable gloves.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Receiver for dirty cotton wool, etc.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Check patient identity and obtain verbal informed consent. As part of this process, you should advise the patient of the possible risks, including the small risk of perforation and infection.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Check for any contraindications or precautions (see Box 20.3).</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-20-3-ear-irrigation-contraindications-and-precautions">Box 20.3 Ear irrigation: contraindications and precautions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-20-3-ear-irrigation-contraindications-and-precautions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="contraindications-to-irrigation">Contraindications to irrigation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contraindications-to-irrigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Do not irrigate the ear. Refer the patient back to the doctor.</p>
<ul>
<li>Never had the procedure done before.</li>
<li>Previous complications following the procedure.</li>
<li>Current perforation or history of mucus discharge from the ear in the last year.</li>
<li>History of middle ear infection within the last six weeks (as there may be a perforation following production of infected mucus in the middle ear).</li>
<li>Any ear surgery apart from grommets that have extruded 18 months previously.</li>
<li>Outer ear infection (otitis externa) with pain.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="precautions">Precautions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#precautions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Check with the nurse or doctor for advice before proceeding.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Healed perforation. The eardrum is not as strong and may re-perforate.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cleft palate (repaired or not). If there is an existing perforation as well, there is a risk of water being directed into the middle ear.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dizziness. Irrigation may make this worse.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Tinnitus. Irrigation may make this worse.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>On oral anticoagulants. The ear canal is more likely to bleed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Decontaminate your hands.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examine the ear while sitting at the same height as the patient. Look around the pinna and check the surrounding scalp for any skin lesions, signs of previous surgery, infection or any other abnormalities.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examine inside the ear canal using an otoscope (preferably one with a white halogen fibre-optic light) fitted with the largest speculum that will fit comfortably into the ear canal. Straighten out the ear canal by</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>gently grasping the cartilaginous part of the pinna and pulling upwards and outwards. Hold the otoscope like a pen or dart and rest your little finger on the patient's cheek while you insert the speculum into the ear canal (see Figure 20.4). Use the right hand to view the right ear and the left hand to view the left ear if you feel you can do this safely. If the wax is obscuring the eardrum and is soft enough, proceed with irrigation.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-635ce0c0.jpeg" alt="img-64.jpeg">
Figure 20.4 Using the otoscope safely</p>
<ul>
<li>Protect the patient with a waterproof cape.</li>
<li>Put the headlight on.</li>
<li>Put on the gloves. Earwax is a body fluid, and there is a small risk of contamination with hepatitis B (Kalcioglu et al. 2004).</li>
<li>Explain the procedure to your patient and ask them to indicate if they have any pain or dizziness during the irrigation, in which case the procedure should be stopped immediately and the patient referred to the nurse or doctor.</li>
<li>Sit at the same height as the patient.</li>
<li>Run the water through the tubing to expel any trapped cold water. This also helps the patient get used to the noise of the irrigator.</li>
<li>Ask the patient to hold the Noots tank under their ear.</li>
<li>Run a few drops of the water on to the patient's earlobe to check if the temperature is comfortable for them.</li>
<li>Direct the headlight at the ear and – while gently lifting the ear upwards and outwards to straighten the ear canal – insert the jet tip just inside the ear, directing it towards the back of the head. If you imagine the eardrum like a clock face, the water stream should be directed at 11 o'clock for the right ear and 1 o'clock for the left ear (see Figure 20.5).</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-968d2164.jpeg" alt="img-65.jpeg">
Figure 20.5 Positioning the jet tip in the ear canal</p>
<p>Starting with minimum pressure, operate the foot pedal to direct a steady stream of water into the ear canal until the wax comes out. Keep the jet tip in the same position and avoid twisting it as this may cause trauma to the ear canal.</p>
<ul>
<li>If no wax comes out after a few minutes, increase the pressure to medium. Check frequently with the otoscope to see if the wax is moving and to make sure there is no inflammation or bleeding in the ear canal, in which case the procedure should be stopped.</li>
<li>Continue until the ear is clear and the eardrum visible or until one tank of water has been used. Use no more than one tank per ear. If you are unsuccessful at this stage, the patient should be advised to continue with olive oil for a few more days before any further attempts are made to irrigate.</li>
<li>When the irrigation is completed and the wax has been removed (or even if it has been unsuccessful), dry the ear canal carefully. Stagnation of water and abrasions sustained during the procedure will predispose to infection (NHS Choices 2018b), so it is essential that dry mopping is performed properly. Ask the patient to tip their head to one side to allow the water to drain as much as possible. Then use cotton wool wrapped securely around the tip of the Jobson Horne Probe or carbon curette, and - with your headlight shining on the ear - insert the cotton wool-covered probe into the ear canal. Mop the skin of the ear canal, gently moving around the canal in a clockwise direction. Personally, I think good-quality cotton buds can also be used for this, and may well be a cheaper option instead of single-use probes or curettes, but please avoid giving patients the impression that it is OK for them to use cotton buds! (Use of cotton buds for drying the ear canal is advice based on the author's personal opinion, and is not included in the Rotherham Ear Care Centre guidelines.) The ear canal is only about  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>2.4</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">2.4\mathrm{cm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">2.4</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">cm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  long, so keep your fingers near the tip of the probe to avoid putting it in too far, and always use a headlight so that you can visualise the tip.</li>
<li>Examine the ear canal again using the otoscope and check that the eardrum is intact with normal features (see Figure 20.3).</li>
<li>Remove your gloves and decontaminate your hands.</li>
<li>Advise your patient to keep the ear dry for the next 24 to 48 hours and to return if they have any problems or concerns following the procedure.</li>
<li>Document the process, including information as follows:</li>
</ul>
<p>a. informed consent obtained;
b. no contraindications;
c. what was seen before (e.g. soft wax obscuring eardrum);</p>
<p>d. procedure (e.g. wax successfully or unsuccessfully irrigated, ear canal dry mopped);
e. what was seen afterwards (e.g. eardrum intact with normal features); and
f. aftercare advice given.</p>
<ul>
<li>Dispose of equipment in line with local policy.</li>
<li>Decontaminate irrigator according to manufacturer’s instructions or follow the Rotherham Ear Care Guidance document (see Box 20.4).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tips">Tips<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tips" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>If the wax is proving stubborn to remove, ask the patient to open and close their mouth a few times. Jaw movement helps to dislodge wax, and this might be all it takes!</li>
<li>If you’re still having trouble, move on to the other ear (assuming the patient needs both ears irrigating!) and then come back to the first ear. Sometimes the water left in the ear will act as a softener and the wax will come out easily after a few minutes. If only one ear is to be irrigated, ask the patient to sit out in the waiting room for 10 minutes while you see to another patient and then try again.</li>
<li>If your patient starts to cough during the procedure, it is a good idea to stop! Coughing may indicate stimulation of the auricular branch of the vagus nerve that runs along the floor of the ear canal. The vagus nerve is responsible for controlling the heart rate and the blood pressure (BP), and if stimulated the heart rate and BP may drop suddenly, causing a vasovagal syncope (or faint). It is not worth continuing with irrigation in this case as the risk of harm to the patient outweighs the benefits.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="box-20-4-decontaminating-the-electronic-irrigator">Box 20.4 Decontaminating the electronic irrigator<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#box-20-4-decontaminating-the-electronic-irrigator" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Follow the manufacturer’s instructions for the machine you are using. These may vary slightly from those given below.</p>
<p>Always wear appropriate personal protective equipment such as gloves, apron and goggles, and only perform decontamination in a well-ventilated room. This is because the solution used for decontamination (sodium dichloroisocyanurate, NaDCC) is very toxic and can cause skin irritation and respiratory problems if inhaled.</p>
<p>The aim of the decontamination process is to clean out the stagnant water and solid particles from the tubing, and it should be carried out before every clinic when there are patients attending for ear irrigation. You should also decontaminate the equipment at the end of a busy clinic.</p>
<ol>
<li>Discard the jet tip and dispose in the clinical waste.</li>
<li>Fill the tank with NaDCC solution made up as per manufacturer’s instructions. Tablets such as Presept, Klorsept and Chlor-clean are suitable for this.</li>
<li>Flush the solution through to the end of the tubing and then leave for 10 minutes (no longer).</li>
<li>Empty the tank and fill with fresh water. Flush the fresh water through the tubing until the tank is empty.</li>
<li>Dry carefully and store the machine in a cupboard until next required.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-bit-about-gadgets">A bit about gadgets!<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-bit-about-gadgets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Patients will often use all manner of gadgets to try to remove wax from their ears, and may well ask your advice about them, so it’s good to have an idea of what is out there.</p>
<p>Try typing ‘home earwax removal’ into images on your search engine and I think you will be amazed and horrified at what is available for people to buy and stick into their ears! Basically, we should be discouraging people from putting ‘anything smaller than their elbow’ into their ear when they are unable to see what they are doing. This is unsafe practice, and is likely to lead to impaction of wax or infection in some patients.</p>
<p>Hopi candling is an alternative therapy that may be useful as a relaxation therapy, but it should not be used at home alone and it does not remove earwax (see Figure 20.6).</p>
<p>The Hopi candle is a fabric candle soaked in beeswax that is put into the ear while the other end is burnt down. The theory is that burning the candle softens the wax and creates a vacuum that sucks it out. This</p>
<p>simply isn't true, and scientific evidence has clearly refuted this claim (Ernst 2004). At the end of the treatment, the client may be shown the 'earwax' along with other dirt and impurities that appear to be collected inside the remnants of the candle. This is simply the remnants of the burnt cotton and beeswax, and is not earwax! In fact, some patients who had no earwax to start with were found to have residues of candle wax in their ears following the procedure!</p>
<p>Quite apart from the fact that this practice does not remove earwax, it is intrinsically dangerous, and there have been many ear injuries associated with candling. So, while it may be used under supervision (and with great care) as an alternative therapy for relaxation, you should not be recommending this to your patients as a method of earwax removal (Rafferty et al. 2007).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260115-91d87bf7.jpeg" alt="img-66.jpeg">
Figure 20.6 Hopi candling - not for wax removal!</p>
<p>Now try the quiz below. You can check back in the text for the answers.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ear-irrigation-quiz">EAR IRRIGATION QUIZ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ear-irrigation-quiz" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Name the three bones found in the middle ear.</li>
<li>What are the two parts of the eardrum called?</li>
<li>The inner ear comprises the cochlea and the semicircular canals. Which of these is the organ of hearing?</li>
<li>What should be used to soften earwax, and how long should it be used for?</li>
<li>Why should you wear a headlight and sit at the same height as the patient when irrigating their ear?</li>
<li>Why might the patient cough when you are irrigating their ear?</li>
<li>What should you do following irrigation, regardless of if the procedure is successful or not, to reduce the risk of infection?</li>
<li>What will you do if the patient answers 'yes' to any of the precautions?</li>
<li>What advice should you give your patient following the procedure?</li>
<li>Why is it important to decontaminate the irrigator in a ventilated room?</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="time-to-reflect">TIME TO REFLECT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time-to-reflect" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Using a framework for reflection (see Chapter 2), try reflecting on a consultation where you watched or performed an ear irrigation.</p>
<p>How did it go and what did you learn?</p>
<p>Remember, nobody died of earwax unless they didn't hear the bus coming!</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="glossary">Glossary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#glossary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Adjuvant: A substance that is added to a vaccine to increase the body's immune response to the vaccine.</p>
<p>Adrenal insufficiency: A condition where the adrenal glands are unable to produce enough steroid hormones such as cortisol. This can be fatal if left untreated.</p>
<p>Alveolus: A tiny air-filled sac at the end of the airways in the lungs. The alveoli are designed to maximise surface area for the exchange of oxygen and carbon dioxide.</p>
<p>Ambulatory blood pressure monitoring (ABPM): A non-invasive method of recording blood pressure over 24 hours while the patient goes about their normal activities. It is thought to provide an accurate reflection of blood pressure.</p>
<p>Anterolateral: Situated in front and to the side.</p>
<p>Anticoagulant: Medication that reduces the ability of the blood to clot.</p>
<p>Antibody (also called an immunoglobulin): A protein manufactured by the body that will neutralise a foreign substance or antigen.</p>
<p>Antigen: A substance that will stimulate an immune response by inducing the production of antibodies. An antigen may be a toxin, chemical, bacteria or virus that comes from outside the body.</p>
<p>Anaemia: A condition where there are fewer red blood cells or less haemoglobin in each red blood cell. Haemoglobin attaches to oxygen, so if there is less of it, the blood is not able to carry as much oxygen around the body.</p>
<p>Anaerobic: Living without oxygen.</p>
<p>Aneurysm: A bulge in an artery wall where the wall is weakened and liable to rupture.</p>
<p>Angina: Pain that comes from the heart. It occurs when the coronary arteries supplying the heart with blood become narrowed.</p>
<p>Anorexia: Lack of appetite.</p>
<p>Anterior: Towards the front.</p>
<p>Antimicrobial: An agent that inhibits the growth of microorganisms or kills them.</p>
<p>Antimuscarinic: The term given to a group of drugs that are smooth muscle relaxants. They will help to dilate or widen the constricted airways in COPD.</p>
<p>Arrhythmia: A problem with the rate or rhythm of the heartbeat.</p>
<p>Arteriole: A very tiny artery, usually the terminal branch of the artery that connects to the capillary.</p>
<p>Arthropathy: Disease of a joint.</p>
<p>Atherosclerosis: A condition where the arteries are narrowed by fatty deposits called plaques or atheromas. The restricted blood flow can damage the affected organ (e.g. the heart) and stop it working properly.</p>
<p>Atrial fibrillation: An abnormal heart rhythm and a major cause of stroke.</p>
<p>Auscultatory: A method of listening to the sounds of the body, such as from the heart or lungs, usually using a stethoscope.</p>
<p>Axilla: The area under the arm. Also known as the armpit.</p>
<p>Bevel: The sloping point at the sharp end of the needle.</p>
<p>Bilirubin: The yellow pigment in bile produced when the liver breaks down old red blood cells.</p>
<p>Bronchodilator: A substance that dilates (opens up) the airways (bronchi and bronchioles) in the lungs.</p>
<p>Bronchus: The large air tube that begins at the end of the trachea and branches into the lungs.</p>
<p>Bundle of His: A bundle of modified heart muscle that transmits the electrical impulse from the atrioventricular node to the right and left ventricles.</p>
<p>Capillary: The smallest blood vessel, where the wall is only one cell thick.</p>
<p>Catalyst: A substance that speeds up a chemical reaction.</p>
<p>Cellulitis: An infection of the deeper layers of the skin, usually caused by group A streptococcus bacteria.</p>
<p>Contraindication: A condition or reason to withhold certain treatment.</p>
<p>Connective tissue: Groups of tissue in the body that maintain the form of the body by supporting, anchoring and connecting the various parts of the body.</p>
<p>Cross-contamination: The transfer of a contaminant from one source to another that may result in infection.</p>
<p>Cyanosis: A bluish discoloration of the skin and mucous membranes, usually due to a lack of oxygen in the blood.</p>
<p>Debridement: The removal of dead, damaged or infected tissue to enable the healing process.</p>
<p>Deep-vein thrombosis (DVT): The formation of a clot in a deep vein, usually in the leg. If a piece of the clot breaks off and travels to the lung, it can cause a pulmonary embolism, which can be fatal.</p>
<p>Dehydration: A reduction in the normal water content of the body, causing an upset in the delicate balance of minerals.</p>
<p>Diaphragm: A sheet of muscle that extends across the bottom of the ribcage separating the thorax from the abdomen.</p>
<p>Domiciliary: Provided at home.</p>
<p>Doppler: Ultrasound used to examine the blood flow in the major arteries and veins.</p>
<p>Efficacy: The capacity to produce a desired result or effect.</p>
<p>Electrolyte: Substances found in the body that carry an electrical charge. They must be present in the right amounts to maintain homeostasis for the proper functioning of the body.</p>
<p>Emphysema: A long-term lung disease where there is damage to the air sacs (alveoli) in the lungs.</p>
<p>Endocarditis: Inflammation of the inner layer of the heart.</p>
<p>Enzyme: Proteins that control the rate of chemical reactions in the body.</p>
<p>Epidemic: A widespread occurrence of an infectious disease occurring in the community during a particular time.</p>
<p>Epithelialisation: Migration of newly formed skin cells across a wound bed during the healing process.</p>
<p>Erythrocyte: Another name for a red blood cell.</p>
<p>Exacerbation: A worsening or flare-up of symptoms.</p>
<p>Excoriation: Tearing or wearing of skin cells, usually due to rubbing or scratching.</p>
<p>Exfoliation: Removal of the dead skin cells on the outermost surface of the skin.</p>
<p>Exudate: Fluid such as pus or clear fluid leaking out of nearby blood vessels into surrounding tissues or wounds where there is inflammation or infection.</p>
<p>Fistula: An abnormal passage or connection between two organs or areas that do not normally connect. This can result from injury, surgery, infection or inflammation.</p>
<p>Fungating: A type of skin lesion characterised by ulceration and necrosis (death of tissue), usually caused by a cancerous growth breaking through the skin. There is usually a characteristic offensive odour.</p>
<p>Gallipot: A small plastic pot used for holding cleaning fluid (e.g. sodium chloride or water) that may be used during a wound dressing or a minor operation.</p>
<p>Genitourinary tract: The system of organs concerned with the production and excretion of urine and those concerned with reproduction.</p>
<p>Haematological test: A blood test that provides information about the type, number and appearance of red and white blood cells and platelets.</p>
<p>Haematuria: Blood in the urine.</p>
<p>Haemoglobin: A substance in red blood cells that combines with and carries oxygen around the body and gives blood its red colour.</p>
<p>Haemophilia: A hereditary condition where the body is unable to control or stop bleeding when a blood vessel is injured.</p>
<p>Heart failure: A condition where the heart is unable to pump enough blood to meet the needs of the body.</p>
<p>Hemosiderosis: Excessive accumulation of iron deposits called hemosiderin in the tissues.</p>
<p>Hydrophilic: Water-loving.</p>
<p>Hypercoaguability: A tendency of the blood to coagulate more quickly than normal, increasing the risk of blood clots.</p>
<p>Hypergranulating: Where tissue is progressing beyond the surface of the wound in the healing process.</p>
<p>Hyperlipidaemia: A high level of fat (cholesterol, low-density lipoprotein and triglycerides) in the blood. An important risk factor for heart disease.</p>
<p>Hyper-responsiveness: A condition where the airways in the lungs get smaller (constrict) when exposed to a trigger or allergen.</p>
<p>Hyperthyroidism: A hormonal condition where the thyroid gland produces too much thyroxine, which speeds up the body's metabolism.</p>
<p>Hypoallergenic: Provokes fewer allergic reactions.</p>
<p>Hypoxia: A deficiency in the amount of oxygen that reaches the tissues of the body.</p>
<p>Immunosuppression: Suppression of the body's immune system, resulting in an inability to fight infection or disease.</p>
<p>Impermeable: Not allowing fluid to pass through.</p>
<p>Inferior vena cava: The large vein that carries deoxygenated blood from the lower half of the body back to the right side of the heart.</p>
<p>In situ: In position.</p>
<p>Insulin resistance: A condition where the cells fail to respond to the normal effects of insulin. May progress to type 2 diabetes.</p>
<p>Intercostal space: The space between two ribs.</p>
<p>Intravenous: Within a vein. Usually refers to giving medication or fluid through a tube or needle inserted into the vein.</p>
<p>Ischaemia: Reduced blood supply depriving an area of essential oxygen and nutrients.</p>
<p>Ketoacidosis: A complication of diabetes where the body is unable to break down glucose because there is not enough insulin. It breaks down fat instead as a source of fuel, and this causes the build-up of a by-product called ketones, which can disrupt the body's metabolism.</p>
<p>Leukaemia: Cancer of the blood or bone marrow.</p>
<p>Lipoprotein: Molecules made of protein and fat that carry cholesterol and similar substances through the blood.</p>
<p>Low adherence: Will not stick easily.</p>
<p>Lymph node clearance: Removal of lymph nodes from the armpit to check if cancer has spread into the nodes and help determine if further treatment is needed. To eliminate any cancerous nodes.</p>
<p>Maceration: A process where the skin is softened and broken down by extended exposure to wetness or moisture.</p>
<p>Malaise: A generalised feeling of discomfort or illness, feeling unwell.</p>
<p>Malpractice: Negligence or incompetence on the part of a professional.</p>
<p>Metabolic rate: The rate at which the body burns calories.</p>
<p>Midclavicular line: An imaginary vertical line crossing through the right or left clavicle (collar bone) to the hip bone.</p>
<p>Mitral valve: A valve made up of a dual flap of skin situated between the left atrium and left ventricle in the heart. It allows blood to flow into the right ventricle when the right atrium contracts, but prevents the backflow of blood when the ventricle contracts.</p>
<p>Mucolytic: A medicine that makes sputum less thick and sticky, and easier to cough up.</p>
<p>Myocardial infarction: A heart attack resulting from an interruption in the blood supply to an area of heart muscle causing the heart cells to be damaged or die.</p>
<p>Necrotic: Death of cells or tissues through injury or disease where there is an inadequate blood supply. It is irreversible.</p>
<p>Opiate analgesic: A class of drugs derived from the opium poppy that are used to relieve moderate to severe pain.</p>
<p>Orthostatic: Related to or caused by standing up.</p>
<p>Palpable: Able to be touched or felt.</p>
<p>Palliative: An area of healthcare focused on relieving pain and suffering, to promote quality of life and manage end-of-life symptoms.</p>
<p>Pandemic: An epidemic of an infectious disease across a much larger region (e.g. across continents or even worldwide).</p>
<p>Pathogen: A microorganism capable of causing disease in its host.</p>
<p>Pericardial tamponade: A collection of fluid in the pericardial sac around the heart. It interferes with the performance of the heart and will cause death if left untreated.</p>
<p>Peripheral vascular disease: A condition where a build-up of fatty deposits in the arteries restricts the blood supply to the leg muscles.</p>
<p>Pneumothorax: A collection of air in the pleural space around the lungs, resulting in collapse of the lung on</p>
<p>the affected side.</p>
<p>Polycythaemia: A condition where there are too many red blood cells in the blood, resulting in increased thickness or stickiness of the blood, reducing blood flow to the organs of the body and sometimes resulting in clots.</p>
<p>Postural hypotension: A reduction of at least 20 mmHg systolic and at least 10 mmHg diastolic blood pressure within three minutes of standing upright. A common cause of falls in the elderly.</p>
<p>Pre-eclampsia: A medical condition characterised by high blood pressure and protein in the urine that occurs during pregnancy, with risk of serious complications to mother and baby.</p>
<p>Proteinuria: The abnormal presence of protein in the urine in detectable quantities, usually defined as an excess of 300 mg protein per day. There are many possible causes, but persistent proteinuria should be investigated.</p>
<p>Pulmonary embolism: A blockage in a blood vessel in the lungs that can cause collapse and death.</p>
<p>Pyelonephritis: A kidney infection.</p>
<p>Renal artery stenosis: Narrowing of the renal artery (the artery that supplies the kidney with blood), which may lead to impaired kidney function.</p>
<p>Reperfusion: Restoration of blood flow after having been blocked.</p>
<p>Sick sinus syndrome: A collection of conditions where there is malfunction of the sinus node, resulting in arrhythmia.</p>
<p>Slough: A layer of dead tissue separated from surrounding living tissue that can result in delayed healing. It is made up of dead cells that have accumulated in the exudate and is typically a white/yellow colour.</p>
<p>Statins: A class of drugs used to lower cholesterol.</p>
<p>Sternal border: The long edge of the breast bone.</p>
<p>Superior vena cava: The large vein that carries deoxygenated blood from the upper half of the body back to the right side of the heart.</p>
<p>Systemic: Affecting the entire body.</p>
<p>Trachea: The large airway that leads from the larynx (voice box) to the bronchi (large airways at the top of the lungs). Also known as the windpipe.</p>
<p>Tricuspid valve: A three-segmented valve that stops blood in the right ventricle from flowing back into the right atrium.</p>
<p>Turbid: Having sediment or particles stirred up or suspended in fluid, clouded, opaque.</p>
<p>Varicose eczema: A type of eczema caused by increased pressure in the veins that affects the legs. Pigment from the blood leaks into the skin, causing discoloration, inflammation and ulceration.</p>
<p>Venuole: A very small blood vessel that allows blood to return from the capillaries to the veins.</p>
